Potentially yes, but with careful guidance. Bipolar Exposure deliberately generates difficult sensations; in PTSD, this can re-traumatise if not carefully titrated. Work with a trauma-informed therapist (familiar with EMDR, CPT, or trauma-informed CBT) who can help you incorporate Bipolar Exposure safely and gradually. The principle — equanimity with difficult sensations — is sound. The application with complex trauma requires expertise. Do not attempt this alone. advanced bipolar exposure, PTSD, trauma-informed, professional, titrate Addiction and Recovery I'm in recovery and struggling with the "refraining from intoxicants" practice. You've already made the most significant commitment — sobriety. MiCBT supports this directly: use the MISS to notice the body sensations that precede cravings (restlessness, loneliness, shame, boredom). Practise equanimity with those sensations in formal meditation. Build community and meaning through the ten factors. Use mindfulness as your primary coping tool for urges. MiCBT addresses the underlying discomfort that fuels use — which is why it's particularly effective as a support alongside recovery programs. intermediate recovery, addiction, sobriety, craving, intoxicants Meditation makes me uncomfortable and triggers urges. What should I do? Start with grounding (feet, hands, breath awareness) rather than full body scanning. Shorter sessions initially (5–10 minutes). Partner meditation with your existing recovery support (12-step meetings, sponsor, group therapy). Urges are impermanent body sensations — gently meditating with them teaches your nervous system that they will pass without you acting on them. This is the core skill for sustained recovery: knowing from direct experience that the urge will pass. Start with the mildest situations and build gradually. intermediate urges, discomfort, grounding, recovery, cravings Depression and Low Mood I'm in a depressive episode and formal meditation feels impossible. What do I do? During acute depression, reduce rather than eliminate practice. Try: (1) Mindfulness of breath for 5–10 minutes only; (2) Gentle loving-kindness toward yourself; (3) Brief body awareness of obvious sensations (hands, feet). Don't force a full 30-minute session when severely depressed — it can backfire and create aversion to practice. Depression is also a signal to seek professional support (therapist, doctor). Medication and meditation serve different functions and complement each other well; medication can restore baseline function, meditation builds resilience. intermediate depression, low mood, reduced practice, professional help, gentleness Parents and Caregivers Can I practise with my kids around when they keep interrupting? Realistically yes, but with adjusted expectations. Shorter, consistent sessions (10–15 minutes) are better than fighting for uninterrupted time. Options: practise during their screen time or after bedtime; involve older children in a modified version (simple breathing together); accept fragmentation. The quality of your presence — your growing equanimity — benefits your children more than perfect meditation conditions. Your authentic practice is itself a lesson in self-regulation that children absorb through observation. beginner children, family, interruptions, parents, caregivers How can I share MiCBT values with my children without forcing them? Model it. Children absorb equanimity, kindness, and patience from your behaviour far more than from instructions. As they grow, introduce concepts age-appropriately: mindfulness of breath from around age 5, simple loving-kindness (for a pet or favourite person) from age 6, ethical discussions from age 8. The book's framework is universal — your authentic practice over years is the most powerful transmission possible. beginner children, values, teaching, modelling, development Couples and Relationships My partner and I both do MiCBT. Can we practise together? Yes. Meditating in the same space (even if practising independently) is grounding and builds a sense of shared commitment. Joint loving-kindness practice — directing kind intentions toward each other during Step 4.1 — can deepen intimacy meaningfully. Resist the urge to monitor each other's practice or offer critique. Post-practice, sharing insights (not evaluations) can strengthen connection. The combination of two people developing equanimity in the same relationship is particularly powerful for how conflicts are handled. beginner couples, together, partner, practice, intimacy The mindful communication step made things tense in my relationship. Will it resolve? Often yes, with time and mutual willingness. Your partner may feel threatened by changes in the dynamic — especially if they're used to the previous pattern. Reassure them: you're not attacking them; you're becoming clearer about your own experience. Inviting them to understand the framework (the book itself, or a joint session with a MiCBT-trained facilitator) helps. If tension persists, couples counselling can bridge the gap. Usually, once partners experience the actual outcomes — better communication, deeper respect — resistance softens considerably. intermediate relationship, tension, assertiveness, couples counselling, change
Using the MiCBT Guide App I lost my practice log in the app. How do I recover it? Practice data is logged locally when you tap the log practice button after each session. If you've reinstalled the app, some data may not be recoverable. Restart from where you remember being — your skills remain in your nervous system regardless of what the app shows. Going forward, log sessions immediately after completing them rather than after a delay. Contact support if you believe data should have been backed up. beginner app, data, log, progress, recover Can I skip ahead to a later step? The app structure allows this, but the program progression is intentional — each step builds the skills required for the next. Skipping is like trying to sprint before you can walk. If a step feels too easy or too familiar, extend the time you spend there rather than rushing ahead. If you're genuinely covering ground faster than the structure allows, consult a MiCBT facilitator before advancing — they can help you identify whether skills are truly consolidated or whether you're bypassing something. beginner skip, ahead, progression, pacing, structure Integration with Other Approaches I'm in therapy. Can I combine MiCBT with my therapist's approach? Absolutely. MiCBT and therapy are complementary: MiCBT develops the interoceptive and equanimity skills that work at the body level; talk therapy addresses narrative, relational patterns, and meaning-making. Many clinicians integrate MiCBT into their practice. Share the framework with your therapist. If they're trained in MiCBT, the integration will be even more direct. The two approaches rarely conflict — if anything, developing equanimity makes therapeutic work more effective by reducing avoidance of difficult material. beginner therapy, integration, complementary, therapist I use medication for anxiety or depression. Should I stop now that I'm meditating? No — never stop medication without medical guidance. Medication and meditation serve different functions. Medication can restore baseline neurochemical function; meditation builds resilience and long-term skills. Many practitioners find the combination optimal — medication reduces the severity of symptoms enough to make meditation possible, and meditation then builds the skills that may eventually reduce medication need. Always work with your prescribing doctor about any changes. intermediate medication, meditation, anxiety, depression, prescriber I practise other meditation traditions. Is MiCBT compatible? Broadly yes. All contemplative traditions share core principles (presence, equanimity, compassion). MiCBT is distinct in its body-centred, systematic approach and explicit integration with psychology. You can maintain respect for other traditions while practising MiCBT as your primary framework during this program. If you are in a tradition with specific daily practices required, check with your teacher about how to combine them. Many practitioners find that MiCBT deepens understanding of other traditions rather than conflicting with them. intermediate traditions, Vipassana, Zen, TM, compatibility, integration Life Circumstances I travel frequently for work. How do I maintain practice on the road? The portability of meditation is one of its great strengths. Pack headphones, use the app, practise in your hotel room or a quiet corner. Shorter sessions (20–30 minutes) are acceptable during travel rather than skipping entirely. Some practitioners find travel energising for practice because novel environments reduce habituation and bring fresh attention. The key is to maintain the twice-daily rhythm even if session duration is reduced — consistency of timing matters more than duration on travel days. beginner travel, work, hotel, consistency, portability I'm approaching retirement or a major life change. How can MiCBT help? MiCBT directly addresses impermanence and equanimity with change — which is precisely what major transitions require. Skills you'll use: (1) Equanimity with the uncertainty and discomfort of transition (Step 1.4); (2) Letting go of previous identity (renunciation factor); (3) Building new meaning and purpose (wisdom factor); (4) Strengthening relationships through better communication (Stage 3). Major life transitions are actually ideal occasions for deepening practice, because the usual structures that sustain routine are disrupted — making the internal anchor of practice more valuable than ever. intermediate retirement, transition, life change, impermanence, meaning
I intellectually understand everything but don't feel it. Why? Intellectual understanding is conceptual; what MiCBT builds is embodied understanding — felt in your nervous system through repetition. This takes weeks to months, not days. The three ways of learning make this clear: intellectual learning alone rarely produces lasting change. Continue practising even when nothing seems to be happening. One day you'll notice: "I used to react to that for an hour; it only lasted 10 minutes." That's the intellectual becoming experiential. It can't be forced — only allowed through consistent practice. intermediate intellectual, experiential, understanding, embodied, patience I'm afraid that deeper practice will change me in ways I don't want. Change is inevitable in life regardless of practice — the question is whether it's intentional. The fear you're feeling is understandable: identity shifts can feel destabilising. It's worth asking: what specific change frightens you? Is this fear protective (a genuine instinct) or reactive (old conditioning protecting itself)? Often, what feels like "losing myself" is actually becoming more authentically yourself — which others in your life typically welcome, even if it requires adjustment. You remain in control of this process; equanimity is its guide, not recklessness. intermediate change, fear, identity, authenticity, control I feel like a failure because I can't meditate "well." The book reframes "good meditation" as showing up consistently and maintaining equanimity with whatever arises — including distraction, frustration, and doubt. A session where you're distracted but keep returning to practice is good meditation. A session where you feel you're failing is actually excellent equanimity practice — you're observing a difficult mental state (self-judgment) without collapsing. There is no perfect meditation; there is only honest effort. Your self-judgment is data for the practice: notice it equanimously. beginner failure, judgment, effort, self-criticism, good meditation I'm worried I'll become too passive or detached from practising equanimity. This is a historically raised concern worth taking seriously. MiCBT addresses it directly through the ten factors and ethical guidelines, which are active and engaged — generosity, effort, determination, truthfulness are not passive qualities. Equanimity is not indifference; it is clarity that enables appropriate action. Many practitioners find they become more engaged with life after developing equanimity, not less — because they're no longer exhausted by reactivity. Genuine MiCBT practice tends to produce people who are both calm and meaningfully engaged. intermediate passivity, detachment, engagement, equanimity, action
Can I practice lying down? For PMR in Step 1.1, lying down is not recommended for two reasons: it increases drowsiness, which prevents the alert awareness the practice requires; and it trains your brain to associate lying down with practice, making it harder to fall asleep normally. During the full program, seated practice is standard. Once the program is complete and skills are established, lying down for maintenance sessions is fine. beginner lying down, posture, position, PMR 11.1 What does "Right Effort" mean in the context of MiCBT? Right Effort in MiCBT refers to the quality of attention brought to practice — not too tense, not too lax. Straining to feel sensations or forcing concentration creates tension that actually blocks the perception you're seeking. Equally, a vague, half-hearted scan produces nothing useful. The image often used is a guitar string: too tight and it snaps, too loose and it produces no sound. Right Effort is the tuned middle — alert, engaged, and at ease simultaneously. This quality is what allows equanimity to develop rather than frustration or drowsiness. intermediate right effort, quality, tension, relaxed, alert, balanced 11.2 I'm worried sweeping will make me less able to notice detailed body sensations. Will I lose precision? No. Sweeping and part-by-part scanning develop complementary skills — they don't replace each other. Sweeping develops breadth and flow; part-by-part develops precision and depth. Both continue to be used throughout the later steps. In fact, alternating between the two tends to produce more refined sensation awareness than either technique alone, because each challenges the attention in a different way. Your part-by-part precision won't erode — it will be enhanced by the contrast. intermediate sweeping, precision, detail, part by part, complementary 22.1 What's the difference between Bipolar Exposure and regular exposure therapy? Traditional exposure therapy works by having you face feared real-world situations repeatedly until the anxiety response habituates. Bipolar Exposure in MiCBT works at the level of body sensation: rather than engineering real situations, you deliberately generate the feared sensation during meditation (by imagining the scenario) and practise equanimity with it directly. This has several advantages — you can practise at any time, in any location, starting with mild scenarios and building gradually; you can rehearse rare or extreme situations that would be impossible to stage; and equanimity is trained rather than mere habituation, meaning the skill generalises more broadly. intermediate bipolar exposure, regular exposure, difference, sensation, habituation, equanimity 22.1 I have a phobia (flying, heights, spiders, etc.). Can Bipolar Exposure help? Yes. Bipolar Exposure is particularly well-suited to phobias because it works directly with the body sensations that constitute the fear response — the racing heart, the dread in the stomach, the urge to flee. By repeatedly generating and observing those sensations with equanimity in meditation, you change their meaning: from "danger signal I must escape" to "familiar sensation that passes." Combined with graded real-world exposure, this is highly effective. For severe phobias, working with a MiCBT-trained facilitator or exposure specialist is recommended. intermediate phobia, flying, heights, fear, bipolar exposure, graded 22.1 Can I skip directly to sweeping in depth, or do I need to build up through the earlier techniques? The sequence matters. Sweeping in depth requires refined interoceptive awareness and stable equanimity that are built through part-by-part scanning and standard sweeping first. Jumping to depth sweeping without those foundations typically results in "blank" experiences — not because depth isn't there, but because the attentional skill to perceive it hasn't developed yet. Follow the progression: establish part-by-part, then sweeping en masse, then depth. Each stage creates the conditions for the next. intermediate sweeping in depth, skip, progression, foundations, interoception 22.1 How do I build genuine confidence after the exposure work? Confidence in MiCBT is built through accumulated evidence: each time you face a feared situation and remain equanimous, your nervous system updates its assessment of your capacity. Unlike confidence built on positive self-talk or past success, this kind is grounded in direct experience — you have genuinely done hard things and remained intact. The key is to let that evidence accumulate consciously: after each successful exposure, take a moment to register what happened ("I faced that and was okay"). Over time this updates the self-concept at a level that abstract affirmations cannot reach. intermediate confidence, exposure, evidence, self-concept, equanimity 22.2 How does loving-kindness practice help with forgiveness? Loving-kindness doesn't require you to condone harm or pretend it didn't happen. It works through a different mechanism: by softening the body sensations associated with resentment (tension, heat, constriction), it gradually reduces the suffering you carry from the grievance — not for the other person's benefit, but for yours. The book distinguishes forgiveness from excusing: you can understand that the other person acted from their own conditioning and suffering (the universal human condition) without approving of what they did. This perspective makes forgiveness possible as a personal act of release, rather than a moral judgment about the other person. advanced forgiveness, loving-kindness, resentment, release, condone 44.1 Rebuilding self-worth after prolonged criticism or abuse — where do I start? Start with self-compassion, not self-improvement. The instinct after chronic criticism is often to fix yourself — to become "good enough" to deserve kindness. MiCBT reverses this: you begin by extending kindness to yourself exactly as you are, with your current wounds and limitations. Practically: start with the first part of loving-kindness (self-compassion), focus on finding even a single sensation of warmth or ease in your body, and pair it with a kind intention. Don't try to feel a lot at once. Over time, consistent small acts of self-kindness rewire the deeply held belief that you are unworthy of care — not through argument, but through felt experience. advanced self-worth, abuse, criticism, rebuilding, self-compassion, worthiness 44.1 Helpful Speech: How do I communicate truthfully without causing harm? The ethical guideline around helpful speech asks you to consider: Is it true? Is it kind? Is it timely? All three matter. Truth without kindness is often just cruelty with a justification. Kindness without truth is often avoidance that ultimately harms more. Timeliness matters too — a true and kind observation delivered at the wrong moment can still cause unnecessary pain. Before speaking, check your body: are you speaking from clarity and genuine care, or from a need to win, punish, or relieve your own discomfort? The sensation tells you before the words do. intermediate helpful speech, truth, kindness, communication, ethical 44.1 What does "refraining from taking lives" mean practically in modern life? At its simplest, it means not causing unnecessary harm to sentient beings. In practice, this is interpreted on a spectrum: most practitioners focus on avoiding deliberate harm to humans and animals. For some, it expands to diet and consumer choices. For others, it includes reducing harm through their professional and social choices. MiCBT doesn't prescribe a specific level — the key word is "unnecessary." Use mindfulness to investigate your own motivations: when you could reduce harm without significant cost, are you doing so? That inquiry, held with equanimity rather than guilt, is the practice. intermediate refraining, taking lives, harm, ethical, animals, practical 44.1 What about "taking what is freely given"? What does that include? This ethical guideline covers more than physical theft. "Taking what is not freely given" includes: taking credit for others' work, using someone's time or energy beyond what they've genuinely offered, taking emotional resources from people who are already depleted, or benefiting from situations where you know consent is compromised. "Freely given" means genuinely, willingly, without coercion or manipulation. Check your intention: are you receiving something that is genuinely offered, or are you engineering a situation in which the other person feels they can't say no? intermediate taking, freely given, ethical, theft, credit, consent 44.1 I'm struggling with the "appropriate sexual action" ethical challenge. What qualifies? The principle is: sexual behaviour that reduces suffering and respects the boundaries of all involved. Ask yourself: Am I acting from genuine connection and desire, or from craving, avoidance, or loneliness? Is this consensual for all parties — not just technically, but genuinely? Will this action build trust and care, or undermine it? MiCBT doesn't offer a prescriptive rule for every situation — it invites you to feel the answer through your body's honest response. If the action leaves you with peace and integrity, it is likely appropriate. If it leaves constriction, inauthenticity, or a need to hide, reconsider. advanced appropriate sexual action, boundaries, consent, ethical, integrity 44.1 Seasonal Affective Disorder (SAD) affects my motivation in the darker months. How do I stay consistent? SAD is real and has a genuine neurobiological basis — reduced light exposure affects serotonin and dopamine. During low seasons: (1) Maintain even brief practice (10–15 minutes) rather than skipping entirely — routine stabilises mood; (2) Practise at a consistent time each day, ideally morning; (3) Pair meditation with light therapy if you use it; (4) Apply kindness and gentleness (two of the ten factors) toward yourself when energy is low — self-criticism for struggling makes everything worse; (5) Consult your doctor if symptoms are severe. Equanimity with the seasonal rhythm — "this is impermanent, spring returns" — is itself the practice. intermediate SAD, seasonal affective disorder, winter, motivation, mood, light My aging parent is struggling with health decline. How do I support them using what I've learned? Your greatest gift to an aging parent is your equanimity in their presence — not fixing or cheerleading, but being genuinely present with their experience without panic or resistance. Practically: model acceptance of what cannot be changed; offer loving-kindness both in practice (silently directing compassion to them) and in action (small, consistent acts of care); use the communication skills from Stage 3 to have honest conversations about what they need and what you can offer. If they are open, gentle introduction of mindfulness of breath as a comfort practice can help. Your own practice is the support — you cannot give from an empty well. intermediate aging parent, elderly, health decline, caregiver, support, compassion I'm elderly and approaching the end of life. How can MiCBT help? MiCBT's later stages — particularly Stage 4 — are directly relevant to life's final chapter. Equanimity with impermanence is the central theme of the program, and death is the ultimate impermanence. Practically: loving-kindness helps release longstanding resentments and open the heart; the ten factors (especially wisdom and renunciation) support graceful letting go; body scanning can ease the suffering of physical symptoms by reducing the reactive layer on top of inevitable sensation. Many elders find that consistent practice deepens whatever spiritual or philosophical framework they hold, regardless of tradition. The end of life is a profound opportunity for deepened practice, not a context in which it no longer applies. intermediate elderly, end of life, aging, impermanence, death, spiritual
What is the MIET and how do I do it? The MIET (Mindfulness-based Interoceptive Exposure Task) is a brief, structured technique for applying equanimity to difficult moments in daily life. It takes about 30 seconds. The six steps are: (1) Identify the area of strongest sensation in your body during the distressing moment. (2) Rate how intense it feels on a scale from least to most. (3) Observe the four characteristics of that sensation — how heavy or light (mass), how warm or cool (temperature), how still or moving (motion), how dense or fluid (density). (4) Apply equanimity — observe those characteristics with unconditional acceptance for about 30 seconds, roughly seven to nine breaths, without judgment or trying to change anything. (5) Observe the four characteristics again — notice what, if anything, has shifted. (6) Re-rate the intensity. That is it. The MIET is designed to be used in real situations, not as a formal sit-down practice. beginner MIET, what is, how to, steps, technique, interoceptive exposure, daily life, stage 1.4, step 1.4, homework 11.4 What are the four characteristics I observe during the MIET? The four characteristics are: (1) Mass — how dense or loose the sensation feels, from loosest to densest. (2) Temperature — how warm or cool it feels, from coldest to hottest. (3) Motion — how still or active it feels, from completely still to most movement. (4) Density/Fluidity — how light or heavy it feels, from lightest to heaviest. These are the same four characteristics you rate on the MISS recording form before and after applying equanimity. With practice, noticing these qualities becomes quick and instinctive — they give you something concrete to observe rather than getting caught up in the story or emotion surrounding the sensation. beginner MIET, four characteristics, mass, temperature, motion, density, fluidity, MISS, observe 11.4 Does the MIET suppress emotions? I am worried it will make me disconnected from my feelings. No — the MIET does the opposite of suppression. Because emotions are composed of body sensations, and the MIET guides you to pay full, unconditional attention to those sensations, you actually learn to feel and accept emotions more fully. Suppression involves pushing feelings away. The MIET asks you to turn toward the physical experience of the emotion and stay with it equanimously. The result is that emotions are processed and released more completely, not avoided. The technique also does not prevent action — by reducing reactive impulses, it allows you to choose your response more deliberately rather than reacting automatically. intermediate MIET, suppress, emotions, disconnected, feelings, suppression, opposite, accept 11.4 How long does a MIET practice take? About 30 seconds — roughly seven to nine breaths. This is deliberate. The MIET is designed to be usable in the middle of real life: during a difficult conversation, in a stressful meeting, waiting in a queue, after receiving bad news. You do not need to sit down or close your eyes. The brevity is the point — it makes equanimity accessible in the moments when you need it most, not just during formal meditation sessions. beginner MIET, how long, 30 seconds, duration, time, brief, daily life 11.4 Where do I find the MIET in the app? How do I access it? The MIET quick-capture button appears in the app from Step 1.4 onwards and remains accessible throughout the rest of the program. To get started: listen to the Introduction to the MIET audio (Track 11 in the app, or in Meditations under Stage 1.4) — this is about 3 minutes and gives essential background. Then use the guided MIET practice audio (Track 12, approximately 6 minutes) the first several times to walk you through each step. Once you are confident with the technique, you use it independently in daily life and record each practice using the MISS in Assessments. beginner MIET, app, where, find, access, button, track, audio, quick capture 11.4 I tried the MIET and the sensation felt the same before and after. Is it working? This is very common in the early sessions, and it does not mean the technique has failed. Equanimity is a skill that builds with repetition — the nervous system needs multiple exposures before it begins to release habitual reactivity. Even if the intensity rating does not drop in a single session, the act of turning toward the sensation and observing it without resistance is the practice. Progress typically shows across sessions over days and weeks, not necessarily within one instance. Look at your MISS recordings over time — the pattern of before and after lines shifting closer together is the meaningful signal, not any single session. intermediate MIET, not working, same, intensity, no change, equanimity, progress, patience 11.4 Should I only use the MIET for strong emotions, or for mild ones too? Use it for both, and start with milder situations when you are learning. Mild distress is easier to work with — the sensations are less overwhelming, so you can observe the four characteristics more clearly and apply equanimity with greater precision. This builds the skill for the moments when intensity is high. Over time, the goal is to use the MIET habitually whenever a stressful moment arises, regardless of intensity level. The more you practise with smaller situations, the more available the skill is when you genuinely need it. beginner MIET, mild, strong, emotions, when to use, any, all situations, practice 11.4 What is the difference between the MIET and the MISS? The MIET is the technique — what you do. The MISS (Mindfulness-based Interoceptive Signature Scale) is the recording form — what you fill in to track what happened. They go together: you do the MIET, then record it on the MISS. The MISS captures your ratings of the four characteristics and the intensity before and after each MIET practice, building a visual record of how your body's stress responses shift over time. Think of the MIET as the practice and the MISS as the logbook. beginner MIET, MISS, difference, recording, technique, form, logbook, track 11.4 I find it hard to identify any body sensation during a stressful moment — my mind just goes blank or races. What do I do? This is one of the most common early challenges. A few things help: first, practise scanning for sensations during calm moments so the skill is available when you need it. Second, start with obvious physical cues — tightness in the chest or throat, heat in the face, heaviness in the stomach — these are the most common locations for distress-related sensations. Third, if your mind is racing, try anchoring in one obvious physical sensation first (even just the pressure of your feet on the floor) before moving to the MIET. The capacity to locate sensations under stress develops with practice — it is not expected to be immediate. intermediate MIET, can't feel, blank, racing mind, locate sensation, difficult, stress, challenging 11.4 Can I use the MIET for physical pain, not just emotional distress? Yes. The MIET was designed to work with any body sensation, including physical pain. The process is the same: locate the area of strongest sensation, observe the four characteristics, apply equanimity for 30 seconds, re-observe. Physical pain often has a significant reactivity component — the fear, resistance, and tension surrounding the sensation can amplify it considerably. Applying equanimity to that reactivity can reduce the intensity of experienced suffering even when the underlying physical cause remains. However, for pain from injury or medical conditions, the MIET complements (rather than replaces) appropriate medical care. intermediate MIET, physical pain, pain, body, sensation, not just emotional, medical 11.4
I m not able to relax despite really trying to. I can only get about 20% of my body to relax. It s frustrating, and I m starting to dislike this exercise. Am I doing it wrong? It might seem counterintuitive, but the purpose of PMR isn t to achieve 100% relaxation. The real goal is to carefully notice the contrast between tension (when you tighten the muscles) and relaxation (when you release that tension). This week, you re training your mind to recognize these differences, which will help you notice and release tension more effectively in daily life. So, when you notice that it s hard to relax muscles after tight ening them, approach this as a learning experience! Being without aversion toward tension and without craving for relaxation will help you be more accepting of the unavoidable tensions that arise in daily life, even those that you cannot readily release. intermediate i m not able to relax despite really trying to. i can only get about 20% of my body to relax. it s f 1 1.1 book I have bulging discs in my back, and I feel scared of tensing my back in case I injure it further. However, the practice of PMR does not create anxiety in and of itself. This anxiety can stem from worries about not performing the exercise correctly, from taking time away from other activities or responsibilities, or from bringing physical sensations of anxiety that were already there into greater awareness. As PMR itself does not train skills to address anxious thoughts, relaxing during practice can allow existing concerns and anxieties to become more prominent. Then refocus attention on the physical sensations of tensing and relaxing each muscle group. If anxiety persists, try practicing with your eyes open for the first few sessions. In your previous email, you mentioned past trauma. Relaxation can sometimes bring painful memories into awareness, which may explain why individuals with Post Traumatic Stress Disorder often avoid relaxation by engaging in distracting activities like watching TV late into the night, using substances, or overworking. 42 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE If you experience flashbacks or an initial increase in anxiety during PMR, there are a few ways to approach the practice. You can keep your eyes half open for the first few sessions to help reduce feelings of vulnerability and provide a sense of control. If closing your eyes constantly trig gers flashbacks, it s perfectly fine to keep them open and proceed with PMR at your own pace. This ground ing practice can help anchor you in the present moment and reduce the intensity of flashbacks. Can I just practice once instead of twice daily? While practicing once daily is better than not practicing at all, research shows that more frequent practice leads to better results [16]. You ll likely experience greater benefits if you commit to practicing twice daily. If finding time feels challenging, consider reor ganizing your activities so your self-care doesn t come last again. We often prioritize others needs out of duty or compassion, but neglect our own needs. This program is an opportunity to improve self-care, which will also support the development of self-compassion in Stage 4 of the program. Our brains have evolved to prioritize tasks based on what we expect the rewards to be. If you currently find it more rewarding to focus on others needs than your own, it s natural to prioritize their care over your own. Behavioral psychology explains this as a conditioned response, where per ceived gains or losses shape our actions. These conditioned tendencies can make it hard to prioritize self-care, including practicing twice daily. Changing this pattern involves recognizing that investing in your well-being benefits not only you but also those around you. WEEK 1: ESTABLISHING SELF-CARE AND COMMITTING TO CHANGE 43 Instead of overthinking, try to approach twice-daily practice as a nonnegotia ble part of your day. Developing the habit of consistent self-care is one of most valuable outcomes arguably even more important than learning to relax! intermediate i m struggling to make time for practice. can i just practice once instead of twice daily? 1 1.1 book I ve tried PMR a couple of times, but I don t like it because I dislike the feeling of tightening my muscles. This happens occasionally. In MiCBT, the PMR technique we use emphasizes the contrast between tension and relaxation. Highlighting this difference makes it easier to detect tension and release it more effectively during daily life. If this contrast is not sufficiently clear during practice, s benefits may not carry over as strongly into the rest of your day. Another reason tensing is helpful is that during periods of anxiety or high stress, we can feel a little disconnected from the body. At these times, tensing can help us feel grounded in the body. That said, if deliberately tensing muscles feels uncomfortable or isn t pos sible, there s an alternative method of practicing PMR in MiCBT that is also very helpful. Instead of a strong contraction, bring your attention to each muscle group following the same sequence and gently contract the muscles the contraction might be just at the threshold of awareness and then allow relaxation, to whatever degree is possible. This approach can be very benefi cial for managing subtle tension and stress throughout the day.
