Tseng Tzu Practice Background and Rationale Building on the foundation established in Week 1, this chapter explores the nature and purpose of mindfulness training. You ll learn how mindfulness of breath can help you gain a deeper understanding of your mental states and how these affect your emotions and reactions. This practice will teach you to notice and let go of uninvited and unhelpful thoughts using a tech nique you can apply beyond your meditation sessions. We ll also examine five common mental obstacles, including aversion, restlessness, and doubt, that can impede your progress. By becoming more able to recognize and work with these hindrances during meditation, you ll develop the skills to handle them more effectively when they arise in daily life. Definition and Purpose of Mindfulness What Is Mindfulness? The term mindfulness is one translation of the Pali term sati. Sati can also be understood as objective attentiveness, memory, or keeping in mind [1, 2]. Western definitions overlap, with some being more specific Week 2: Regulating Attention and Gaining More Control of Your Mind 46 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE than others. At its core, mindfulness is an alert, receptive, unbiased, and equanimous observation of present moment experience. It is a mental skill set or tool of investigation that can be developed to cultivate insight into the impermanence of all phenomena, including the sense of self, thereby decreasing suffering [3]. To be optimally effective, mindfulness practice must include a specific skill called equanimity. Equanimity, which will be discussed further in later chapters, fosters a balanced and nonreactive attitude. This attitude serves as the foundation for developing mindfulness skills, enabling you to investigate your experiences with safety, objectivity, and healthy curiosity. Together, mindfulness and equanimity allow us to cultivate a profound discernment of what is being examined. They help you become aware of the intentions behind your thoughts, body sensations, and behaviors, and dis cern those that are wholesome relieving suffering from those that are unwholesome promoting suffering. The clearer this distinction becomes, the more your decisions and actions contribute to your own well-being and that of others. It can be helpful to remember three important aspects of training mindful ness skills: 1 Only observe! Mindfulness is essentially a tool of investigation; it is a means of observing natural phenomena, especially our inner experi ences, objectively, so they can be comprehended accurately, just the way they are. Accordingly, developing this ability requires you to observe your internal experience without altering it in any way. Don t add to or subtract from what you notice. Instead, aim for absolute objectivity. This means without changing anything about it t control your breath or interpret your experience through your own beliefs, culture, or values. For instance, if you feel a sensation of warmth in your chest while meditating, avoid labeling it as good (if you associate warmth with love) or (if you associate it with anger). Simply notice the warmth as it is, without attaching any particular meaning to it. 2 Just accept! Mindfulness also involves the unconditional acceptance of the internal phenomena you are observing. This means being free from craving and aversion so that your objectivity is preserved. For example, if a thought arises such as ll never overcome this addiction, don t get caught up in its content or feel defeated. Instead, acknowledge that a thought has arisen. You re not accepting the idea that you ll never suc ceed; rather, you re accepting that this thought popped up momentarily! This is what we mean by accept. Recognize it as a passing mental event, not as an absolute truth, and let it go without further engagement. 3 Feel safe! Mindfulness is not harmful. It doesn t create the phenomena you observe; it simply helps you become aware of what is already pre sent. For example, noticing an itch on your nose doesn t create the WEEK 2: REGULATING ATTENTION 47 itch it was already there. Mindfulness simply allows you to become conscious of it, offering an opportunity to witness it without reacting it. This nonreactive awareness can help you approach your internal experience with a sense of safety and curiosity, even when it involves discomfort. Why Train Mindfulness Skills? Our mental and emotional difficulties often originate from our own minds. Mindfulness offers a powerful way to address these challenges by helping us understand and regulate the processes that shape our thoughts, emotions, and behaviors. One reason to practice mindfulness is to develop greater clarity about how personal importance assigned to thoughts whether con sciously or subconsciously produces body sensations that can escalate into emotions 6]. For example, worrying about an upcoming confrontation might lead to tightness in the chest, which then intensifies into anxiety. By learning to observe and understand these processes, we can gain greater agency over them. Another key reason to practice mindfulness is to learn to live more fully in the present. When we dwell on past issues or become preoccupied with future concerns without realizing it, we unintentionally disconnect from what s happening now. Without mindfulness, it s easy to get lost in self focused thoughts. Our brains are wired to prioritize any thought associated with and As a result, neural connections in the brain s frontal areas are constantly on standby, ready to be activated and relate whatever we perceive to ourselves[7], often comparing others experiences, views, and attitudes with our own. Additionally, thinking about ourselves, even nega tively, can feel mentally compelling often drawing our attention without us realizing it [8]. Mindfulness helps us recognize whether our mental state is helpful or being influenced by unhelpful thoughts. The stronger our mindfulness skills, the more aware we become of unwholesome mental states, allowing us to make deliberate choices rather than falling into the usual traps that promote suffering. Ultimately, the true purpose of mindfulness is to develop wisdom and well-being [9]. Through mindfulness training, we learn to see things more objectively, gaining insight into the causes of our reactivity or absentmindedness. As the relationship between mind and body becomes clearer, we are able to more fully experience the present moment with less reactivity and less identifica tion with our internal experience [10]. This creates opportunities to express our underlying values, fostering a sense of authenticity and enhancing our sense of connectedness with the world. Mindfulness also improves our ability to regulate emotions, reducing the likelihood of developing or relapsing into anxiety, depression, or other mental health conditions benefit supported by over 40years of mindfulness research [11]. 48 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE This week, you ll start developing mindfulness skills by focusing on your breath. Mindfulness of breath teaches us to remain focused in the present moment, concentrating on the breath while letting go of distractions. It also an essential foundation for next week s practice, where we ll begin mindfulness of body sensations through body-scanning practices. While starting something new can be challenging, a strong commitment to daily practice will help you navigate these initial hurdles (see section below). That s why dedicating time and effort now is essential to laying the ground work for the next step. Neurologically Wired to Think Your practice in mindfulness of breath provides a rich source of experiential learning. Let s now discuss why and how thoughts can arise unintentionally in consciousness. Understanding this process will help you put your medita tion skills to immediate use in daily life, preventing unwholesome mental states while fostering beneficial ones. What Are Thoughts? We can look at the arising and passing of thoughts as a natural occurrence, part of the normal workings of the brain and mind. After all, we think more than we do almost anything else in fact, we have up to 60,000 thoughts a day! Even while we re asleep, our minds remain active, generating thoughts in the form of dreams. A thought can simply be understood as a mental event. More precisely, a thought is the result of a cognitive process during which the mind generates, organizes, and manipulates information. Notably, thoughts can occur either intentionally or spontaneously, as the brain integrates and responds to vari ous stimuli [12]. As we ll explore in detail in Week 3, thoughts are often intertwined with sensory perceptions and emotions. Advancements in brain imaging technology have made it increasingly possible to observe and measure the effects of thoughts on the brain. Using artificial intelligence based technologies, researchers can now even reconstruct complex thoughts and intentions from brain activity patterns [13]. We known for some time that specific thoughts activate particular brain pathways. For example, angry or fearful thoughts typically activate areas of the right prefrontal cortex and regions of the limbic system, such as the amygdala [14]. This intimate interaction between the mind and brain highlights their inter dependence in shaping all our experiences. The remarkable discovery that our thoughts can physically alter the brain [15] has revolutionized our under standing of both the mind and brain. However, the exact process of how we make intentional decisions remains an open question in Western science. In Western psychology, the awareness of one s mental states is called metacognitive awareness [16], while the mindful recognition that thoughts WEEK 2: REGULATING ATTENTION 49 are just thoughts, and not inherently part of you, is referred to as metacog nitive insight [17]. As your mindfulness practice deepens and you begin to observe thought processes more easily, you ll notice that some thoughts feel fleeting and seem to exist just in the mind, while others are more embodied, producing clear sensations in the body. Attachment to Thoughts Thoughts can be surprisingly addictive! Without realizing it, we often become deeply attached to them, clinging to thoughts just as we do to other sensory experiences like tastes, smells, or sounds. Thoughts act as magnets for our attention, making them challenging to release. Thankfully, this attachment is a learned habit, and like any habit, it can be unlearned. As Albert Einstein pointed out, we can t solve a problem with the means that created it. If you try to improve your emotional well-being while reacting with craving to pleasant thoughts or aversion to unpleasant ones, you re essentially trying to address reactivity with more reactivity. It s like trying to extinguish a fire by adding more wood to it. How can that possibly work? The key lies in changing the way you relate to your thoughts, not the thoughts themselves. By practicing mindfulness of breath, you re training your mind to step out of this habitual cycle of entanglement with thoughts. However, it s not easy to just observe the arising and passing of thoughts, despite the simplicity of the practice. The mind can be overly busy, wild, restless, and prone to fixat ing on self-critical or other negative patterns. Thoughts can be challenging in part because we subconsciously identify with them, which can lead to catastrophizing or woefulizing unpleasant situations. As you learned last week, these reactions stem from our natural aversion to not getting what we want or being faced with what we don t want. Getting caught up in thoughts and reacting to them can happen in your meditation practice this week, too! Given how wired our brains are to think, why should we expect to be free from thoughts during meditation? As you begin to practice mindfulness of breath this week, it s natural to wonder why thoughts that you didn t intentionally choose seem to arise in your mind. To illustrate this phenomenon of thought intrusions, let s explore a helpful metaphor. Fasting Metaphor To explain the arising of intrusive thoughts into conscious awareness during meditation, let s use the fasting metaphor, which was frequently shared by N. Goenka, one of our meditation teachers. If you want to lose weight, part of the process involves either reducing your food intake or increasing your physical activity while maintaining your current diet. When your body needs more energy than it receives from food, it taps into its reserves, stored as body fat. The mind works in a similar way. As with the body, the mind needs input in order to function. Mental inputs take the form of thoughts and images. Much as the body changes food into fat and stores it for future use, the mind 50 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE transforms perception of events into thoughts and stores them for future use. The storage for the mind is our memory bank. When you focus your attention on the entrance of the nostrils, monitoring the breath, you are limiting mental input to just the physical sensations in that small area of the body. This means you are not engaging with typical sources of new thoughts, such as reading, talking, scrolling through your phone, or listening to music. In this sense, the mind is fasting. Yet, even when you stop adding new mental input, you will notice that thoughts continue to arise, sometimes relentlessly, every few seconds. This happens even if you actively try not to think. Why is this? The mind must be finding them somewhere! It is the scarcity of mental input during mindful ness meditation that allows thoughts stored in the memory bank to emerge. If you don t get involved with the memories and instead just observe them, just witnessing them as they emerge, the importance and related emotional tone you have given them in the past will weaken. Over time, these memories become what psychologists call desensitized, and, as you will progressively discover, over time, will cease to have the emotional intensity they once had. Therefore, when you experience, for example, a thought or image related to a past trauma, the related unpleasant emotion that you previously would have experienced will not be as intense. The more thoughts, images, and memories that are reprocessed in this way, the freer you will be from your past. The Mind s Predictive Nature ve discussed memories, but what about the spontaneous thoughts about the future that can arise during practice? Although these thoughts appear forward-looking, they are deeply influenced by past experiences [18]. Our minds use memories as building blocks, creatively combining them in new ways to imagine future scenarios [19]. For instance, if you were worrying yesterday about an upcoming work deadline, it s likely that during this even s meditation, your mind will revisit those concerns. s important to understand that these future-oriented thoughts aren t just simply passive replaying of memories. Instead, they represent your brain way of predicting and preparing for potential future events. These thoughts draw on past experiences to imagine what might happen [20]. This means that during meditation, uninvited spontaneous thoughts about tomorrow will arise from how your mind processes yesterday s experiences. While the brain mechanisms underlying this predictive tendency are beyond the scope of this book, the next section will explain how these mental and emotional experiences can be observed objectively during your meditation practice. Three Internal Causes of Intrusive Thoughts In the early weeks of meditation practice, you may find a large variety of external stimuli drawing your attention away. These distractions might include sounds outside your room, such as dogs barking or cars passing, or WEEK 2: REGULATING ATTENTION 51 household noises like a phone ringing or a pet scratching at the door. As a beginner, it s common to notice your attention repeatedly being pulled toward these external distractions. To manage this, it s helpful to adopt a patient and accepting attitude toward these interruptions, whether they are sounds, smells, or tastes. Do your best to keep your attention on the breath, and as soon as you notice your attention has been pulled away, return your attention to the breath without frustration or intolerance. Patience will help you sustain effort and develop the necessary skills over time. While external distractions can be challenging, they generally become easier to manage as you continue practicing and reduce your reactivity to external stimuli. Even thoughts triggered by external cues, such as a thought about eating when you smell food cooking, can be managed relatively easily, much of the time. However, internally generated thoughts and sensations those that arise spontaneously within us are often more challenging when starting mindfulness practice. However, by understanding the nature of these internally generated thoughts and the factors that cause them to arise, we can begin to observe them with greater objectivity. This shift in perspective creates a sense of distance, making thoughts feel less compelling and easier to manage. Three key factors contribute to the spontaneous emergence of thoughts in our mind: the recency, frequency, and depth of their activation in the brain, as detailed below. Thoughts tied to recent experiences, those frequently repeated, or those charged with strong emotions tend to occupy our mental space. Recency Effect When we start our practice, thoughts that we have had most recently tend to emerge first. For example, during your evening practice tonight, you might have thoughts about a work meeting from this morning or a phone call from the afternoon. You might even notice a spontaneous thought about this section of the book! This phenomenon easier recall of recent events linked to specific areas of the brain that retain some level of stimulation fol lowing these experiences [21]. These neural pathways remain slightly acti vated, making it easier to retrieve recent memories. This aligns with decades of research on memory decay, which shows that as time passes, ordinary memories fade, making them harder to recall [22, 23]. Frequency Effect Another reason specific thoughts arise spontaneously during practice is the frequency with which we think them. The more often you think about some thing, the more likely it is to pop up uninvited. Teachers have long recog nized the power of repetition in learning! For example, if you frequently sing or whistle your favorite song on your way to work, then it s likely that this song will pop into your mind during meditation. This is exactly what 52 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE one of our clients told us this morning. She joined a choir a few weeks ago and keeps hearing the songs they perform during her meditation practice. At the level of the brain, this occurs because frequent activation of the neural pathways associated with a particular memory strengthens the syn aptic connections between neurons, making it more likely that those par ticular cells will be activated again [24]. Because of this, topics we think about more often tend to arise uninvited in our mind during mindfulness meditation; while this process also occurs in daily life, especially when our mind is not engaged in a task, we become more aware of it during medita tion. This, of course, also includes worries and other mental habits! Co-emergence Effect The third internal factor contributing to spontaneous thoughts is the co emergence of thoughts and body sensations, a phenomenon referred to as the embodiment of the mind [25, 26]. Just a note on the term emergence, as you will be seeing it used frequently from here on. Two things co-emerge when they emerge at the same time, where one of them causes the other to arise. One may seem more apparent or feel as though it occurred first, but they are intrinsically linked. The principle of co-emergence between mind and body, and how it contributes to emotional reactivity, will be explored in detail in the next chapter. When we evaluate a thought or situation as personally significant, this eval uation co-emerges with sensations in the body. The more emotionally charged the thought, the more strongly it activates brain pathways connected to emotions, resulting in simultaneous bodily sensations. This interconnected ness explains why a thought can carry an emotional tone and intensity is tied to physical sensations. Simply put: as we think, so we feel. Over time, our brains become wired in this way. Each of us has stored millions of memories linked to specific body sensations. These connections can explain why certain smells, sounds, or even subtle body sensations can act as cues for memories of past events. For example, the smell of your neighbor s dinner might bring back memories of a childhood meal, or a song on the radio might remind you of the first time you fell in love. Similarly, a sensation in the body can act as a cue for memories. This mechanism has profound implications. It suggests that much of our daily experience may be subconsciously influenced, or even determined, by past experiences. A recent example from a client, Andrew, illustrates this clearly: Andrew had experienced a number of significant changes in his life in the last few months, including moving from another country for work, and separating from his partner. During a meditation practice, while sitting on his deck in the sun, he experienced spontaneous thoughts and images about his previous part ner and their life in a warmer climate together. Andrew clearly recognised that these memories had been triggered by the warmth of the sun while meditating. He was amazed by this new insight into how body sensations in the present WEEK 2: REGULATING ATTENTION 53 moment could trigger romantic memories of his partner from the past. He also realized how, without this insight, he would have misinterpreted these memo ries as missing his partner, rather than resulting from an association between current body sensations and past experiences. It is important to note that the memories tied to these sensations often remain subconscious. Here is a nice case illustration: A few years ago, Bob, a middle-aged man with neurodivergence and a history of trauma, sought therapy to reduce his heightened sensitivity to sensory stim uli, which regularly led to intense anger. He was very confused about certain aspects of his reactions. For instance, he couldn t understand why he was fre quently angry when driving with his partner. As therapy progressed, Bob confusion about his anger began to clear. During his mindfulness meditation, while feeling a certain area of the body, he suddenly had a flashback of his mother wearing a particular perfume that he had found intensely uncomfort able as a child. His mother had been cruel to him as well. Coincidentally, his wife s perfume was the same scent his mother had often worn! Given that the smell was more obvious within the enclosed space of the car, it was a regular trigger for unpleasant body sensations that had been stored in memory during childhood, along with memories of the smell of his mother s perfume. Feeling the same unpleasant sensations in the present led to the same anger reactions he had had in the past as a child. The memories of his mother were initially subconscious, until he started feeling the body sensations consciously during his meditation practice. Whether we are consciously aware of it or not, if a current body sensation is sufficiently similar to one felt years ago, then the current sensation can trigger related memories. In this way, the body acts as a memory bank for emotional experiences, ranging from mild to intense. This has important implications for your understanding of spontaneous thoughts, both within and outside your meditation practice. It can explain many of the thoughts that seem to appear randomly during practice or when daydreaming. Another effect of co-emergence can be observed when two separate events with a similar emotion are stored in memory. When the events are different but the sensations in the body are sufficiently similar, they can act as memory cues: thinking of one event can bring up the other event in consciousness. Maureen s experience with co-emergence provides an example of this. After losing her daughter to suicide a few months prior, she sought therapy to help her let go of ruminative thoughts. However, she was initially reluctant to practice because she felt as though it would stop her from thinking about her daughter, and that this would mean that she was abandoning her. Over time, however, she became able to grieve without guilt. She was progressively able to let go of guilt-ridden thoughts and fear of falling apart, while preserving loving memories of her daughter. 54 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE Then, unexpectedly, she started reliving the grief she had experienced about 30years earlier, when her mother died. She was very surprised that the mem ory of her previous grief experience was still present so vividly after 30years. Once Maureen was able to grieve her daughter without additional feelings of guilt, the sensations of grief mirrored the feelings she had after her mother death grief without guilt. This similarity in body sensations triggered the memories of the time after her mother s passing. Essentially, the unimpeded grief she felt for her daughter brought up memories of her earlier experience with her mother, as both involved grief unburdened by additional emotional layers. Maureen s experience illustrates how deeply interconnected our body sen sations and memories can be. This shows us that grief and emotions, even from decades ago, can resurface when evoked by similar body sensations. Summary s summarize all this rather complex information into several simple points: 1 Based on current understanding, the kinds of thoughts you experience are influenced by the activation of specific brain cells. 2 There are three main internal reasons thoughts arise during meditation: a Recency: Thoughts that have occurred recently or are closely related to recent experiences tend to resurface. b Frequency: The more often a thought or a similar one has been repeated, the more likely it is to emerge. c Co-emergence: Current body sensations can trigger memories asso ciated with similar sensations felt in the past. Co-emergence in Dreams It is highly likely that the same principles apply to dreams, especially since one of the roles of dreaming is to consolidate memories [27]. For example, if you feel cold while sleeping, body sensations of coldness may affect your dream content. These sensations might trigger dreams of actual times when you felt cold, or they might be processed in the dream into an unrealistic, imaginative scenario, such as wandering through a magnificent ice castle made of spar kling frost, while avoiding snow monsters intent on stealing your warmth. Bruno shares two funny stories that vividly illustrate this phenomenon well perhaps not so funny at the time: The first occurred during my childhood. I dreamt I was one of the three little pigs (yes, the Walt Disney ones). I was walking really fast up a hill on a cartoon-like walking track, trying to arrive at the top before the wolf, who WEEK 2: REGULATING ATTENTION 55 appeared from a nearby clearing. I finally reached the top of the hill and found a toilet, in which I immediately relieved myself. The wolf didn t catch me but, as you can already guess, I wet my bed! Now, we could go on speculating all sorts of scenarios but the simplest and most obvious explanation for this dream is that the thoughts and related emo tions in the dream were triggered by sensations in the body. The unpleasant body sensations caused by the pressure on my bladder created a context of subconscious anxiety about wetting myself, perhaps symbolized by the scary wolf. Ironically, it was this subconscious attempt to not wet myself that led me to do so in real life. The other story occurred during my travels in the Middle East over 42years ago. I was staying in a small Bedouin fishing village in Egypt called Dahab, located on the coast of the Sinai Peninsula. Back then, the very small village was not at all equipped for tourism, and I was sleeping on a straw mat in a tiny hut a couple of square meters in area made of woven palm tree leaves. To decorate the inside of the hut, the owner had hung the skull of a goat on one of the walls. During a windy night, I woke up suddenly from a dream that someone had punched my face really hard. In the dream, I saw a fist approach my face very quickly and hit me. I immediately woke up and felt pain on my forehead. The wind had shaken the walls of the hut so much that the goat skull had fallen on my forehead. As in my childhood dream, the intense sensation in the body dictated the thought and triggered the topic of the dream. What was remarkable in this dream was the speed with which the co-emergence of pain and dream content took place. The dream that someone s fist was punching my head appeared almost at the same time that the sensation occurred. These personal examples underscore how co-emergence can deeply influ ence our dreams, often in very immediate ways! Training Mindfulness Skills How did your practice go this week? Were you able to commit to practic ing Progressive Muscle Relaxation (PMR) twice daily? If so, did you notice any benefits? Many people find that PMR promotes relaxation, but at times, the mind can still be distracted or even restless. This is particularly so when our mind is accustomed to high levels of stimulation from daily concerns like work or studying. You may have also realized just how strong the attachment to thinking can be. It might feel like you simply t stop thinking. While physical relaxation is undoubtedly beneficial, it doesn t inherently equip us with the tools to address persistent thoughts. Letting go of thoughts can be much more challenging than letting go of physical tension and in fact, it can be harder to release thoughts than to part with our belongings. Even if we let go of our most treasured possessions, our thoughts about 56 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE them would still persist. But if we could release our attachment to thoughts, the possessions themselves would hold much less power over us. This high lights the core purpose of mindfulness training: cultivating mental skills that enable us to approach thinking with greater ease and discernment. Practice Set-Up Just as we choose appropriate clothing for work or holidays to suit the occasion, creating a favorable context for mindfulness practice is equally important. When you re just starting out, minimizing sensory stimulation is key. This helps you direct attention inward. This means minimizing see ing, hearing, smelling, tasting, touching, moving the body, and voluntary thinking. By decreasing the input of information to be processed by the mind, you make it much easier to notice the valuable output that occurs during practice, such as spontaneous thoughts, memories, and body sensations. If the environment is overly stimulating, your attention is likely to be drawn to external stimuli, such as the sound of waves, birdsong, the touch of the wind, or the warmth of sunshine on your skin. These distractions can make it more difficult to focus on your breath and internal experiences. For this reason, the best context for mindfulness meditation is a quiet indoor space with minimal external distractions. As you refine your skills, you ll be able to practice anywhere. The environment in which you practice, along with your attitude and physical state, plays an important role in supporting you to develop your skills. Let s start with the essentials: choosing the right location and setting up your practice space. Creating an Optimal Practice Environment Choose a quiet space where you won t be disturbed for 30minutes each morning and afternoon or evening. You may want to wear loose clothing and untie or take off your shoes to feel more comfortable. s best to keep the light at a moderate level. Too bright a room can be distracting and make it harder to concentrate, while too little light can contribute to drowsiness. A dim lamp works well for most people. Similarly, keeping the room at a comfortable temperature is also important too hot, and you may feel sleepy; too cold, and you may become restless. When it comes to sounds, a quieter space can be most helpful, as ambient sounds can be distracting at the start. However, this isn t always possible, and in these cases, allow the sounds of the barking dog, leaf blower, chil dren, or traffic to become opportunities to develop patience and tolerance when attention is drawn to one of these sounds, acknowledge this with an accepting attitude, without resentment, and then move attention back to the object of meditation, which this week is the breath. As you progress in your WEEK 2: REGULATING ATTENTION 57 practice and develop a better ability to focus, you ll become less affected by your environment, where temperature and sounds will matter less, but for now, small adjustments like the above can make a big difference. Approaching Practice with a Clear Mind It is essential to approach your practice with a clear mind. Substances like alcohol, marijuana, or other drugs interfere with the concentration and equanimity needed for mindfulness. These substances impair the brain s pre frontal regions, which are crucial for regulating thoughts and reactions. Without this regulation, we are more likely to react impulsively to thoughts and sensations, which runs counter to the purpose of mindfulness practice. If you choose to use intoxicants, it s best to do so after practice rather than before, and in moderation, to minimize any residual effects on future practice. You might also find that after 30minutes of practicing mindfulness meditation, your cravings for substances tend to be reduced. Similarly, practicing mindfulness on a full stomach is not ideal, as diges tion takes up energy and often leads to drowsiness. Aim to practice either before meals or at least a couple of hours after eating. If you do eat before practicing, try to keep your stomach only three-quarters full the lighter your meal, the better your focus is likely to be. Posture We practice with eyes closed to minimize distractions. However, if closing your eyes feels uncomfortable or triggers anxiety perhaps due to past trauma or a fear of losing control there s an alternative. You can close your eyes halfway and direct your gaze down to the floor, about 2 or 3m ahead. As you begin to feel safer and comfortable that way, you can gradually close your eyes further with each practice session. Unlike the very relaxed sitting posture used during PMR, mindfulness meditation requires a posture that keeps your neck and back comfortably straight. You can choose to sit on a chair or on a very thick cushion on the floor, as shown in Figures 2.1 and 2.2. If using a chair, avoid leaning back against the support unless a medical condition makes this necessary. Whichever option you choose, ensure that your knees are positioned lower than your hips, which allows for a natural curve in your spine without put ting strain on your lower back. This will also keep you more alert and atten tive despite the lack of external stimulation. If you will be sitting on the floor, Figure 2.2 provides an example of one way of sitting comfortably. Place a blanket or mat on the floor to create a soft surface for your feet and knees, then add one or two thick cushions for support. There s no need to adopt a complicated or uncomfortable posture. In fact, creating pain intentionally produces sensory stimulation that can become a distraction from the actual task. If you can, have your knees touching the floor. This will prevent slouching and will keep your back in an upright position with ease, without tiring you. However, if you find this 58 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE difficult at first, that s perfectly fine. You can place folded towels under your knees or use a higher cushion to provide additional support. If you find yourself feeling cold while sitting still for an extended period, consider using a blanket or shawl for added comfort. Mindfulness of Breath The Practice With this meditation technique, you will cultivate mindfulness of mental phe nomena, such as thoughts (dhammanupassana in Pali). This is the second of four mindfulness training modalities, following last week s applied practice focusing on mindfulness of the body, and is taught here as it has been taught for the past 2,500years. With your eyes closed, seated in a comfortable upright position, you ll begin by taking half a minute to relax by doing a quick scan through the body and releasing any tension you may feel. Notice that this is a Figure 2.1 Posture on a chair. WEEK 2: REGULATING ATTENTION 59 different technique from last week, when you were tensing and relaxing vari ous muscles. And take a moment to remind yourself why you re practicing perhaps to cultivate being in the reality of the present moment or to let go of any mental or emotional states that no longer serve you. While starting with a calm mind is not always possible, do your best to bring your attention to the present moment, making a concerted effort to set aside everything other than focusing your attention on the entrance of the nostrils. Bring your attention to the small triangular area around the nostrils and the area above the upper lip, as shown in Figure 2.3. Focus primarily on the sensations created by the breath entering and exiting the nostrils, such as sensations of temperature and friction on the inner walls of the nostrils and just above the upper lip. In this practice, you will gradually learn to attend more and more exclu sively to your breath, without trying to change it in any way. Let go of all your concerns, all your planning; just attend to the sensations of the breath. If the breath is agitated, then witness and accept the agitation of the breath, while doing your best to remain mentally calm. If you find yourself trying to change the breath in order to calm it, this reactivity to your experience of breathing will, in turn, further agitate you. On the other hand, if you accept that the breath feels agitated, then the mind begins to relax as a consequence of your acceptance, which in turn will settle the breath. Figure 2.2 Posture on a cushion. 60 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE At times, you might find yourself so caught up in thoughts that main taining focus on the breath for even 5 10seconds feels difficult. If this happens now or in the future during challenging periods you can use the three-second rule. This involves making a firm commitment to return your attention to your breath within three seconds of noticing that attention has moved to a thought. In those first few seconds, the contrast between feeling the breath on the skin at the entrance of the nostrils and the thought is distinct enough to make bringing attention back easier. However, if you linger on the thought for longer than about 3seconds, you risk becoming so engrossed that it s harder to recognize it as just a passing mental event. Cultivating the ability to sustain attention, inhibit habitual responses to thoughts, and redirect focus will form a strong foundation for the advanced techniques you ll encounter next. Ensure that you don t visualize anything or use verbalization techniques, such as counting breaths or using a mantra. Mindfulness is the practice of observing your experience of reality exactly as it unfolds, moment to moment. When you use visualization techniques or mantras, you re direct ing your focus toward imagined images or words. This shifts your attention away from the actual sensations and spontaneously arising thoughts occur ring in the present moment. s important to use the downloadable audio instructions provided with this book or the Mindfulness-integrated Cognitive Behavior Therapy (MiCBT) phone application, the MiCBT Guide app, even if you ve followed other guided practices in the past. While many meditation instructions share similarities, practice methods can vary widely. Each approach has its own pros and cons, and to avoid confusion, it s best not to mix techniques during Figure 2.3 Area of focus for mindfulness of breath. WEEK 2: REGULATING ATTENTION 61 this program. For optimal results, we encourage you to set aside other methods while completing this program. Practicing with precision is key, as accurate practice leads to faster and more targeted changes in the brain regions responsible for the expected benefits. Skills You Will Learn The skills you ll be learning might seem unfamiliar at first, but they are not mystical they are well-recognized by Western psychology as executive functions. These mental processes enable problem-solving, planning, decision-making, and self-regulation, which allow us to manage and organ ize our thoughts, emotions, and actions effectively. Mindfulness of breath trains three key executive functions: 1 Sustaining attention on the breath while holding in mind the intention to notice spontaneous thoughts objectively (working memory). 2 Inhibiting the urge to engage with those thoughts as they arise (response inhibition). 3 Switching attention back to the task at hand (attention shifting). Research has shown that mindfulness meditation fosters neuroplasticity in the prefrontal areas of the brain, where executive functions tend to oper 30]. Beyond improving focus and emotional regulation in daily life, these skills lay a strong foundation for the mindfulness techniques you ll be developing throughout this program. The first skill you ll build is the ability to sustain attention for increasingly longer periods while noticing spontaneous mental events as they arise. This alone is invaluable in every aspect of life, as our ability to focus directly affects how effectively we perform tasks. At first, especially if you re new to the practice, thoughts that you didn t actively choose to think may surface after just a few seconds. Sustaining attention on the breath serves as a prac tical anchor, allowing you to observe thoughts as they emerge without iden tifying with them or considering them part of the Although we are practicing mindfulness of breath, a large part of our practice is developing mindfulness of thoughts! The second skill involves learning not to engage with thoughts. Without mindfulness training, when a thought enters our mind, we go for a ride, without questioning whether or not this is what we want to do, and whether it could be useful or harmful. Instead of automatically following where each thought leads, you ll learn to inhibit this usual response. This is called cogni tive inhibition, which means you are choosing not to act on or think through a thought. Keep in mind, you re not suppressing the thought; re just refraining from engaging with it and further elaborating on it. The third skill is the ability to switch focus back to your intended object of concentration, in this case, the breath. As soon as you recognize that a thought has arisen in the mind and you are not getting caught up in it, 62 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE immediately and patiently, return attention to your breath at the entrance of the nostrils. On occasions, we can get completely carried away by a thought. When this occurs, as soon as you realize this has happened, simply switch focus back to the breath without delay and without losing your confidence. By repeatedly making this effort, with acceptance of your experience and without frustration, you cultivate greater mental flexibility and capacity to tolerate unwanted thoughts that extend to daily life. Because doing a prac tice like this is likely new, you may initially find it somewhat challenging. That s normal! Taken together, these three skills are powerful! When an unhelpful thought arises during the day, you ll start to notice it more readily and make a con scious decision whether to engage with it or let it pass. This process becomes easier because your twice-daily practice serves as a kind of workout for your brain. Just as physical exercise strengthens muscles, your practice creates and strengthens the neural pathways necessary for regulating thoughts and emotions. These three skills help reinforce your mental resilience, enabling you to break free from automatic reactions that might have driven your responses in the past. As your skills improve, you ll become more able to discern whether thoughts are helpful or unhelpful. In the context of MiCBT, unhelpful thoughts are those that produce co-emerging body sensations that lead to craving or aversion, which in turn become a source of suffering. Helpful thoughts may also lead to co-emerging sensations in the body, but we don feel the automatic pull toward reactivity; the sensations, if any, are more neutral, and our response is benign or beneficial. Practicing with this kind of discernment is what the Buddha called Right Mindfulness [31] (samma sati in Pali), which has also been translated as complete mindfulness. This discernment leads you away from suffering, toward personal growth and well-being. We will be exploring how to practice this in more detail in the Applied Practice section below. The Five Hindrances As you practice mindfulness of breath this week, you are likely to encounter specific challenges in your practice. Far from being problems, these five common patterns craving, aversion, lethargy, restlessness, and doubt are invaluable opportunities to deepen your practice by recognizing, under standing, and ultimately transforming them [5]. These five challenges, termed hindrances in the traditional teaching of mindfulness, don t just occur during meditation they re present through out daily life too. After all, the brain s habitual patterns don t switch off when we stop meditating; instead, they continue to shape how we perceive stimuli and respond to them throughout the day! By learning to observe and work skillfully with these five common patterns during your meditation practice, you ll also be building the skills to work with them effectively as they arise in everyday situations. WEEK 2: REGULATING ATTENTION 63 Craving: The Pull of Desire Craving is one of the most common hindrances, both in meditation and daily life. As we discussed earlier, craving can be wholesome or unwhole some. Here, we are focusing on unwholesome craving: the desire for pleas urable experiences or the longing for things to be different from what they are (aversion). In meditation, this can take many forms, including reliving pleasant memories, fantasizing about future events, or wanting more of a specific sensation in the body. One of the causes of difficulty in resisting unwholesome craving is that it is portrayed as having desirable consequences in our lives. We assign a spe cial value to what we crave; we feel nourished by it. Consequently, we attach ourselves to what we crave in a way that gives us the impression that it is part of who we are, that we absolutely should have it, and that it should never leave. We want the object of craving to be permanent. Therein lies a great source of suffering because everything changes, even the things we desire most, such as pleasure and love. There is nothing new here. A multi tude of myths and legends from different cultures have been written based on this very simple theme. More specifically, craving is a learned response based on past attachment to pleasant body sensations. When a pleasant sensation is associated with a thought or an external object, thinking the thought or imagining the object recreates the same sensation. This leads us to crave more of the thought or the object because it feels good to do so. To address craving, it s essential to recognize that whether in daily life or during meditation, our attachment isn t to the external object, thought, or a particular wish for our future, but rather to the pleasant bodily sensations that accompany it. For example, some meditators initially describe feeling attached to the voice on the guided audio instructions! Some find they don want to stop using the recording next week when it s time to do so. If this resonates with you, pay close attention to the experience in your body. You may find that it s actually the pleasant sensations co-emerging with hearing the voice that you re clinging to. Conversely, you might notice unpleasant body sensations, which we ll explore in the section on aversion. In either case, it s the co-emerging sensations you re attached to or trying to avoid not the voice itself. A practical first step to working with craving during meditation is to notice it is present! Ask yourself: What is it about this internal state that allows me to recognize it as craving? Recognizing craving is invaluable, especially in daily life, as it allows us to step out of its spell. Perhaps it is taking the form of wishing for the experience to last longer or wanting it to feel better than it is, or longing for something else entirely. When craving arises whether triggered by a memory, a fantasy, or a pleasant sensation begin by acknowledging its presence with curiosity, without judgment. Next, become curious about how craving is manifesting in the body. For example, body sensations like a warm tingling in the abdomen or face often 64 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE act as triggers for these desires. By observing these sensations with curiosity and equanimity, without giving in to the accompanying attachment, you can begin to weaken the hold of craving. Then, just like with other sensations, calmly and patiently bring your attention back to the breath without clinging to the desire for more pleas antness. Gently renouncing craving in this way recognizing it, observing it, and then abandoning it by redirecting your attention can help you reduce its grip over time. Aversion: The Push of Discomfort Aversion, like craving, is one of the most pervasive hindrances in meditation as well as in daily life. Aversion, which we can also think of as craving to avoid, is the mind s attempt to push away unpleasantness, whether it arises in the form of unpleasant sensations, unpleasant thoughts, or unpleasant emotional states. One of the first steps is recognizing aversion when it arises, without judg ment. Ask yourself: What is it about this internal state that allows me to recognize it as aversion? When we experience aversion toward physical sen sations, like an itch or an area of discomfort, it can manifest as a powerful desire to stop practicing or to react impulsively by fidgeting, shifting pos ture, or allowing the mind to wander. For example, when unpleasant sensa tions arise in meditation, the mind might begin a series of thoughts like, This is unbearable, I can t do this. During meditation, we can also experience aversion to unwanted thoughts, such as worries or painful memories, and to mental states, such as anger, sadness, or fear. Aversion to these can manifest as resentment or frustration, because of our unmet expectation that the mind should quiet down but doesn t. Aversion is natural, but it s important to recognize that aversive reactions only reinforce aversion over time. To start working with aversion, observe the body sensations that accom pany it with patience and tolerance, keeping in mind that they are imperma nent. Tolerance allows us to patiently endure unpleasantness without reactivity or needing to change it or escape it. Tolerance involves a degree of restraint, as it s primarily about not acting on the discomfort. For example, when toler ating an itch, you might think, This itch is here, and I don t need to react because it will pass away, and allow the itch to be there. Tolerance can be seen as the foundational step, providing stability in the face of discomfort. Experiential acceptance builds on tolerance of your experience by moving beyond enduring unpleasant sensations to actively acknowledging and mak ing space for them, allowing them to be as they are without trying to change or suppress them. While tolerance involves restraint, experiential acceptance involves openness and a willingness to engage with your experience fully. It is the inner recognition that, This is part of my current experience. It is not a way of identifying with the thought or sensation, but rather is a way of acknowledging its presence without resistance. For example, if sadness arises WEEK 2: REGULATING ATTENTION 65 during meditation, experiential acceptance might look like noticing the heav iness in your chest or the constriction in your throat, and noting, Sadness is here right now and it s ok. This practice doesn t mean approving of the sad ness or resigning yourself to it; instead, it reflects an attitude of allowing creating space for the experience to unfold naturally without judgment. Equanimity introduces yet another dimension to working with unpleas ant sensations or thoughts. While tolerance helps us endure, and acceptance allows us to acknowledge, equanimity capitalizes on tolerance and accept ance and invites a genuine willingness to experience sensations as they are, while not identifying with them or reacting to them. Next week, we explore equanimity in greater depth. For now, remember that unpleasant sensations are not or yours. They are temporary experiences that arise and pass continually. This week, the practice of returning your attention to the breath, while remaining tolerant, accepting, or equanimous with unpleasant sensations, builds resilience both in meditation and in daily life. Over time, you develop the ability to approach discomfort with greater ease and less resist ance, allowing you to remain balanced and calm even when challenges arise. Sloth and Drowsiness: The Fog in the Mind Lack of motivation and drowsiness colorfully described as sloth and torpor are common challenges during meditation! They can make it dif ficult to stay focused, causing the mind to feel dull or sluggish. In medita tion, a lack of motivation for the necessary mental effort can show up as difficulty focusing, while drowsiness may tempt you to fall asleep. These states can arise even after a good night s sleep or when you re physically rested. This is because the mind, especially when not used to moments of calm and stillness, may interpret quiet or relaxation as an invitation to dis engage or sleep. This hindrance can also be subtle, manifesting as a lack of clarity in practice or as a general disinterest in meditation. Working with lethargy and drowsiness requires a combination of practi cal adjustments and mental effort, as these states can become an obstacle to mindfulness if left unchecked. Instead of becoming frustrated with yourself, acknowledge that this mental state has arisen. The moment you notice it, ve already begun to break its grip. It is crucial not to identify with it or feel discouraged. As with craving or aversion, the sensations of lethargy or drowsiness are temporary and will pass with skillful practice of mindful ness. Here are practical strategies you can apply when lethargy or drowsi ness arise during meditation: 1 Adjust Your Environment External factors can often exacerbate lethargy and drowsiness. If you are sitting in a room that is too warm, too dark, or in a posture that is overly comfortable, the mind may associate these conditions with sleep or rest, leading to drowsiness. Try practicing in a slightly cooler room 66 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE with lighting that is neither too bright nor too dark. An upright posture, with your back straight and unsupported, will also help to keep your mind alert. Even small amounts of alcohol before meditation can hinder your practice, making it much less effective and unproductive. Similarly, avoid meditating right after a heavy meal, as digestion can drain energy and lead to sleepiness. 2 Alter Your Breathing When you notice that dullness or lethargy is settling in, try breathing slightly more deeply and quickly for a minute or two. This can help sharpen your focus and make sensations at the nostrils more noticeable. However, be cautious not to hyperventilate just a gentle increase in the pace and depth of your breath is usually enough. Once you feel more awake, return to your regular, natural breathing pattern without trying to control it. 3 Open Your Eyes If drowsiness persists, try opening your eyes slightly. Allowing a little light in can signal your brain to stay awake and alert. Focusing your gaze gently on the floor a few feet in front of you, without allowing yourself to become distracted by looking at what s around you, can stimulate wakefulness while maintaining a sense of calm. Once you feel a little more present and awake, resume your practice with your eyes closed. The Buddha encouraged meditators to imagine a bright light for a short while to help them stay awake. 4 Stand or Walk If the mind remains dull or drowsy, try practicing while standing for a few minutes. If drowsiness persists, take a brief break to go for a short walk. Physical movement helps to re-energize the body and mind. Once you feel more alert, return to your sitting practice with renewed focus. Drowsiness and lethargy are natural challenges that arise for almost eve ryone during meditation. Instead of viewing these states as obstacles, try to see them as opportunities to strengthen your ability to make an effort when things feel difficult, and deepen your equanimity to whatever is arising in your internal experience in the present moment! Agitation and Restlessness: The Fidgety Mind When it comes to restlessness and agitation, this hindrance tends to manifest as a mind that struggles to stay calm and still. It can leap from one thought to another, anxiously replaying scenarios or obsessing over future possibilities, or become consumed by feelings of guilt, regret, or remorse. Restlessness can also show up as physical discomfort or agitation, making it challenging to focus. This hindrance can be particularly tricky because it often masquerades as legitimate concerns or problem-solving, convincing us these thoughts are worth engaging with. For example, if you ve had an argument earlier in the day, restlessness might compel you to dwell on what was said, replaying the WEEK 2: REGULATING ATTENTION 67 event in your mind and fueling anxiety. While this mental rehearsal may feel important, it actually intensifies the agitation. To address restlessness skillfully during meditation, the first step is to recognize that it is present. Restlessness is often accompanied by physical sensations, such as jitteriness or tension in the body. At this stage of your training, simply refocus your attention gently and firmly on the breath, doing your best to maintain a calm mind. Notice how focusing on the natu ral rhythm of the breath can help settle both the body and mind. Next week, we will learn a specific technique for addressing these sensations directly. The key here is patience restlessness may continue to pull your attention away, but each time it does, don t take it personally. Calmly and persistently return to the breath. This repeated redirection of attention is an essential part of the practice. And intrusive thoughts are also part of the practice, as you wouldn t be able to develop your ability to control attention in this way without having thoughts to work with! Restlessness, as a mental state, doesn t need to be eradicated but rather understood and transformed. Learning to work with restlessness in meditation gives you the tools to bring calm and focus to everyday situations where the mind has a tendency to become agitated. Doubt and Confusion: The Voice of Uncertainty The hindrance of doubt is particularly subtle, often manifesting as skepti cism about whether meditation actually works, uncertainty about your abil ity to meditate effectively, or questioning whether you re doing the practice correctly. Like all hindrances, doubt is simply another mental process more inherently than agitation or drowsiness. Doubt typically manifests in three forms: doubt about the technique, doubt about the teacher, or doubt about yourself. Self-doubt is often the most persistent, creating a sense of hesitation or paralysis, and poses the greatest risk of stopping your practice. You might think, m not cut out for this, or I don t have time for this. This voice of self-doubt is an internal critic that saps your motivation and makes you question your ability to fol low through, whether with mindfulness or other pursuits. Like all hindrances, doubt emerges both in meditation and in daily life. While sitting, it might take the form of wondering, Is this even working? Am I wasting my time? In daily life, it might sound like, ll never be able to handle this, or m not good enough. These patterns of self criticism and uncertainty are not unique to meditation but reflect deeper habits of the mind. As with the other hindrances, recognizing when doubt is present is the first step. Shift your attention from the content of the doubt to its nature as just another mental state, arising alongside co-emerging sensations. Observe it with curiosity and avoid judgment. Then, gently refocus your attention on the breath, trusting in your ability to learn and grow through the practice. 68 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE The five hindrances and useful ways of addressing them are summarized in Table 2.1. Remembering them will be invaluable during your practice, as well as in daily life. While beneficial on its own, mindfulness of breath is also a foundational practice that prepares you for the next method. As will be discussed next Table 2.1 The five hindrances and ways of addressing them constructively. Hindrance Skillful approaches Craving Examine the impermanence of body sensations When you notice a craving arising in the mind, such as a desire to engage further with a memory or expectation, observe the sensations in your body that accompany it. Notice their impermanence: how they arise, remain for a while, and then pass away. By doing this, you can loosen the grip of craving and respond more skillfully. Aversion Respond with kindness and compassion When an unpleasant memory, expectation, emotion, or sensation arises, meet it with equanimity and kindness. Recognize it as a temporary experience rather than judging it. Allow it to pass naturally, and gently return your attention to the breath. Lethargy and drowsiness Renew effort and remember your goals Acknowledge the mental fog and make gentle adjustments, such as sitting upright, taking deeper breaths, or opening your eyes slightly to regain alertness. Reflect on your intention for practice and re-engage with practice with renewed effort. Agitation and restlessness Calm the mind on the breath When restlessness arises, recognize it for what it is without becoming entangled in the agitation. Shift your focus calmly back to the breath. Use the experience to strengthen both your concentration and patience. If the restlessness feels overwhelming, pause to slow and relax your breath for a minute or two before returning to observing the natural rhythm of your breathing. Doubt Verify the evidence and trust your ability to learn When doubt creeps in whether about the practice, the teacher, or yourself notice it as just another impermanent mental state, and not a definitive truth about your capability. Recall moments of growth to reinforce your confidence. Commit to testing the technique for a week, allowing your direct experience to provide evidence for its value. WEEK 2: REGULATING ATTENTION 69 week, you ll learn to remain attentive to different areas of the body, even when there is minimal sensory stimulation (such as an itch, pain, or move ment) in those areas. It is your practice of mindfulness of breath that will enable you to focus on these areas and cultivate the next skillset. Integrating Skills in Daily Life Right Effort Applying your meditative skills in daily life is essential. In Buddhist psychology, integrating skills in this way is called Right Effort, which is also understood as the most useful or helpful effort. Right Effort involves directing effort toward helpful, wholesome outcomes that reduce suffering and promote well-being, while avoiding unproductive exertion or reliance on sheer willpower. This practice, known as mindfulness of mental states (cittanupassana in Pali), is the third mindfulness training modality and an important step in establishing mindfulness in daily life. By cultivating beneficial mental states and preventing unwholesome ones throughout the day, Right Effort also supports mindfulness and concentration, making it easier for the mind to settle during meditation. Right Effort has four key aspects: 1 Preventing unhelpful mental and emotional states from arising 2 Abandoning unhelpful states that have already arisen 3 Initiating helpful states that have not yet arisen 4 Maintaining and enhancing helpful states that have already arisen Right Effort can be applied to many aspects of our lives, including to the development of wisdom, ethics, selflessness, and compassion. And all of these rely on a fundamental skill: thinking in a helpful way. Applying Right Effort to your thoughts and mental states throughout the day this week will require being mindful of your mental and emotional states, such as feeling worried, depressed, angered, saddened, joyful, etc., and their accompanying thoughts. s important to note that we approach thoughts differently depending on whether they arise during meditation or during the rest of the day. During meditation, the focus is on observing whatever thoughts spontaneously appear helpful, unhelpful, or neutral without reacting to them. The goal is to use these thoughts as tools to strengthen the three executive functions. If you go to the gym and lift weights, it doesn t matter whether the weights are red, yellow, or blue. All that matters is how heavy they are, as this deter mines the amount of effort you need to make and, in turn, the strength you will develop. In the same way that we don t pay attention to the color of the weights in the gym, we don t pay attention to what kind of thought helpful, unhelpful, or neutral has arisen in the mind while we are meditating. 70 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE In contrast, during the rest of the day, thinking is a necessity, and we can just deal with problematic thoughts by refocusing on the breath. However, we can choose our thoughts and how we engage with them, recognizing their significant impact on our well-being! Thoughts that involve self-judgment, comparisons, or relating external events to our identity (e.g., My son failing at school means I m a bad mother ) reinforce an illusory sense of self and promote craving and aversive reactions, thereby increasing suffering. This approach relies on the three executive functions you are developing through mindfulness of breath, as discussed earlier (1) noticing spontane ous thoughts while paying attention to the breath, (2) preventing your habit of identifying with thoughts and engaging with them, and (3) shifting your attention back to the task at hand, i.e., monitoring the breath. Recognizing when your attention has become caught up in unhelpful thoughts during meditation and then disengaging from them will be especially helpful in daily life! This ability to choose which thoughts and mental states to cultivate and which to abandon marks the beginning of true freedom of will. Effort to Protect the Mind from Unhelpful Thoughts The effort to protect the mind involves preventing unhelpful mental states from arising, especially those driven by craving, aversion, and unawareness of impermanence. Through daily mindfulness practice, you become more skilled at recognizing the early signs of a harmful thought and preventing it from taking root. This week, as you practice mindfulness of breath, you are strengthening the skill of noticing thoughts as they arise and gently shifting attention back to the breath. This ability to quickly notice and disengage from unhelpful thoughts is invaluable in daily life. Keep unhelpful thoughts at arm s length! For example, imagine you are enjoying a peaceful moment when an old habit of worrying surfaces perhaps the thought, What if I ve forgotten something important? In this moment, the Effort to Protect comes into play. You recognize that this is the early stage of an unhelpful series of thoughts arising from a mental state of worry and consciously decide not to entertain them. Instead, you gently remind yourself of the present moment peacefulness and refocus on your current activity. Or, perhaps you re scrolling through social media and initially feel con tent. Then you notice a subtle shift in your mental state and a thought starts to creep in: Why am I not as successful as them? In this moment, Right Effort means recognizing the comparison as an unhelpful thought and choosing not to engage with it, understanding that such comparisons are unproductive and likely to create suffering. Instead of allowing the compari son to spiral, you might shift your perspective by reflecting on gratitude for your own accomplishments or simply return your attention to the present moment without judgment. As your mindfulness training continues, you ll become more adept at discerning helpful from unhelpful thoughts. Unhelpful thoughts and mental WEEK 2: REGULATING ATTENTION 71 states tend to co-emerge with stronger body sensations than others, making them easier to identify. This week, focus on preventing thoughts tied to craving such as desires for things you cannot have or wishing to be superior to others and those promoting aversion, like resentment toward yourself or others. By learning to notice and act on these early signs, you can stop harmful mental states from escalating, keeping your mind clear and grounded in the present. Effort to Abandon Unhelpful Thoughts When unhelpful thoughts or emotions have already arisen, the Effort to Abandon is the practice of consciously letting them go. It requires renounc ing attachment to these thoughts, no matter how compelling they may feel in the moment. This type of effort is essential for building mental resilience and breaking free from reactive patterns. The three executive functions will enable you to carefully choose what and how to think, rather than falling prey to passing thoughts. For instance, imagine you receive critical feedback from a coworker and later in the day find yourself thinking, m a failure; I ll never succeed. With Effort to Abandon, you first notice the thought objectively and recognize it as just a mental event, and its content is not an objective fact. Next, you inhibit the urge to engage with it, thereby gently stopping yourself from spiraling into further negative thinking. Finally, you redirect your attention, perhaps back to the task at hand, breaking the cycle of self-criticism. Abandoning unhelpful thoughts takes practice, but the more you apply this skill, the easier it becomes to relinquish thoughts that don t serve your or others well-being. And there are many opportunities to practice whether during mindfulness of breath, daily activities, or even in bed before falling asleep! Think of your mind as the garden of your future, where the fertile soil will let anything grow even seeds you didn t intentionally plant. Birds may drop seeds from afar, or seeds that were long buried may find the right con ditions to sprout. As a mindful gardener, you accept this natural process and make the effort to pull out the weeds regularly! With repeated effort and patience, you ll find that unhelpful thoughts become less intrusive, and your ability to release them will grow ever more skillful and easier. Effort to Cultivate Wholesome States of Mind The effort to develop helpful mental states is about actively cultivating men tal qualities that foster well-being. The reason is simple: a peaceful and rewarding life starts with a peaceful and rewarding mind. Qualities such as generosity, kindness, compassion, and gratitude are some of the most skillful and helpful mental states to cultivate. These qualities are diametrically opposed to greed, vengefulness, indifference, disrespect, and ignorance. By consistently making the effort to cultivate wholesome mental states, you not only grow more accepting of yourself and others but also strengthen your sense of self-worth and connection with others. 72 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE Cultivating a wholesome state of mind can be surprisingly straightfor ward when we re motivated. Even the simple act of feeling content in the present moment is an expression of a wholesome mental state. Thinking positively about embarking on this journey of mindfulness and personal growth is also wholesome. However, there will be times when creating wholesome mental states requires more deliberate effort. When life feels routine or uninspiring, and nothing seems particularly exciting or reward ing, it s important to consciously find reasons to think in ways that are uplifting and fulfilling. Take, for example, a rainy day. If you typically prefer sunshine, with the Effort to Cultivate gratitude, you could think of all the good things that the rain brings to the land and how the trees and plants will benefit, and feel grateful for the rain. Similarly, if you find that generosity is a mental quality you relate to well, perhaps you could think of being generous to someone, an animal, or yourself. If you relate well to kindness, perhaps you could think of calling a friend who is lonely as soon as you have a moment. If you find that forgiveness resonates with you, you might reflect on how letting go of a grudge can lighten your emotional load, remembering that those who acted in a harmful or disappointing way may have been unaware of the harm they caused. Coming back to the mind as a garden to be cultivated, the seeds you plant grow into the trees, herbs, and flowers you wish to enjoy. Similarly, the thoughts you cultivate form the mind you live with and shape your life from. And so, we make a daily effort to cultivate helpful mental states and thoughts, thoughts that make life meaningful and rewarding. Each time you plant a thought of kindness, gratitude, or wisdom, you are actively shaping the future of your garden, ensuring it flourishes with the qualities that sup port its lushness and beauty. Effort to Mature Wholesome States of Mind The effort to mature the mind is about nurturing and strengthening help ful mental states that have already arisen, such as patience, contentment, or compassion. Using the garden metaphor, imagine you have already planted seeds of kindness, gratitude, or mindfulness. Now that these plants have taken root, you need to protect and fertilize them. In the same way, for wholesome thoughts and mental states to become well established, you need to consciously cultivate them through repeated practice. But remember, the mind, like fertile soil, can easily allow weeds of unhelpful thoughts to grow, such as negative self-talk, anxiety, or frustration, and we don t need to work very hard to think unhelpful thoughts. We seem to always have some in reserve! Unless we train our minds otherwise, it s so easy to get into habits of ruminating and worrying. Each time we practice a new skill or way of thinking, new neural connec tions are formed in specific areas of the brain. With repetition, neurons that communicate with each other to perform the new task become more strongly WEEK 2: REGULATING ATTENTION 73 connected, and a new habit is formed. The task becomes more automated and less effortful. And this applies to both helpful and unhelpful thoughts as well. Just as we can easily slip into negative thinking, going forward, we can create the same ease with positive mental states through consistent practice. The effort to mature the mind will help you construct and solidify new neu ral configurations in prefrontal areas of the brain and cultivate peaceful and rewarding ways of thinking about yourself, others, and the world. Beginning this week, in addition to your twice-daily meditation, we invite you to practice this fourfold Right Effort as often as possible in your daily life. Over time, you ll notice that life seems more meaningful and connected to your deeper values. You are truly the architect of your own mind, and the more care and effort you put into this process, the more satisfyingly your inner landscape will grow. This Week s Practice Mindfulness of Breath As you begin this week s meditation practice, it s essential to establish con sistency in your routine. Practicing regularly is key to making the gradual yet lasting changes in your brain that contribute to long-term well-being. The duration of your sessions is equally important, as effective practice requires enough time and repetition to allow the brain to adapt and rewire itself [32]. From this point forward, aim for one hour of practice daily, divided into two 30-minute sessions. This structure provides the time needed for your practice to have the desired impact on both your mindfulness skills and overall mental health. The benefits of regular meditation are often notice able relatively quickly. Remember, it s essential to commit to three key aspects of practice: frequency (practicing twice a day), duration (30min utes per session), and accuracy (following the audio and written instruc tions). Although the benefits vary between people t forget we also need to deal with our baggage with these essentials in place, our clients typically experience clear initial benefits, including a decrease in stress, ruminative thinking, and worry, after a week of committed and accurate practice. Applying Your Skills: Mindfulness of Thoughts During the This week, focus on developing mindfulness of thoughts and mental states by applying Right Effort throughout the day. Remember, Right Effort means working skillfully with your mental states preventing unhelpful thoughts from arising, abandoning those that have already surfaced, encouraging beneficial thoughts in their place, and cultivating beneficial thoughts that are already present. This applied practice will be most beneficial if imple mented numerous times throughout the day, allowing you to recognize 74 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE unhelpful thoughts and switch your attention to more productive thoughts with greater ease and acceptance. The practice itself doesn t create sensations; it brings to light sen sations that may have been just under the surface of your awareness. In other words, it allows you to feel what is already there.
