At this early stage of your practice, if the uncomfortable sensations are manageable, the key is to not give them too much significance. Continue practicing calmly, without attaching negative judgments to the sensations. Feel the discomfort with equanimity, and then systematically return your attention to the breath. By fully re-engaging attention with the breath, you can avoid getting caught in stories or judgments about the sensations. If the sensations are intense to the extent that you cannot focus on the breath, then bring your attention to the area of most intense sensation for 30 seconds to a minute. Observe the sensations with curiosity and openness, noticing their qualities without taking them personally. These sensations are just transient experiences arising in the body they are not your pain. They are impermanent, with a beginning, a middle, and an end. Research suggests that focusing on the area of discomfort with equanimity can significantly reduce the intensity of unpleasantness within 30seconds! [33, 34] We ll explore this technique further in Week WEEK 2: REGULATING ATTENTION 77 As you practice, remind yourself that these sensations do not define you. They are not permanent and do not belong to you. Instead, they are simply arising in this moment. With time and patient observation, you may find your relationship with the discomfort shifting, even if the sensations them selves persist. To do this, ll need to be attentive to any thoughts of doubt and the body sensations that arise alongside them. Instead of reacting to these sensations, observe them with curiosity and kindness. Notice that thoughts of self-doubt often center on and This focus on the self can amplify feelings of inadequacy, as we can always find something to criticize about ourselves. It can be a real relief to shift from judging yourself to focusing on the breath, remaining kind and patient with yourself. Abandon all expectations about yourself during the practice. When self-critical thoughts arise, see them for what they are just mental phenomena, not truths about you. Let go of the view that these thoughts define you. You can make it a key focus of your mindfulness practice this week, as an experiment! intermediate i don t really believe mindfulness practice is for me. my friends told me so many good things about 1 1.2 book Is being focused on something, like when I m really absorbed in a task, the same as mindfulness? It depends! While being mindful always involves attentiveness, not all attentiveness is mindfulness. For instance, a cat poised in front of a mouse hole or a sniper ready to shoot are both examples of being fully con centrated on the here and now. However, in these cases, the focus is driven by craving (catching the mouse) or aversion (fear of being attacked). This type of attention lacks the qualities of complete or right mindfulness. In mindfulness, attention is free from craving or aversion. For instance, when we focus on the breath during meditation, the aim is not to crave peace of mind or avoid unwanted thoughts, but rather to observe the experi ence as it is, without attachment. Being attentive can help us complete tasks 78 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE or remain alert, but mindfulness goes beyond that. It requires a state of openness, acceptance, and non-reactivity qualities that help us relate to our experiences without identifying with them. intermediate is being focused on something, like when i m really absorbed in a task, the same as mindfulness? 1 1.2 book I feel very tired, and I need to relax my back. However, especially when you re already tired, lying down tends to increase drowsiness even if you re not consciously aware of it. Your practice space might even turn into a snoring camp If you re feeling too tired or drowsy to practice, take a short rest. Having a 5- to 10- minute nap, followed by walking around to wake up, can help. Then, sit down to practice with refreshed energy. s also helpful to reflect on the desire to lie down. It may stem from a craving for relaxation and relief or an aversion to unpleasant body sensa tions. Avoidance can fool you if you re not vigilant. If you want the best and quickest results, we encourage you to practice in the way that mindfulness meditators have done for the past 2,500years. I still get caught up in thoughts for ages before I realize it is happening. How do you stay focused? This is common, no need for alarm! That moment of recogniz ing that attention has been caught up in thinking is actually very valuable. Congratulate yourself for making the effort to notice that this has occurred, because this is an important skill in and of itself. The quickest way to train your mind is to have the clear intention and firm determination that you will return attention to your breath within 3seconds of noticing a thought arising. This is called the 3-second rule. You might have observed that it s easier to recognize a thought the moment it appears than after a few seconds, once you ve already become engaged with it. If you don t let go of the thought immediately, you can easily get carried away and lost in it once you have succumbed to the temptation of thinking, as thinking is surprisingly addictive. Thinking is addictive because we get attached to feeling the pleasant or unpleasant body sensations that co-emerge with certain thoughts and to the brain chemicals that the brain produces at the same time. For example, a very pleasant thought will co-emerge with a very pleasurable body sensation and the associated dopamine, which can be very reinforcing. We also iden tify with thoughts and feel that they are part of who we are. You may already have noticed that it s easier to let go of thoughts that t have an emotional charge. In contrast, emotionally charged thoughts often co-emerge with strong body sensations, capturing your attention and WEEK 2: REGULATING ATTENTION 79 making them harder to release. When this happens, try identifying where these sensations are in the body. Shift your attention to that area and observe the sensations equanimously. Focus there until the intensity subsides, and then gently return your attention to the breath. Cultivating tolerance and patience is also essential for working with thoughts. Remind yourself that these are just thoughts and all of them share the same fundamental quality: they arise and pass away, and none of them are permanent. Not getting what we want whether it s focus or calmness while remaining patient strengthens our ability to respond similarly in daily life. This happens because we use the same brain in both contexts. When we develop patience in meditation, it naturally carries over into everyday situations. intermediate my mind is all over the place at times. i still get caught up in thoughts for ages before i realize 1 1.2 book During the meditation, I often ruminate over past issues with my ex, and I worry about what s going to happen in the future. I noticed that when I bring my attention to my breath, I immediately feel better. This week, I kept on doing this, but it s like my mind is obsessive, and these thoughts keep coming back. Should I keep doing the same thing? It doesn seem to be working. Redirecting attention from an unhelpful mental state to a neutral object of attention such as the breath can be helpful in some contexts. However, this is not likely to address the deeper aspects of the problem, which is why it doesn t seem to be working. When you refocus on the breath, you may be avoiding the sensations that co-emerge with your rumination and worry. Until the intensity of these sensations diminishes through mind ful and equanimous attention, the associated thoughts are likely to persist. Think of a thought as a helium balloon attached to a hook by a rope. The hook represents the body sensations that come with challenging thoughts. Until the rope is untied, the balloon cannot float freely. Similarly, unhelpful thoughts may continue to resurface until the underlying sensations are acknowledged and released. When intrusive thoughts are persistent, interrupt your focus on the breath and redirect your attention to the area of strongest sensation in your body. Observe the sensations with curiosity and acceptance, recognizing them as body sensations with the same fundamental characteristics as a thought: they are impermanent, therefore they cannot belong to you or be part of who you are, and identifying with them and reacting to them only brings more suffering. In daily life, too, refocusing attention on the breath is better than worry ing, but it may also become a way of avoiding what s happening in your body. Instead, notice how thoughts arise alongside body sensations and how these sensations influence the mind. By observing this interplay, you can begin to untie the rope, allowing both the thoughts and sensations to sub side more naturally. 80 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE intermediate during the meditation, i often ruminate over past issues with my ex, and i worry about what s going 1 1.2 book I keep thinking of my divorce and the kids, and I find it very difficult to focus after just a few seconds. You re beginning to see the process of thinking, rather than just the content of your thoughts. This is progress! Now, your task is to observe this mental state mindfully without resenting it for being the way it is. Simply acknowledge that this agitation is impermanent and will subside in time. Resisting or resenting it only prolongs the agitation. This habit of wishing things were different is what creates many of our challenges in life. We don t want them to be as they are. Mindfulness gives you the opportunity to change this habit of resenting internal experience and wishing it was different. Accepting your internal experience as it is, in some ways, is accepting parts of yourself. This alone can calm the mind both in meditation and in daily life. We are simply observing the way things are the reality of the present moment as it manifests itself when you breathe in and out, from moment to moment accepting whatever we discover. ve mentioned concerns about the divorce and your children, which may be contributing to the restlessness. It put me off practicing after the first 2 days. WEEK 2: REGULATING ATTENTION 81 Can I meditate with music in the background? I ve done this in the past, it helped distract me from the memories, and then it felt like I could practice better. Flashbacks and anxiety during mindfulness practice are actually relatively common, as many of us have experienced painful situations in life. Remember that practicing mindfulness is not only about concentrat ing, but also about building awareness of how our mind and body interact and function. While background music may have been soothing, it did not address the underlying painful memories, which are resurfacing during your practice nowadays. Music served as a way to avoid discomfort, offering temporary relief but not resolving the emotional charge of the memories. This avoid ance, while initially offering temporary relief, can actually intensify the emo tional charge of these memories through negative reinforcement. Each time you reduce discomfort by distracting yourself, you strengthen the habit of distraction. One of the problems with avoidance is that we cannot always avoid the memory, and the distress it creates doesn t get resolved. This is an opportunity to look more deeply at what these memories are made of. It s not the thoughts or memories themselves that create the reac tion; it is the accompanying body sensations that provoke discomfort and anxiety. By observing the sensations that co-emerge with the memories, you can deepen your understanding of the physicality of your emotional experi ence and further recognize the habitual tendency to react with aversion in order to avoid discomfort. Our minds are conditioned to react emotionally when sensations in the body become intense. This process is something you can directly observe, dissect, and understand in greater and greater detail in the laboratory of your practice. This mindful observation allows you to be sufficiently detached from the emotional intensity of the memory and view it as just a mental event, rather than as part of who you are you learn to not get caught up in it, to not make it yours or you. If the sensations become particularly intense, consider shifting your focus from the breath to the body sensations themselves. Direct your attention to the area of strongest sensation and study it carefully. Is there a predomi nance of certain characteristics, like temperature, heaviness, movement, or solidity? Is it dense or diffuse? Is it hot, warm, or cold? Is it heavy or light? Stay with it for a little while, remaining as calm and patient as possible. Keep in mind that the actual event is in the past and cannot harm you in the present. What matters now is how you relate to the sensations accompany ing the thoughts in the present. Note to the reader: While the approach described above can be very help ful, working with traumatic memories requires careful attention to your capacity and safety. If you find yourself consistently overwhelmed, consider 82 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE practicing under the guidance of a qualified therapist, preferably one trained in integrating mindfulness with exposure techniques. Bruno A. Cayoun and Andrea D. Grabovac. Great balance sustains all things. Chuang Tzu Practice Background and Rationale This week, we ll explore how our mind and body process emotional information. In this section of the chapter, you ll be introduced to the co-emergence model of reinforcement, which is a five-step framework for understanding emotional reactivity. It integrates Buddhist and Western psychologies with current findings in neuroscience, and we re excited to share it with you! You may find it helpful to reread this section a few times over the coming weeks, as it describes the important role of body sensations in emotion regulation and explains the rationale for focusing on body-scanning for the duration of this program. You explore the core elements of emotional states, what leads to emotional reactivity, and how to defuse this reactivity skillfully and effectively. Understanding the Mechanisms of Mind and Behavior How do our internal mental processes often operating below the surface of conscious awareness shape the way we react to daily events and challenges? In this chapter, we ll use the co-emergence model of reinforcement [1] to explore how the mind and body work together to process incoming informa tion. If you are a researcher or clinical professional, we encourage you to explore the detailed account of this model in the work of Bruno Cayoun and Week 3: Understanding and Regulating Emotions 86 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE Reaction or chosen response Sensory perception Evaluation Factual or self-referential, conscious or subconscious Co-emerging body sensations Situation internal or external Figure 3.1 The co-emergence model of reinforcement in an equilibrium state. Adapted from Cayoun (2011). Alice Shires, who demonstrate how incorporating this understanding into therapy can reduce distress across a wide range of mental health conditions [2]. The co-emergence model is not just a theoretical framework, it is also a prac tical tool grounded in centuries-old traditions and modern psychological sci ence [2]. It builds upon foundational principles from Buddhist psychology, particularly the concept of dependent origination, which teaches that all phe nomena, including thoughts, emotions, and behaviors, arise in dependence upon each other [3]. This ancient perspective aligns with modern neuroscience, which increasingly demonstrates how mental states and physiological processes are deeply interconnected in the generation of our emotional experiences. While Western psychology has long emphasized the relationship between cognition (thinking), emotion (feeling), and behavior (acting), the integration of bodily sensations into this framework is relatively recent. The co-emergence model bridges this gap, bringing together aspects of cognitive-behavioral therapy (CBT), affective neuroscience, and embodied cognition with the five aggregates of the mind and body in Buddhist psychology The result is a comprehensive model that represents how our mental and physical sensations co-arise and mutually reinforce each other, creating patterns of emotional response and reactivity. These patterns shape how we experience the world and how we conceptualize our sense of self. Understanding these interactions is key to transforming unhelpful emotional patterns into more adaptive, health-promoting decisions and actions. The Co-emergence Model of Reinforcement The five components of the co-emergence model are illustrated in Figure 3.1. Each box represents a specific function of the mind that is intimately depend ent on the others. Together, they provide a framework for understanding how we process incoming information. WEEK 3: UNDERSTANDING AND REGULATING EMOTIONS 87 To build a clear understanding, let s start with a description of each component and how it operates in daily life. Once we ve laid this founda tion, we ll examine what happens when this finely tuned system falls into disequilibrium. In this state, the harmonious interplay between sensory perception, cognitive evaluation, co-emergent body sensations, and subse quent behavior becomes destabilized, often leading to emotional distress or heightened reactivity. Fortunately, mindfulness training offers a powerful, scientifically sup ported way to restore equilibrium. By rebalancing this system, mindfulness fosters adaptive responses to life s challenges and supports emotional well-being. Situation Every experience occurs in the context of a situation. Thus, the first component of the co-emergence model is the Situation the external or internal event that triggers an unfolding process. The technical term for this initial trigger stimulus. An external event can be anything from the world around you the rain falling on the roof before you consciously register its sound, someone speak ing to you before you are aware of hearing them, or even the sight of a griz zly bear before you become consciously aware of its presence. Internal events include mental phenomena such as thoughts, images, internal sounds (like a tune playing in your head), and spontaneous memo ries. They also include physical states experienced through body sensations before we consciously perceive them for instance, the subtle tension from sitting in a particular posture or the dull ache of an arthritic knee. Sensory Perception The second component is Sensory Perception, which refers to our awareness of a situation as it makes contact with our senses. External situations are perceived through the five physical senses: smell, sight, taste, touch, and sound. But how do we perceive internal mental or physical events? The awareness of a thought entering your mind such as the spontaneous memory of an event is also considered a sensory perception. This process, known as metacognitive awareness, allows you to notice and recognize thoughts and mental states as they arise. The ability to sense internal physical sensations produced by a situation, such as feeling hot or cold due to room temperature or feeling pain from an injury, relies on interoception. This perceptual ability helps us detect sig nals originating from within the body, providing crucial information about our internal state like hunger, thirst, or discomfort. Together, these form the seven basic perceptual senses that most people experience, barring specific conditions. While individuals may vary in their sensitivity to certain senses, the fundamental mechanisms are consistent: we 88 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE generally taste salt as salt (not as sugar), see black as black (not as white), and smell perfume as perfume (not as sewage). However, the sensitivity and accuracy of our sensory perception can shift dramatically under stress or challenging experiences. For instance, during heightened emotional states, certain senses become more or less acute. This variability plays a key role in how we process intense situations, as we explore further in the disequilibrium section. Evaluation The third component of the co-emergence model is Evaluation, where we assign meaning to the sensory information we have just perceived. If you think about it, the sheer volume of information the brain processes each minute of the day is phenomenal! The mind s ability to evaluate incoming information quickly and efficiently has been essential for our survival as a species. Over millennia, humans have evolved to make rapid judgments about whether a situation is safe or dangerous. Much of the time this evalu ation occurs automatically, without conscious thought. For example, when we react instinctively to a car swerving in traffic or quickly regain our bal ance after slipping on ice, we rely on automatic processes that bypass detailed conscious evaluation. These same automatized processes are what allow us to learn complex skills, such as playing a musical instrument or riding a bicycle. However, while this rapid processing is very helpful in moments of physical danger, during sports, or for repetitive tasks, it also predisposes us to jump to conclusions at times and make inaccurate or unhelpful assessments. This is where automatized, habitual information processing can become problematic. Let s explore this in more detail. How is it that two people can share a dessert, and one of them enjoys it greatly while the other dislikes it? Or one movie critic applauds the latest block buster, whereas another tears it apart? These may seem like obvious examples, but they point to an underlying process that shapes every moment of our day. We each assess our circumstances based on their personal implications and what they signify for us. For example, rain may mean something very different to you than to someone else if you recently had a bad car accident in the rain and they hadn t. This may contribute to you driving more care fully in rainy conditions. Similarly, feeling tightness in the chest may mean something very different to you than to someone else if the last time you felt this kind of chest pain was just before a heart attack. The personal differ ences in how we evaluate the same event enable us to make predictions about possible outcomes and adapt our decisions and actions accordingly. Note that we always do so based on past experiences and the current situa tion being perceived. This applies to all situations that we perceive in the world and within ourselves. WEEK 3: UNDERSTANDING AND REGULATING EMOTIONS 89 Hence, when we try to make sense of a situation, we automatically filter it and interpret it through our own frame of reference, which is necessarily based on our past experiences. These filters include our beliefs, values and preferences, culture, needs, religion, memories of personal events, personality, and other aspects of ourselves, in a way that is consistent with our sense of who we are our sense of self. Because this is mostly a subconscious process, we are continu ally affected by our past, as previous experiences shape our evaluation of the current situation, whether or not they are truly relevant to the situation at hand. Predictive Processing and Core Schemas An important aspect of the way we evaluate the world and our experiences is the brain s inherent tendency to predict outcomes based on past experi ences, a process known as predictive processing. This is a fundamental sur vival mechanism embedded in the brain. To achieve this, the brain relies heavily on memories and learned patterns from the past to generate expecta tions about what will happen next, reducing uncertainty and enabling more efficient responses to environmental and internal stimuli [7]. These predic tions are continuously processed and updated as new information enters our awareness. Throughout the day, and of course, also when practicing mind fulness, these predictions can manifest as spontaneous evaluative thoughts. Several brain networks are involved in predictive processing. One of these, the default mode network, is a group of brain areas active when we are not focused on demanding tasks, like when we are daydreaming [8, 9]. This network is active when we think about ourselves, remember our past, and imagine possible scenarios about our future. For this reason, it is some times called the me network [10], as it becomes active when our evalua tions are based on our values, past experiences, and personal views. As will be explored in the next section, Co-Emerging Body Sensations, the brain makes predictions about outcomes based on how we feel when we think in certain ways. While specific memories of past experiences may be difficult to recall, their associated body sensations are often much more accessible, sometimes in the form of emotions. In essence, most important memories are associated with indeed, encased within body sensations, which emerge to guide our response. This mechanism of predictive processing allows us to navigate the world efficiently by relying on prior knowledge, without us needing to consciously think about it. However, this also means we are conditioned by the past [11]. At the most subtle level of conditioning, systems of belief develop through out our lives [12, 13]. While we might be aware of some of these beliefs, such as kind people should not suffer, and I shouldn t have to put up with my partner s attitude, much of the time, they function in the back ground, influencing our decision-making without our awareness. Many of these beliefs are fundamental to how we see ourselves and oth ers and were created earlier in life. In cognitive therapy, these are called 90 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE core schemas. Typical examples of unhelpful core schemas are m not good enough, There s something fundamentally wrong with me, Nobody loves me, or People I love will abandon me. You will note that core schemas are characterized by an emphasis on the and the When we keep associating these belief systems with our sense of who we are, we both maintain our attachment to our sense of self and keep on living in the past. In turn, this slows or even prevents inner change; it prevents us from outgrowing our past. Maladaptive schemas are irrational views not because they completely lack evidence in our present daily life, but because they place an overly high focus on the and the As such, they are the source of great suffering. We will discuss this topic further in Week In contrast, helpful schemas promote healthier ways of relating to our selves and others. Examples include I am capable of learning and growing, I deserve love and respect, and so do others, and Mistakes are part of being human, and they do not define me. These adaptive schemas encour age flexibility, compassion, and resilience. Schemas, whether helpful or unhelpful, are not fixed. The brain continu ously updates its predictions with new information. Mindfulness training leverages this capacity, helping us process information more accurately and shift toward healthier patterns, allowing us to live with greater free dom and joy. Co-emerging Body Sensations Co-emerging Body Sensations, the fourth component in the co-emergence model, describes the internal physical sensations that arise as an immediate consequence of our evaluation of incoming sensory information. These sensa tions are the bodily manifestation of our mental evaluations. This is why the simultaneous co-occurrence of mind and body is called co-emergence. Imagine a scale where the more personally significant a situation is whether positively or negatively the stronger and more noticeable the body sensations become. This phenomenon is well-supported by modern emotion research, which demonstrates that body sensations are integral to emotional processing [14]. Indeed, body sensations are the building blocks of emotions [15]. Without feeling specific bodily sensations even if only vaguely we cannot experience emotions. Although these sensations often remain below the threshold of awareness, when they become sufficiently intense, their signals in the brain increase and the brain alerts us to something that could be important to consider. We then consciously perceive them as pain, pleasure, or fully formed emotions. Two Sources of Body Sensations It is important to distinguish between body sensations that arise from direct sensory perception those triggered by immediate sensory contact with the physical world and sensations that co-emerge with our mental evaluations. WEEK 3: UNDERSTANDING AND REGULATING EMOTIONS 91 Sensory perception, the second component of this model discussed earlier, occurs almost instantaneously in response to physical stimuli. It represents the very early, rapid stage of information processing that precedes our conscious evaluation of an event. For example, if we touch something extremely hot, the sensation of heat occurs almost instantly, and the body reacts to the physical stimulus before any conscious thought occurs. In contrast, co-emergent sensa tions arise as a consequence of our mental evaluation of a situation. For instance, the butterflies in the stomach experienced during fear don t arise directly from sensory perception but are triggered by our evaluation of a potential threat, which arises from the brain s default mode network. Both types of sensations are processed through interoception our ability to sense internal bodily states. Interoception is primarily processed in the insula, a brain region that connects the cerebral cortex (including the default mode network) with the limbic area of the brain, where the amygdala triggers emo tional responses. Interoceptive awareness allows us to feel both types of sensa tions, whether they arise from physical stimuli or co-emerge with conscious or subconscious mental evaluations. In this way, body sensations reflect a com plex interplay between the mind and body, and the role of co-emergent sensa tions is particularly critical, as they are shaped by our thoughts and beliefs. Two Relationships Between Mind and Body Past evaluations can create predictive pathways that inform how quickly and intensely body sensations arise in the present. For example, simply see ing an object resembling a snake, like a coiled rope or garden hose, might evoke the same intense body sensations of fear as seeing a real snake. This occurs because the brain has already made a rapid evaluation based on past experiences, setting off an automatic cascade of body sensations. This fast route to co-emergence allows for a swift, often subconscious, reaction. In contrast, core schemas may take a longer path, generating body sensa tions over time as more complex cognitive processes are activated. Via this slower route, neutral thoughts those that don t involve personally impor tant matters produce only neutral body sensations. These are often so sub tle that they go unnoticed unless we are deeply relaxed or highly skilled in mindfulness. Understanding how thoughts create co-emerging body sensations requires examining two fundamental relationships between mind and body. These relationships help explain both the intensity and quality of the sensations we experience: 1 Intensity: The more a thought is about a topic that we find personally important, thereby activating our me network [10], the more intense the sensation we feel in the body though this does not necessarily mean that we will notice it or even become aware of it [16]. This constitutes one relationship between mind and body: the level of identification with a thought determines the intensity of the body sensation (its salience 92 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE Indeed, it all starts with the The more we identify with the circumstances we evaluate, the stronger the sensations in the body, and the more intense our emotional response becomes. This includes associations with reputation, philosophy, car, family, health, etc. Since anything we label as becomes part of thinking about it will naturally trigger body sensations. In other words, the more we evaluate something as having personal implications, and therefore linked to our sense of self (the ), the more intense the co-emerging sensations in the body will be. This experience is likely familiar, and it is consistently supported by neu roscience research [17]. 2 Quality: Additionally, the more we evaluate a situation as agreeable, the more pleasant the body sensations are. Equally, the more we view a situ ation as disagreeable, the more unpleasant the body sensations become. This describes the second relationship between mind and body: the agreeableness of the thought determines the type and degree of pleasant ness or unpleasantness of the body sensation (its hedonic tone Ripples in a Lake Imagine yourself at the center of concentric circles, with each ring represent ing different levels of personal relevance. Just as ripples in a pond are strong est at the center and weaken as they spread outward, our body sensations are most intense when situations affect the innermost circle our sense of ourselves and become progressively weaker as they involve people or events further from our core sense of who we are. s explore in more detail how our evaluation of a situation influences the intensity of these co-emerging body sensations. Consider the following three scenarios: In the first, you hear that a friendly neighbor s cousin has recently been diagnosed with an incurable disease. Since you don t know this person, how does this news affect you? It s likely that, while you may feel some sympathy, it is unlikely to significantly disrupt your day. Now, in the second scenario, imagine hearing that it is your neighbor who has received this diagnosis. How would this news change your experience? The personal connection to your neighbor increases the personal signifi cance of the situation for you, and you are likely to feel more discomfort, anxiety, or sadness compared to the first scenario. Finally, in the third scenario, you receive the news that you yourself have been diagnosed with this incurable disease. There is little doubt that the intensity of your body sensations would increase manyfold. You might feel intense anxiety, often felt as changes in body temperature, faster movement, and constriction in various areas of the body. These may be experienced as cold hands, a racing heart, and shortness of breath. If the news is really shocking, you might even dissociate, feeling numb or disconnected from your body, as a way of coping. Alternatively, you might feel sadness, typi cally experienced as heaviness and tightness in various areas of the body. WEEK 3: UNDERSTANDING AND REGULATING EMOTIONS 93 When our own health is threatened, we suffer emotionally, even though the health of a stranger is just as valuable and costly to lose. It is only when the issue relates to or someone we closely identify with (e.g., my child), that suffering arises through the medium of unpleasant and intense body sensations. The same principle applies to pleasant situations, in which case the body sensations are pleasant. Again, let s put this in context through the following three scenarios. In the first, you hear that a friend of your sister is very fond of a man she met a few months ago, and that he recently said to her, You mean everything to me; I really love you. How would your evaluation of this news lead you to feel in your body? Since you don t know this person, your sensations might be neutral or mildly pleasant. In the second scenario, you learn that it is your sister s new boyfriend who said, You mean everything to me; I really love you. Would you feel the same as in the first scenario? In the third scenario, imagine that a person whom you are truly fond of says to you, You mean everything to me; I really love you. How would your evaluation of these words lead you to feel in your body this time? Likely, your experience would be much more intense! The same words, and the same potential for love, yet such a different experience in each case. This pattern will be consistent with other pleasant events, such as three people who have just won the lottery or achieved a major goal. The more personal importance we place on a situation, the stronger our pleasant body sensa tions will be. Reaction The final component of the co-emergence model is Reaction. This refers to our habitual ways of reducing distress or amplifying pleasure through behaviors, thoughts, or actions that reduce unpleasant sensations or amplify pleasant ones. The strength of the signals from the brain s insula, which is responsible for interoception, to the amygdala influences how likely we are to react [18]. Note that a reaction is not the same as an action. By and large, reacting is a form of re-enacting a learned behavior that has reduced discomfort or increased pleasure in the past. A reaction always depends on preceding bod ily sensations. In contrast, actions are more deliberate and may involve novel or mindful responses. Not all reactions result in external behavior. Many reactions occur inter nally as thoughts or mental patterns, such as worry, rumination, or obses sions. For example, you might have sexual desire for your partner who would likely welcome it, but you don t express it because you fear that revealing your desire could be embarrassing. Alternatively, you might feel hurt by someone who is verbally abusive, but you choose to suppress what you feel because you fear this person might abandon you. In both situations, there is an internal reaction that is unexpressed. 94 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE Two Relationships Between Feeling and Acting As is the case between Evaluation and Co-emerging Body Sensations, there are two key relationships between Co-Emerging Body Sensation and Reaction: 1 Intensity of sensations determines likelihood of reactivity: The stronger the body sensation, the more likely we are to react. For example, an intense panic symptom, such as a pounding heart and difficulty breath ing, usually triggers a stronger reaction than mild discomfort in the abdomen associated with worry. Thus, sensation intensity directly influ ences reaction intensity. 2 Hedonic tone of sensations determines reaction type: The quality or tone of the sensation whether pleasant, unpleasant, or neutral determines the type of reaction. Pleasant sensations trigger attachment and lead to craving. When sensations feel good, we tend to desire more of them, wanting to extend or increase the experience. This corresponds to the craving to obtain discussed in Week 1. For example, despite being satisfied after a delicious meal, we may keep eating to sustain the pleasurable sensations produced by both the food and our positive eval uation of the food. Unpleasant sensations trigger aversion and lead to avoidance. When sensations feel bad, we instinctively try to avoid or reduce them. This is the craving to avoid from Week 1. For example, if we have a phobia of spiders and believe some might be present in a friend s house, being invited over for dinner might trigger intense fear and co-emerging body sensations, prompting us to decline the invitation with an excuse. On to the Next Loop and the Process of Learning The time between perceiving a stimulus (situation) and reacting is less than a second! Once we react, the reaction itself becomes the new stimulus, or situation, for the next perception and we go around for another loop! This cycle of perception and reaction never stops, even during sleep. Reactions themselves are continually evaluated as agreeable or disagreeable, generating new body sensations, which in turn lead to further reactions. Consistent with Buddhist thought [19], findings from affective neuroscience and behavioral studies confirm that this cycle of co-emergent mind and body interactions does not cease 23]. This continuous flow of information processing is central to principles of conditioning, where habitual reactions are either reinforced or transformed based on their outcomes. For instance, when a very pleasurable body sensa tion is associated with a specific behavior, we are likely to repeat that behav ior to recreate the same pleasurable experience. If this sensation arises from a rewarding action, such as the satisfaction of being kind to someone, it will reinforce the desire to be kind, encouraging kindness to become a more frequent habit. WEEK 3: UNDERSTANDING AND REGULATING EMOTIONS 95 Similarly, behaviors like overeating or smoking can become conditioned through their association with pleasant sensations. In other words, we are likely to use overeating or smoking to obtain more of these pleasant sensa tions in the body. These behaviors are reinforced because they produce a positive experience that we subconsciously seek to recreate this is called positive reinforcement. Conversely, if overeating or smoking helps relieve unpleasant sensations, such as anxiety or discomfort, these behaviors are reinforced as coping mechanisms, even when the reaction occurs subconsciously [16]. This is called negative reinforcement, as the behavior is reinforced by reducing or removing an unpleasant ( negative ) experience. Common habits like emotional eating or excessive screen time whether through TV, computers, or smartphones are really just attempts to avoid unpleasant sensations, or to experience pleasant body sensations, which we have become attached to. If watching TV or playing games on our phone alleviates unpleasant sensations or creates pleasant ones, we can easily become addicted to, or dependent on, these activities. The same principle applies to consumerism in general, where the act of buying things stimulates pleasant sensations, fueling the urge for more. s essential to recognize that we are not addicted to the stimulus or situation itself: we are addicted to the sensations co-emerging with our evaluation of it, from the meaning we assign to it. This understanding of conditioning has significant implications for how we understand and address subconsciously learned behaviors, as discussed in Bob s story in the previous chapter. System in Disequilibrium When we experience a stressful situation, the balance between the four func tions of the mind sensory perception, evaluation, co-emerging body sensa tions, and reaction becomes disrupted. This state of disequilibrium is depicted in Figure 3.2, where the unequal sizes of the boxes represent how attention is withdrawn from sensory perception and co-emerging body sen sations and reallocated toward the evaluative and reactive functions of the mind [24]. Neuroaffective research shows that this shift increases our reli ance on assumptions stored in memory. While this process enables the rapid reactions necessary for survival, it simultaneously reduces our capacity to perceive situations objectively and to feel early emotional signals arising in the body. This state of disequilibrium occurs during distressing experiences and can become a learned pattern when feeling distressed becomes habitual, as seen in chronic depression [25, 26]. You may have noticed that when you are stressed, it becomes harder to engage your senses effectively. For instance, during an emergency, your ability to smell, taste, hear, and see as clearly may diminish compared to normal conditions. This reduction in sensory capacity has been well-documented in studies investigating eyewitness testimony 96 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE after crimes [27, 28] witnesses often have poor visual memory. Similarly, it becomes difficult to feel common body sensations when we are distressed. The more distressed you are, the harder it is to perceive these sensations. Imagine being chased by a bear or a shark. Would you notice a mosquito biting you on your forehead? Most likely, you would not. In contrast, you would likely feel a mosquito bite now as you are reading this chapter. This is similar to what happens in high-impact sports, where athletes may sustain injuries without feeling pain during the game but experience significant pain once the game is over. When we perceive a situation as a potential threat, we tend to dissociate from our bodies to varying degrees, reducing our ability to feel bodily sensa tions. This dissociation serves as a coping mechanism, allowing us to fight or flee without being hindered by intense sensations. For example, brief dis sociation can enable someone to escape a fire without being distracted by the pain of minor burns or panic symptoms. It also helps a paramedic get on with the job while witnessing severe injuries. In cases of trauma, disso ciation can lead to difficulties remembering aspects of the event, particularly the most distressing parts. People may recall fragments like a smell or sound but struggle to remember the complete sequence of events. For most of us, this disequilibrium state is just as common in our daily lives as the equilibrium state. Our nervous system is designed to handle short-term stress and usually recovers well from it. Short-term stress responses are not inherently problematic and can even be functional [29]. However, prolonged or habitual stress leads to chronic disequilibrium. When it becomes chronic, disequilibrium can become our new normal. Over time, brain regions associated with persistently evaluative and reactive Sensory perception Reaction internalized or externalized Attempt to decrease unpleasant sensations or increase pleasant sensations Co-emerging body sensations Situation internal or external Evaluation conscious or subconscious Factual information is minimized and self-referential information is maximized Figure 3.2 The co-emergence model of reinforcement in a disequilibrium state. Adapted from Cayoun (2011). WEEK 3: UNDERSTANDING AND REGULATING EMOTIONS 97 mental states become more strongly interconnected [30]. We inadvertently train our brains to become overly judgmental and reactive, which shapes our personality. Neurological research shows that patterns of neural network connectivity come to reflect this disequilibrium over time [31]. Specifically, the salience network, which includes the insular cortices that enable us to feel bodily sensations, plays a key role in coordinating the brain s response to emo tional stimuli [32]. It does this by balancing the default mode network (which evaluates situations based on past experiences) and the executive network (which regulates rational decision-making and inhibits emotional reactivity). During disequilibrium, this balance is momentarily disrupted through overactivation of the default mode network and underactivation the executive network. This imbalance results in our expressed suppressed emotional reactivity. Each time we get caught up in a thought during meditation, or when we ruminate, worry, or daydream, we are operating in a reactive mode. When a thought pops up in the mind (the situation in Figure 3.2), our ability to see it objectively as just a thought becomes limited by our identification with it (reduced sensory perception, including metacognitive awareness, in Figure 3.2). We may become judgmental about the thought or its content (increased evalu ation), leading to co-emerging body sensations that we may not consciously clearly feel (reduced body sensation). We then react with more thoughts because the sensation is either pleasant or unpleasant (increased reaction). When this imbalance persists over time, brain networks reconfigure to reflect this habitual pattern, making reactivity and judgment our default state. Over time, we might internalize this reactivity as part of our identity, concluding, That s just the way I am! Example of a Disequilibrium State Josh, a neurodivergent teenager with severe obsessive-compulsive disorder (OCD) and on the autism spectrum, spent most of his day on a couch watch ing TV and repeatedly playing the same video game while his mother was at work. He also slept on that couch rather than in his bed, fearing that his bedsheets were too dirty. He struggled with an overwhelming urge to send unfiltered or rude emails, which had gotten him in trouble at school. Unless he avoided using devices altogether, he couldn t resist this compulsion, which seemed to tighten its grip each time he tried to restrain it. Recognizing that avoiding devices wasn t a sustainable solution especially since so much learning takes place online Josh decided to take a break from his studies for the rest of the year. During this time, he hoped that therapy might help him manage his impulses and ease the relentless pull of his compulsions. Figure 3.3 shows a schematic representation of Josh s difficult experience with trying to practice Progressive Muscle Relaxation during Week 1, understood through the disequilibrium state in the co-emergence model of reinforcement. This information was recorded using the Diary of Reactive 98 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE Habits, which we can use to examine stressful situations within the five boxes of the co-emergence model (see Figure 3.4 below). 1 Situation: For Josh, the stressful situation was thinking about listening to PMR audio instructions on his smartphone or computer. He didn have a CD player available to play the CD version at the time he later borrowed his uncle s CD player. 2 Sensory perception: He was unable to simply notice the thoughts about needing to use his device as just thoughts. Instead, all his attention was directed to the meaning and implications of the thoughts, which intensi fied the evaluation aspect of his experience. 3 Evaluation: When examining the evaluation component, we repeatedly ask what a particular thought or belief means to us, if it were true. In Josh s case, the conversation unfolded like this: Therapist: What does it mean to you that you need to use your phone or computer? Josh: If I use the device, I might do something bad with it, like sending rude messages to someone. Therapist: And if that happened, what would it mean to you? Josh: It would mean I could get in trouble. Therapist: And what would be the implication of getting in trouble? Sensory perception Evaluation Co-emerging body sensations Situation external Reaction external partly conscious Figure 3.3 Example of the co-emergence model of reinforcement in disequilib rium state for a neurodivergent client with severe OCD. WEEK 3: UNDERSTANDING AND REGULATING EMOTIONS 99 Josh: I could get expelled from school. Therapist: If you were expelled, what would that mean to you? Josh: My future will be damaged. Therapist: Can you tell me more about how it might be damaged? Josh: Yeah, I ll be useless. I won t be able to do anything with my life. This exchange reveals that simply thinking the mere thought of using his phone triggered a deep-seated failure schema in Josh a belief that he wouldn t be able to achieve anything in life without an education. While his concern about jeopardizing his future career was understandable given the realities of schooling, this failure schema had its roots in earlier experiences. Formed in childhood, it stemmed from peer rejection and bullying. Over time, this led to a persistent sense of low self-worth and unworthiness, particularly in relationships. This was later reinforced by feeling responsible for his father s decision to leave the family. 4 Co-emergent body sensation: Sensations of constriction in the chest and abdomen immediately co-emerged with the failure schema, though he was not consciously aware of them at the time. Nevertheless, he did identify a vague feeling of being upset. This disconnect is common because during distressing events our ability to feel body sensations can be limited. Sensory perception Evaluation Co-emerging body sensations Situation internal or external Reaction internalized or externalized conscious or subconscious Figure 3.4 Diary of Reactive Habits for tracking disequilibrium states. Record a recent stressful experience to identify your own pattern of disequilibrium. 100 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE 5 Reaction: When asked, What did you do to make yourself feel better?" Josh replied, I avoided using all devices and didn t do my practice until my uncle brought me his CD player, and then I used your CD. 6 Next loop and its reinforcing habit: The immediate sense of relief from avoiding his device was rapidly detected through Sensory Perception (box 2). In turn, this confirmed his usual understanding through Evaluation (box 3) that avoiding anxiety-provoking situations is the most effective way of feeling relieved. The simultaneous consequence of this rapid, automatic, reassuring evaluation prevented the proliferation of further anxiety symptoms through Co-emerging Body Sensations (box 4). As a result, his previously learned avoidant behavior through Reaction (box 5) was once again reinforced because it stopped the expe rience of unpleasant body sensations. s important to note that during the stressful experience, what Josh was actually avoiding was his bodily sensations, and he was not directly avoiding the consequences of sending rude emails. Therefore, Josh s ther apy needed to focus on developing skills to discover this mechanism through his own experience and unlearn the habit of avoiding unpleas ant body sensations hence the advantage of practicing mindfulness meditation in Mindfulness-integrated Cognitive Behavior Therapy (MiCBT). Can you recall a specific stressful situation where you clearly experienced a state of disequilibrium? If so, take a moment to reflect on it and record your experience in Figure 3.4. Consequences of Prolonged Disequilibrium State of Disequilibrium and Memory The disequilibrium state has significant consequences for cognitive func tioning, particularly memory [33]. From early childhood, every personally significant experience is processed and stored in memory with a co-emerging body sensation. However, as discussed in the earlier example of trauma, one consequence of disequilibrium is a reduced capacity to feel body sensations. This diminished awareness of sensations can impair memory retrieval, as it is difficult to recall emotional events clearly without accessing the associated body sensations [2]. This memory impairment is also reflected in the common complaint among individuals experiencing high levels of stress, anxiety, or depression: difficulty with concentration and memory [34]. Even simple tasks, such as reading and remembering what we ve read, can feel challenging due in part to varying levels of dissociation, which reduces both the ability to concen trate and to feel bodily sensations. When we are distressed, we often lose the ability to feel these sensations clearly even though they may be more intense disrupting the link needed for effective recall. WEEK 3: UNDERSTANDING AND REGULATING EMOTIONS 101 Until the nervous system re-establishes balance between Sensory Perception, Evaluation, Co-emerging Body Sensations, and Reaction, tasks requiring focus and memory will likely remain difficult. Essentially, the more relaxed and attuned we are to our body sensations, the more effec tively we can recall benign, subtle, or older memories. This balance will be explored further in the following chapters. State of Disequilibrium and the Sense of Self The state of disequilibrium significantly impacts our sense of presence. As discussed earlier, our sense of self is deeply intertwined with body sensa tions, which we rely on to understand who we are both physiologically and emotionally [17]. For individuals who have experienced traumatic events in childhood particularly emotional abuse or neglect there is often an adaptive tendency to avoid feeling bodily sensations associated with emotional pain. While this avoidance may serve as a protective mechanism in the short term, over time, it can contribute to clinical conditions such as chronic depression and diffi culties in recognizing or understanding emotions as they arise. Avoiding body sensations can also contribute to fragmenting one s sense of identity. For instance, individuals with borderline personality disorder, many of whom report sustained and complex trauma, often describe being unable to identify their emotions. They may feel persistently dissociated, sometimes engaging in self-harm as a way to feel real [35]. Maintaining a state of disequilibrium in how we process information not only impairs the ability to recall traumatic memories but can also disrupt our sense of presence and aliveness. This brings us back to the fundamental principle discussed earlier: our sense of self is in large part constructed through our capacity to feel body sensations, and when this is chronically impaired, so too is our experience of being a coherent, present self. The Role of Mindfulness Restoring Equilibrium, Abandoning Suffering As you can see, maintaining disequilibrium between the four mental func tions in the co-emergence model Sensory Perception, Evaluation, Co emergent Interoception, and Reaction perpetuates distress. Thirty years of psychological research demonstrate that mindfulness meditation applies mental effort skillfully to restore balance among these components [1]. In essence, practicing mindfulness meditation is practicing equilibrium itself. An effective mindfulness meditation technique requires us to pay atten tion to our bodily experience (increasing awareness of body sensations) in an objective way (increasing objective sensory perception), without making judgments about the sensations (decreasing self-referential evaluation), and without reacting to any co-emerging sensations (decreasing reaction). Many 102 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE studies support the beneficial impacts of meditation on the brain. For exam ple, a pivotal study by Norman Farb and his colleagues at the University of Toronto [36] found that meditation decreased activity in the brain s default mode network the network associated with mind-wandering and rumina tion. This reduction fosters a clearer, calmer focus on body sensations and more balanced emotional responses. The more we practice, the stronger and more established this equilibrium becomes. Over time, we become more aware and less judgmental, leading to decreased emotional reactivity and a heightened capacity to choose adaptive responses proactively. This shift often brings about profound personal trans formation, as even personality traits are not fixed and can be reshaped by letting go of the factors that sustain them [37]. These changes are well documented through biological evidence, which shows reduced connectivity in brain regions that maintain unhelpful mental habits, alongside the creation of new neural connections that support well-being [38]. Ongoing neuroscience research continues to map these changes to specific areas of the brain, illustrating the tangible impact of mindfulness on mental and emotional health [36, 39, 40]. Body Sensations and Emotional Freedom As we ve discussed, we are less likely to react unless body sensations reach a certain level of intensity or salience. When sensations of sufficient inten sity do arise whether or not we are fully aware of them and we lack the skills to address them effectively, reacting becomes almost inevitable. This underscores the significance of body sensations as the locus of reinforce ment [1, 2], the very place from which reinforcement of our conditioned sense of self is perpetuated. It is also the place from which our conditioned well-being can emerge. By learning to feel body sensations without reacting to or identifying with them, we create the conditions necessary for desensitization. This process neutralizes habitual reactivity, irrespective of the situation. Over time, this skill enables us to engage with challenging situations without becoming trapped in cycles of emotional pain or avoiding them when they arise. Can you imagine the profound transformation that could take place through training these skills to minimize emotional reactivity? The broader field of mindfulness research has shown remarkable benefits across a wide range of psychological and physical conditions, highlighting its transforma tive potential. For an overview of how MiCBT contributes to this growing body of evidence, see the text box below. MiCBT Research in Brief The effectiveness of MiCBT across a range of diagnoses has been increas ingly supported by recent research. Studies have shown that MiCBT effec tively treats anxiety and depression in pregnant women [41], women with multiple sclerosis [42], and individuals with type 2 diabetes [43]. It has also WEEK 3: UNDERSTANDING AND REGULATING EMOTIONS 103 proven effective in managing chronic pain [44, 45], acute pain [46], and improving pain self-efficacy in women with breast cancer [47]. Additional benefits include reducing anxiety, depression, and fatigue, while enhancing sleep quality and hope in adults with multiple sclerosis [48], and improving emotion regulation and reducing body image-related distress in cancer sur vivors post-mastectomy [49]. MiCBT has also been found to reduce sub stance addiction compared to established treatments [50], and to decrease sports-related anxiety and pessimism while enhancing flow states and adherence to self-care through mindfulness training in competitive athletes [51]. A study by Sarah Francis and colleagues examined MiCBT s transdiag nostic effectiveness on psychological distress in adults with various condi tions. Results showed that MiCBT was significantly more effective than treatment as usual in reducing anxiety, depression, and stress while improv ing flourishing across a wide range of conditions [52]. Notably, equanimity emerged as the most potent mechanism of change, suggesting that this core skill underlies MiCBT s transdiagnostic efficacy. These psychological benefits may even extend to genetic expression. An epigenetic study investigating potential genetic markers for perinatal depres sion found that the expression of four genes differed between depressed and nondepressed women. After an 8-week MiCBT program, depression fully subsided, and gene expression patterns matched those of the nondepressed group [53]. Furthermore, a study comparing four treatments for OCD MiCBT, stand ard CBT, Acceptance and Commitment Therapy (ACT), and Metacognitive Therapy (MT) found that while all treatments were initially effective, only participants in the MiCBT and ACT groups maintained their gains at one month follow-up [54]. While further research is needed, these findings high light MiCBT s potential as a robust approach to treating OCD. The studies summarized here underscore MiCBT s role in mindfulness research, showcasing its impact across various conditions. These findings highlight the profound changes possible when mindfulness becomes a way of being moving from practicing mindfulness to progressively becoming mindful. Training Mindfulness Skills Checking Your Readiness Congratulations on completing your first week of mindfulness training! How did it go? Were you able to consistently practice for 30minutes twice a day? If so, have you noticed any benefits? Are you able to maintain your attention on the breath without getting immediately carried away by 104 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE thoughts? This doesn t mean thoughts don t arise s about staying focused on the breath even as thoughts arise and pass in the background. When to Proceed to the Next Step Before moving on to the next stage, take a moment to assess the quality of your mindfulness of breath practice. Are you able to let go of thoughts more quickly than when you first began? Can you focus on the sensations of the breath for at least 30seconds (around six to nine breaths)? Do you notice a calmer mind and less emotional charge in the thoughts that arise during practice? If so, you re ready to begin practicing mindfulness of body sensa tions using the body-scanning technique described below and in the accom panying audio guide. If You Need More Time s common to need a bit more time to refine your mindfulness of breath practice, especially if you ve been feeling stressed, anxious, or agitated. Does your mind frequently feel unsettled or emotionally charged during practice? If so, this is not a problem at all! Take a few more days, or a week or two, to continue practicing mindfulness of breath on your own, this time without any audio recording, in silence, as this can sharpen your focus and help you stay alert. And another week of practice will give you more time to strengthen your ability to shift attention from a thought back to the breath again, fur ther developing equanimity in the process. When the mind is easily distracted or agitated, it becomes difficult to notice and feel subtle sensations, which can make body-scanning practice more challenging than it would otherwise be. This is why continuing to practice mindfulness of breath for another week can be so beneficial helps calm the mind and improve your ability to connect with bodily sensa tions. As discussed above, the disequilibrium state associated with high lev els of stress or anxiety can have a dissociative effect on the mind, reducing your ability to feel common bodily sensations. If you decide to continue with mindfulness of breath for now, pause here and revisit the rest of this chapter once you re ready to begin body-scanning. This ensures that what you are reading aligns with your current practice, supporting a balanced integration of experiential learning and theoretical understanding. Unilateral Body-Scanning The Practice This week, you will begin learning the practice of body-scanning, as taught in the Burmese Vipassana tradition [55], which has been practiced for over 2,500years [56]. In this meditation technique, you will cultivate mindful ness of body sensations (vedananupassana in Pali), adding the fourth and WEEK 3: UNDERSTANDING AND REGULATING EMOTIONS 105 final modality of mindfulness training to your journey. The goal is to progressively become an impartial observer of body sensations, paying care ful and nonjudgmental attention to them and their pleasant, unpleasant, or neutral tone, without engaging in them. The term scanning is metaphori cal; it describes systematically directing your attention across the entire body to feel sensations, rather than visualizing them. Using the audio instructions on track 8 or the Body-Scanning section in Stage 1.3 of the MiCBT Guide app, you will systematically survey the body from head to toe and back again, moving your attention through 2- to 3-in. (5- to 8-cm) areas at a time. Your task is to observe and accept all sensations as they are, without trying to change them. This includes welcoming pleas ant, unpleasant, and neutral sensations alike. As you scan, practice non reactivity maintain equanimity and move to the next area without clinging to or avoiding any particular sensation. Then return to the top of the head in the same way. Be patient and atten tive, observing and accepting each sensation before moving on. The key is equanimity allowing sensations to arise and pass without attachment or aversion. This practice mirrors how you can work with emotions in daily life: feel them, accept them for what they are, and move forward without reactivity. This is known as skill transfer, as the abilities cultivated in med itation carry over into everyday situations. At first, you may not feel sensations in every part of the body, as the brain pathways for sensing those areas may not be well-connected yet. No wor ries! An important aspect of body-scanning is approaching these blank areas with a patient and kind attitude. This gentle attention helps train the areas of your brain responsible for sensing body sensations, promoting neuroplasticity the brain s ability to reorganize and form new neural con nections. Just as you train muscles to grow stronger through consistent exer cise, your ability to perceive subtle body sensations will develop with practice. t let self-doubt creep in! If you don t feel sensations in a particular area a completely normal experience at this stage of training gently keep your attention on that blank area for up to 10seconds. With patience and equanimity, simply observe, and then move on without reacting. Note also that a body sensation is anything you feel in the body. This includes a vague, subtle warmth when relaxed, itches caused by dry skin, discomfort related to sitting posture, or injury, tightness in the neck and shoulders due to stress, or an overall heaviness from fatigue. Sometimes, we mistakenly believe we can t feel anything because we re searching for some thing extraordinary or out of the ordinary, making it easy to feel disheart ened and discouraged. Remember, if you feel it, it s a sensation! Measuring Awareness of Body Sensations After your first and last body-scanning practices this week, measure your interoceptive awareness using the Interoceptive Awareness Indicator in 106 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE Figure 3.5. Color in the parts of the silhouette where you felt any type of body sensation during your most recent practice. For example, if you only notice bodily sensations in your fingers and toes, color only the fingers and toes. Please avoid leaving empty spaces where you can feel sensations. Ignore the squares and numbers while coloring in the body parts. It is okay if you color slightly over the silhouette edge. This will give you a visual representation of how much of the body you are feeling. You can also calculate the percentage of your body s surface area in which you can feel sensations, as per the following instructions: Total interoceptive awareness= ( _________% front+_________% back)/2 _________%.
