Skills You Will Learn Restoring Equilibrium Through Body-Scanning Body-scanning practices enhance emotion regulation and restore equilib rium in information processing by cultivating interoceptive awareness the ability to sense and be aware of our body s internal sensations [57, 58]. Increasing awareness of co-emerging body sensations while decreasing reac tivity to their unpleasant, pleasant, and neutral tone helps create balance and breaks the cycle of emotional reactivity. Perhaps you ve had the experience of watching clouds in the sky. While walking or riding in a moving vehicle, clouds may seem stationary. But when we pause and look closely, we notice their motion and ever-changing forms. Similarly, body-scanning allows you to pause and become aware of subtle sensations that might otherwise go unnoticed. During mindfulness of breath, you may have already glimpsed connections between your thoughts and body sensations for example, how a specific thought can trigger physical sensations, which in turn compel you to react. Recognizing these interac tions between mind and body is key to understanding and reducing emo tional reactivity. Body-scanning takes us deeper into the experience of body sensations and their intimate link to emotions. Let s look more closely at why awareness of body sensations with equanimity is so essential to emotional regulation. What Are Emotions Made of? At first glance, emotions might appear to be indivisible, complex experi ences a fusion of thoughts, physical sensations, and reactions. However, emotions are not things we think or do; they are things we feel. They seem unified because their components evaluation, co-emerging body sensa tions, and reactions are processed in less than a second, making them appear inseparable! Because these three mental components are processed so rapidly, they can appear to occur as a single experience. For example, when our mind evaluates a situation as being disagreeable, unpleasant body sensations co-emerge almost immediately, often leading to a reaction. This rapid sequence creates the illusion that the emotion resides entirely in the reaction. When we examine emotions more closely, we see they originate as body sensations, often before we are consciously aware of them. Most emotions begin at a preconscious level of information processing and remain subcon scious if they are subtle. As their intensity increases, they may enter con scious awareness as a felt sense in the body. At moderate intensity, we can 108 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE notice their presence without feeling compelled to react. However, if the emotion becomes intense, the most obvious aspect of it might be our reac tive response! For example, when asked how they knew they were angry during an interaction, people often describe their actions, such as I slammed the door, I stormed out, or I shouted. This suggests that when sensations remain subconscious, emotions are first identified through reactive behav ior. This is why we are more likely to say I am angry, rather than I feel angry, which reinforces identification with the emotion. Through body scanning, we can observe these components more clearly and begin to step back from identifying with emotions as my anger or my anxiety. Instead, we start to see emotions as temporary events that arise and pass within the mind and body. Neuroscientific research supports this understanding, showing that body sensations are central to emotions [59]. Specific emotions, such as anger, sadness, or joy, manifest as unique and identifiable patterns of body sensa tions. A study by Nummenmaa and colleagues demonstrates that specific emotions anger, sadness, joy manifest as unique maps of sensations in the body [60]. More recent research has expanded on this, showing variabil ity in how individuals experience sensations associated with emotions, influ enced by factors such as personal experiences, cultural background, and situational context [61]. These findings align with 2,500years of consistent observations from mindfulness meditation [4]. The Four Characteristics of Body Sensations Through body-scanning, we develop a nuanced awareness of body sensa tions and the four primary characteristics they embody: mass, motion, tem perature, and density. Recognizing these characteristics allows us to understand how different emotions manifest physically, helping us relate to them with increased objectivity and reduced reactivity. To appreciate the simplicity and universality of these characteristics, con sider analogous systems in other sensory domains. Just as we only need four basic colors red, yellow, blue, and grayscale to produce an infinite spec trum of hues, or five basic tastes sweet, sour, salty, bitter, and umami create the vast array of flavors in cuisines worldwide, we only need these four interoceptive characteristics to generate an infinite combination of bodily sensations and emotions. By focusing on these elements, we gain a clearer understanding of our internal experiences, which, though complex, are built upon simple, universal sensory patterns. Let s explore each charac teristic in more detail: 1 Mass: Sensations can range from light to heavy. For example, sadness is often associated with heaviness, creating a feeling of weight in the chest, head, or limbs. Conversely, joy or happiness might manifest as lightness or buoyancy. WEEK 3: UNDERSTANDING AND REGULATING EMOTIONS 109 2 Motion: Sensations can feel still or dynamic. Anxiety often brings a sense of internal movement, such as fluttering or butterflies in the stomach, or restlessness in the body. In contrast, calmness or content ment is frequently accompanied by a sense of stillness or stability. 3 Temperature: Sensations can vary in warmth or coolness. Anger is fre quently experienced as heat, often as a flush in the face or warmth throughout the body. Fear might be experienced as cold chills. Noticing these temperature shifts can provide early clues about emotional states. 4 Density: Sensations can range from tight and constricted to loose and expansive. Stress or fear often feels like tightness in the chest, throat, or abdomen. Love and joy, on the other hand, may manifest as openness or spaciousness in the heart area. With pleasure, sadness, and anger, this characteristic can also manifest as a flow-like wave of sensations. These four characteristics are not exclusive to emotional experiences; they are present in any co-emerging sensation, whether or not an emotion is involved. For instance, when you think about an upcoming event, you might notice a combination of lightness (mass), excitement (motion), warmth (temperature), and openness (density), even if no strong emotion is attached. This universality highlights the foundational role these four characteristics play in our moment-to-moment experience. Moreover, body sensations are neither purely physical nor purely mental. In the Vipassana meditation tradition from which the body-scanning prac tices in this program are derived, sensations are considered to be a link between the mind and the body [19]. This perspective underscores the deep interconnection between our physical sensations and mental states, with each continuously influencing the other. Figure 3.6 illustrates these four characteristics and places them along measurable dimensions. This simple method of recording how we feel marking each characteristic on a line provides a practical way to track our sensory experiences and emotional states. These four characteristics provide a valuable framework for recognizing how emotions manifest in the body. While each emotion has its own unique signature in the body, the way we experience them remains deeply per sonal. Here are four frequently reported descriptions: Anger may present as increased motion (agitation or rapid heart rate), elevated temperature (heat in the face or chest), and increased density (tightness in the jaw or fists). Sadness might manifest as increased mass (heaviness in the head or heart, slumped shoulders) and increased density (a constricted sensa tion, like a lump in the throat). Fear often involves decreased mass (increased lightness or lightheaded ness), increased motion (a racing heartbeat), decreased temperature (cold sweats), and increased density (tightness in the chest or abdomen). 110 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE Joy can manifest as increased lightness (in the chest area), increased warmth (in the chest area), and decreased density (a buoyant feeling throughout the body). When perceived in this objective way, as sensations in the body with a predominance of one or more of the four characteristics, even intense or unpleasant emotions can become easier to work with. We might think we cannot handle intense fear or deep sadness, but we rarely feel the same about dealing with sensations of heaviness, tightness, or warmth. These physical sensations are less personal, allowing us to engage with our emotions more skillfully, even with painful memories that sometimes emerge during meditation. In summary, mass, motion, temperature, and density serve as the building blocks of both our physical and emotional experiences. By paying attention to these characteristics, we gain valuable insights into our internal states, empowering us to navigate emotions with greater skill and ease. As It Is Within, So It Is Without These four characteristics of body sensations also reflect the universal char acteristics of the physical world. They exist not only within us but also outside our bodies. In Buddhist psychology [62], these characteristics are considered the smallest and indivisible units of matter [63], as perceived by the mind. They are akin to subatomic particles, the energy at the core of every particle of matter [64]. In many Eastern and Western ancient tradi tions, they were known as the four elements of matter, also known as earth, air, fire, and water [65]. Densest Most movement Hottest Heaviest Loosest Still Coldest Lightest MOTION MASS TEMPERATURE DENSITY Neutral Neutral Neutral Neutral Figure 3.6 The four basic characteristics of interoception. Cayoun (2011)/John Wiley & Sons. WEEK 3: UNDERSTANDING AND REGULATING EMOTIONS 111 This conceptualization originates from the Buddha s well-known teaching in the R hulov da Sutta, initially addressed to his son, Rahula. In this teach ing, he explained that the entire universe is only composed of six elements four of which constitute matter and that these elements are constantly changing. He emphasized the importance of refraining from identifying with or becoming attached to what is so ephemeral. The element of earth (sensations of mass) corresponds to the weight of mat ter, from the smallest particle to the entire cosmos, including the heaviness we feel in our bodies. The element of air (sensations of motion) relates to the movement of gases, both within our bodies and in the atmosphere, such as the gentle flow of breath or the movement of wind. The element of fire (sensations of temperature) refers to the temperature of the body and the temperature of our external environment, such as the climate. The element of water (sensa tions of density or fluidity) aligns with the flow of bodily fluids, the rivers and oceans of the world, and the interplay of solidity and liquidity in matter. Beyond these physical elements, Buddhist psychology recognizes two other elements related to nonphysical dimensions of experience. One is the element of consciousness, representing our purest awareness of phenomena, and is precisely what we are further developing through mindfulness train ing. The other is the element of space, which is neither mental nor physical, and refers to the sense of spaciousness or emptiness both within and out side the body. Observing the four elements that compose the emotional experience objectively helps us take the ups and downs of daily life less personally. Viewing bodily sensations and thoughts as manifestations of basic elemental processes reduces our experience of them as or mine, and modifies the habitual self-referential evaluation that drives reactivity. With this per spective, when we observe the elements arising and passing within the body, we can approach them with genuine openness and equanimity, welcoming them as part of the natural, impermanent flow of existence. Equanimity: Your Power Within Definition of Equanimity The term equanimity comes from the French quanimit , which originates from the Latin aequanimitas, combining aequus ( equal ) and animus mind ). While equanimity first appeared in English literature in the early 17th century, the concept of maintaining mental calmness and composure in difficult situations has been present in European thought for much longer. Philosophers such as the Stoics in ancient Greece and Rome discussed similar ideas, emphasizing the importance of inner balance and resilience. The 1913 edition of Webster s Dictionary defined equanimity as, That calm temper or firmness of mind which is not easily elated or depressed [66]. 112 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE Interestingly, the concept of equanimity was also a central theme in Eastern traditions. Over 2,500years ago, it was referred to as upekkha in Pali. Despite the significant chronological and geographical differences, upekkha carries a meaning similar to the English equanimity but with a more specific technical definition. For the Buddha, equanimity referred to the balance and neutrality of the mind that prevents identification with internal experiences. In an equanimous state, the mind remains nonreactive, even in the face of intense sensory experiences, avoiding craving for pleasant sensations or aversion to unpleasant ones. In MiCBT, equanimity plays a foundational role in how we relate to our inner experience. When working with clients, Andrea describes it as bring ing an equal interest to pleasant, unpleasant, and neutral sensations without reactivity and without identifying with them [67]. At its core, this means cultivating a willingness to fully feel whatever arises whether it s a moment of physical discomfort, emotional pain, or fleeting joy while maintaining a sense of openness to the internal experience as it unfolds. Equanimity enables you to observe sensations as they arise and pass, without proliferating them through craving or aversion. As we discussed above, research into interoception shows that bodily sensations are deeply tied to our sense of self [16, 68]. By being equanimous, you learn to step back from identifying with these sensations, weakening the automatic link between bodily experiences and self-referential thoughts [69]. This shift allows you to approach your inner world with greater psychological flexi bility. For example, long-held beliefs like I am unworthy or I must be perfect may feel less compelling, as you begin observing them with equa nimity and curiosity, reducing their influence over your emotions and decisions. This practice transforms your relationship with sensations. Rather than suppressing or avoiding them, equanimity allows you to feel sensations as they are, interrupting cycles of craving and aversion that fuel emotional distress. For instance, when you notice tightness in your chest during anxi ety, equanimity enables you to observe it non-reactively. Over time, this cre ates space to experience emotions without being overwhelmed, fostering a deeper sense of calm and clarity. Ultimately, equanimity frees you from the habit of attaching personal mean ing to sensations and thoughts. This freedom paves the way for significant changes in how you think, feel, and respond. With practice, equanimity can help you dissolve deeply ingrained patterns and develop a more adaptive, harmonious way of being both with yourself and the world around you. Small Effort for Big Results Equanimity is a true power within. When well understood and applied effec tively, any fear can be desensitized, any learned response can be unlearned, and any personality trait can be transformed. This is because equanimity targets the very core of our reactive habits [2]. WEEK 3: UNDERSTANDING AND REGULATING EMOTIONS 113 Recently, a middle-aged man had been referred to one of us by his doctor in the context of a depressive episode. He felt deep sadness and shame because he believed he had failed as a father and husband despite what he perceived as genuine efforts over the past 20years. He grew up on a South Pacific island with a deeply entrenched patriarchal culture, where family hierarchy was strictly defined. The elders held the highest authority, and their decisions were not to be disputed. Next in line was the father, who wielded significant control over the household, while children were expected to follow direction without question. Challenging their father s authority was seen as a serious offense, often met with physical punishment. Raised in this cultural environment, he struggled to adapt to parenting in Australia and continued to parent the same way he himself had experienced growing up. As his son became a teenager, the young man rebelled against his authority. Despite his intention to be a good father, he would react imme diately with physical force or intimidation, and this same scenario kept recurring. Under the pressure of his wife, he ended up leaving the family home and lost hope of finding a solution. During a therapy session, he explained that he couldn t help being so rigid and too tough with the children, as he believed his son had to have respect for his elders. He had a strong motiva tion to change and had made many attempts to do so, but kept on falling back into the same reactive pattern. In the context of this belief, he couldn stop himself from reacting with anger. It was so rapid, so impulsive. What was missing was a skill he had never learned: equanimity. Through the cultivation of equanimity, he began to understand that his suffering was deeply tied to his attachment to his sense of self as an author ity figure his identity as the father whose role was to enforce respect. Over just a few weeks, he learned how to apply equanimity toward his internal experience. His wife regained some hope and allowed limited inter actions with his children again. By becoming aware with equanimity of the unpleasant sensations that arose when he felt his son was being disrespect ful, he was able to refrain from automatically reacting to them, and in this way was able to break the cycle of conflict. As he developed greater composure and patience, the family dynamic began to shift. As his reactions softened, his children s responses changed too, leading to a more harmonious environment. Equanimity not only helped him stop the reactive anger that had previously defined his parenting, but it also allowed him to reintegrate into his family with compassion and understanding. This personal transformation illustrates the profound power of equanim ity. It doesn t require a grand effort; rather, it develops through consistent practice to achieve profound and lasting results. Neuroscientific studies echo this, showing that equanimity plays a central role in emotional regula tion during meditation [69]. Research has demonstrated that people who meditate while bringing equanimity to moment-to-moment experience 114 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE handle pain more effectively than those who simply focus their attention without equanimity [70, 71]. Their ability to remain present with difficult sensations without reacting allows them to regulate their pain experience more effectively. Just as the father learned, mindfulness combined with equanimity can transform not only our internal experience but also our relationships, leading to lasting, meaningful change. Research evidence sug gests that this meditative approach is one of the most beneficial approaches in the long term [72]. Equanimity: What It Is and What It Isn Equanimity is sometimes misunderstood. A common misconception is equating equanimity with indifference, detachment, or aloofness. However, being equanimous doesn t mean becoming indifferent to life or emotionally disengaged. In Buddhist psychology, indifference is referred to as a near enemy of equanimity it may appear similar on the surface but is funda mentally different. Indifference involves a withdrawal of care and concern, whereas equanimity allows us to remain deeply engaged with life, others, and our communities while responding with discernment and compassion. These misunderstandings can occasionally cause challenges for those beginning to practice mindfulness meditation. For example, a client, Elena, shared her frustration after her partner returned from a meditation retreat. Since he came back, she said, s been lazy. He plays his guitar all the time, and when I ask for help with dinner, he tells me, Ohhh, you re react ing! What s wrong with him? Elena s partner had misunderstood equanimity. In reality, he was still caught in craving and aversion. He craved playing his guitar for the pleasant sensations it evoked and avoided helping with dinner to escape the discom fort of related thoughts and sensations. True equanimity would have allowed him to acknowledge and accept the initial discomfort associated with help ing, without resisting or identifying with it. With further practice, he came to understand that equanimity involved the acceptance of the initial unpleasant body sensations that arose as a result of his evaluation of helping with dinner, without identifying with these sen sations. Over time, he found he could delay the enjoyment of pleasant sensa tions associated with playing his guitar without resentment. This led to cooking with joy, enjoying the warm water on his hands, experiencing grati tude for his partner s care, and feeling more loving toward her. Both bene fited from his understanding and effort. Another misunderstanding is the belief that equanimity dulls emotions or prevents us from feeling deeply. In fact, equanimity enhances our capacity to fully experience emotions, as it involves staying open to feeling the bodily sensations accompanying them. By not avoiding or shutting down difficult emotions like grief, we also cultivate the ability to feel positive emotions like joy more fully. Equanimity does not diminish our emotional range it expands it by fostering openness to all experiences, pleasant and unpleasant. WEEK 3: UNDERSTANDING AND REGULATING EMOTIONS 115 A related misunderstanding is the belief that equanimity means not enjoy ing pleasant experiences, or not disliking unpleasant ones. This is where it important to discern between enjoyment and attachment, and between dis liking and aversion. Equanimity doesn t mean we should avoid pleasure or suppress discomfort; instead, it means we can experience pleasant and unpleasant sensations without clinging to or pushing them away. For exam ple, enjoying a piece of chocolate with equanimity means savoring it with out feeling compelled to have another or fearing its end. Equanimity, in this case, means allowing the pleasant sensations to arise and fade naturally, without attachment, enjoying them while they re here and, after they pass, being free from the compulsion to fill their absence with something else. By practicing this balance, we free ourselves from the habitual patterns of craving and aversion that create suffering. We can enjoy a pleasant moment without becoming attached to its continuation, and we can experience dis comfort without becoming resentful of its presence. Applying Equanimity in Body-Scanning to Regulate Emotions As you begin your body-scanning practice, equanimity becomes your guide in cultivating a balanced, nonreactive mind. This practice is not only about noticing sensations but also about relating to them with equanimity allowing each sensation to arise, be experienced, and fade away without reacting. Whether you encounter an itch, discomfort, or a pleasant sensa tion, your task is to observe it with curiosity and openness, without attach ing a story to it or reacting impulsively. These sensations are temporary and need not define you. With consistent practice, you ll develop the mental strength to stay fully present with all experiences pleasant, unpleasant, or neutral building a foundation for lasting emotional well-being. Integrating Skills in Daily Life Cultivating Interoceptive Awareness This week, part of your practice involves cultivating interoceptive awareness your ability to notice and feel body sensations throughout your day. By doing so, you ll be training your brain to strengthen neural connections in areas such as the insular and somatosensory cortices. This will enhance your ability to feel body sensations during meditation and, importantly, in moments that typically trigger emotional reactions in daily life. Your effort to remain aware needs to be accompanied by equanimity, so that your awareness remains free from the habit of reacting to body sensations. The practice itself is straightforward but requires commitment and effort to integrate into your routine. The goal is to keep your attention anchored in the sensations of the body as consistently as possible, in any situation and 116 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE during any activity. This begins the moment you wake up and continues until you fall asleep. For instance, you might notice the feeling of your feet on the floor as you walk, the weight of your hands resting on the keyboard as you type, or the rise and fall of your chest as you breathe. At bedtime, you can even train yourself to fall asleep while gently main taining awareness of body sensations but without scanning your body, as this could keep you awake. Simply let sensations be as they are, noticing them without attaching judgments or stories. They are just sensations that arise and pass away, part of the natural flow of experience. There is also great benefit in noticing the continual interaction between mind and body during the day. Be curious about how certain thoughts co-emerge with body sensations and how noticeable sensations co-emerge with thoughts. Notice how your neck or shoulders might tense during or after a difficult con versation, how your jaw clenches when you think about something frustrating, or how your breathing becomes shallow when you worry. With practice, this ongoing awareness accompanied by equanimity will become a foundation for remaining grounded and calm, no matter what arises in your day. Understanding the Experience of Co-Emergence This week, you can begin to directly experience the important interaction between mind and body how one causes the other to arise in consciousness which is usually a relatively subconscious phenomenon. This awareness pro vides direct evidence of a crucial insight: we don t react to triggers themselves. Instead, we react to the body sensations that co-emerge with how we evalu ate those triggers. Understanding this process explains why MiCBT focuses on learning how not to react to body sensations, using body-scanning as the training method of choice. To gain firsthand insight into the co-emergence process as it happens in your daily life, use the Diary of Reactive Habits shown on Figure 3.4 to record stressful situations you encounter this week. Fill it in as soon as pos sible after as many situations as possible to capture the details of your inner experience. This journaling exercise helps you integrate your understanding of co-emergence into daily life. You can download unlimited copies of the Diary of Reactive Habits from the book s resource page: http://www.micbt forwellbeing.com/forms. This Week s Practice Unilateral Body-Scanning The specific body-scanning method used in this program is designed to train equanimity, rather than just relaxation or concentration, and will guide you through a process distinct from other approaches [73]. Please avoid mixing techniques by using audio recordings of other body-scanning practices, as this can create confusion or delay the development of core skills. While WEEK 3: UNDERSTANDING AND REGULATING EMOTIONS 117 other types of body-scanning have their benefits, we recommend only using the audio instructions specific to the MiCBT program. Applying Your Skills: Cultivating Interoceptive Awareness Do your best to maintain an awareness of body sensations throughout the day, at least the most obvious ones. For example, when walking, be aware of the soles of your feet pressing against the ground. When sitting, be aware of the pressure you feel on your buttocks and under your thighs. When eating, be aware of the sensation of food in your mouth, the sensations created in your throat and esophagus by swallowing, and when your stomach feels full. And importantly, when emotions arise, be aware of the corresponding body sensations, taking this opportunity to cultivate equanimity. For instance, when feeling uncertain about something, you might notice a subtle heaviness settling in your chest or a restless fluttering sensation just below your ribcage. When feeling pleased about something, you might notice a lightness or expansion in your chest area. A helpful habit, now and in the future, is to be aware of body sensations soon after you wake up, before getting out of bed in the morning, and soon after you hop back into bed at night, before falling asleep. These bookend moments help establish a continuous thread of awareness that naturally extends into your daily activities. Frequently Asked Questions intermediate when i practice, i have flashbacks of something bad that hap pened, and i become anxious. it put me 1 1.2 book Why is it important to move my attention during body-scanning in this particular order? There are three reasons for this. The first is that scanning in the same order each time ensures you don t forget any areas of the body, espe cially as the mind can be so easily distracted. This is not a big issue this week because you are using the audio instructions, but it can become an issue next week, when you ll be practicing in silence. The second reason for scanning in this order is that our attention natu rally gravitates toward areas with obvious sensations, making it easy to neglect parts of the body that are difficult to feel. This can happen without us being consciously aware of it! If we neglect these subtle sensations, we miss out on opportunities to feel the very early signs of change in the body, such as the early signs of emotions. So, by scanning your body in the same order each time, you give equal attention to all areas, ensuring you don miss any, including those that don t have many sensations or that feel blank. This practice strengthens neural connections in the insula and somatosen sory cortex, which will help you feel more of your body over time. 120 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE The third reason for scanning in this particular order is to lay the ground work for the more advanced methods that we will soon learn. Unilateral scanning is the most basic body-scanning method and one that we will only use for 2 weeks before starting more advanced scanning techniques. In future weeks, you ll be learning techniques like sweeping, where you move your attention fluidly through the body in a single, continuous motion, eventually at the speed of your breath. This requires a vertical approach to scanning. While it s fine to move horizontally in small areas, such as the face, prioritizing vertical scanning now will make it easier to progress later. intermediate why is it important to move my attention during body-scanning in this particular order? 1 1.3 book How fast should I go to scan the whole body? This week, the audio instructions will guide you through one full body cycle during your 30-minute session. A body cycle means scan ning from the top of your head to the tips of your toes, and then back up again. The slower pace this week helps you become familiar with the prac tice and builds a foundation for mindfulness of body sensations. Next week, you ll begin scanning at your own pace, completing at least two body cycles in each 30-minute session. intermediate how fast should i go to scan the whole body? 1 1.3 book I can t feel body sensations. What am I looking for? It s entirely normal, especially when starting body-scanning, to experience some parts of the body as blank, with no noticeable sensa tions. As mentioned earlier in the chapter, this will gradually change over time as the brain wires to feel sensations in these areas. At times, though, it can seem like you can t feel any sensations. This can happen when we expect body sensations to be special or distinct in some way. They are not! During body-scanning, we are simply observing ordi nary, everyday sensations as they arise. Anything you feel counts as a body sensation. This might be sensations of temperature, such as feeling warmth or coolness in some part of your body. It could be sensations associated with movement, such as the movement of your chest wall or abdomen when breathing. It could be sensations of heaviness or lightness, such as feeling heaviness in the neck and other places due to fatigue. Sensations could also be related to the experience of density, such as feeling a sense of expansion or looseness when we are relaxed or joyful. We practice to observe our experiences in the body just as they are. If you t feel a particular area of your body, simply accept that you can t feel sensations in this particular part at this moment. Keep attention on that area for up to 10seconds. Bring an equal interest to each sensation, whether pleasant, unpleasant, or neutral, without taking it personally. As your ability to sustain attention and not get lost in thoughts improves week to week, ll be able to stay focused on blank spots more easily for up to 30seconds before moving on. WEEK 3: UNDERSTANDING AND REGULATING EMOTIONS 121 Remember: sensation or not, keep smiling and move on. This attitude of patience and acceptance cultivated during meditation carries over into daily life. When you don t get what you want or encounter challenges, you ll find yourself more tolerant, less agitated, and better equipped to respond skill fully. After all, the brain you use for meditation is the brain you also use in daily life. How you shape your brain during practice directly influences how it responds to everyday situations. intermediate i can t feel body sensations. what am i looking for? 1 1.3 book I ve had strong feelings of sadness, frustration, and anxiety dur ing my last few practices. Scanning through them in the body helped to resolve them a little, but they creep back. There was a recent incident involv ing my eight-year-old son that upset me, and even though the issue is now resolved, I still feel overly emotional about it. It sounds like you are genuinely trying to remain objective and equanimous. This is a great opportunity to further develop your equanim ity. First, it s important to acknowledge that your emotional reaction is entirely normal most people would react strongly if something were to happen to their child! Recognizing this can help you accept your experi ence with greater self-compassion. At the same time, your lingering dis tress suggests that while the external situation is resolved, thinking about it is still creating distress internally. Unresolved emotions consist of related thoughts or memories and their co-emerging body sensations. In your case, the sensations are co-emerging with memories of the incident involving your son, not the incident itself. Keeping this distinction in mind can help you bring genuine equanimity to the sensations. Your growing ability to feel body sensations means that you re becoming more aware of subtle sensations in daily life that you might not usually notice. This is similar to using a magnifying glass with enough magnifica tion, even the pores on your skin can look like large holes! The more you refine your awareness of subtle sensations, the more those that were only mildly uncomfortable seem more intense. You need to ensure that you create a good balance between interoceptive awareness (which serves as your mag nifying glass) and equanimity i.e., a balance between feeling and accepting body sensations. This will not only soothe your emotional experience, but it will also increase your ability to feel even subtler sensations during practice. If you re finding it challenging to sustain equanimity, try pausing your body-scanning practice briefly. Instead, focus on the area where sensations feel most intense. Rather than resisting or wishing the sensations away, examine the area of intensity carefully. Ask yourself: Do the sensations feel heavy or light? Do they move or are they still? What temperature do they have, if any? Are they dense or more diffuse? Keep attention on the area for 122 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE 60seconds and notice its changing nature, while accepting it fully. Doing this is likely to assist you in your effort to manage your sadness. intermediate i ve had strong feelings of sadness, frustration, and anxiety dur ing my last few practices. scannin 1 1.3 book The scanning is going well, but I still can t feel some parts of my chest and stomach, and I get frustrated. Am I doing something wrong? Not at all. It s perfectly normal not to feel sensations in every part of the body at this stage. There are two main reasons for this. First, you may have noticed that it s much easier to feel sensations in the face, hands, and feet than in the torso. This is because there are more brain cells dedicated to feeling the face, hands, and feet than there are for the chest and abdomen. Second, it s natural to dislike unpleasant sensations in the abdomen or chest, such as those that occur with anxiety or chronic stress. Over time, avoiding these sensations can turn down the volume of all sensations in those areas, making them harder to detect [74]. For this reason, it can take a little longer to feel sensations in the front part of the torso compared to other parts of the body. Remember, not feeling sensations in some parts of the body is also impermanent; it will change with consistent practice. When you encounter blank area, try to maintain your attention calmly and equanimously on the blank area, as learning to be patient when we can t feel sensations is also part of the practice. However, if you practice twice daily and find that larger areas remain blank for several days, then focus within those blank spots by expanding your attention to cover a wider area at a time, about 3 4 in. in diameter, for around 30 seconds. Increasing the size of the area you are paying attention to will increase the chances of feeling a sen sation. Move on when you feel something or after approximately six to eight breaths. intermediate the scanning is going well, but i still can t feel some parts of my chest and stomach, and i get fru 1 1.3 book I feel agitated when I practice. Is that normal? Yes, experiencing agitation or restlessness is common when we first start the practice. It s nothing to be concerned about. In fact, this may be a valuable opportunity to carefully observe where sensations associated with agitation are experienced. Hold your attention there while remaining calm and equanimous for a minute or two. Investigate, dissect, and study what you witness with curiosity and genuine interest. Are they warm or cool? Still or moving? These sensations are impermanent, and just like everything else in life, they are not here to stay. s also important to remind yourself that the experience of agitation is just that: an experience. It doesn t define you, and it s not something you need to call or mine. Remember this each time you start to become agitated about the agitation! If you observe the experience in an equani mous way without identifying with it, it won t take long before the agitation transforms into something else. WEEK 3: UNDERSTANDING AND REGULATING EMOTIONS 123 If the agitation is caused by persistent thoughts, instead of focusing on the thoughts or their co-emerging sensations, focus on the breath and nothing else, keeping your mind as stable as possible despite the underlying agita tion. Once your mind has settled, return to scanning the body. You might find it helpful to remember the acronym FAME: Feel, Accept, and Move with Equanimity, which was created by one of our clients. This can serve as a simple reminder of how to approach sensations and the atti tude to bring to them during your practice. intermediate i feel agitated when i practice. is that normal? 1 1.3 book I ve noticed that my eyes move to the area of the body I focusing on. For example, when I try to feel the top of my head, my eyes look up. When I scan my left shoulder, my eyes look toward that shoulder. Basically, I scan with my eyes, too! It s very tiring, and I get a small head ache. Is it ok for my eyes to be moving like this? This is a common experience when starting body-scanning. Many of us instinctively use our visual modality to support the move ment of attention by visualizing the body parts we re focusing on. This can lead to unintentional eye movements, as the brain is more accustomed to relying on vision than on interoception (the ability to sense internal body sensations). When we can t easily feel certain areas of the body, the brain tries to them instead. Doing this for 30minutes can easily lead to a headache. Bruno recalls experiencing the same thing when he began practicing over 35years ago. The sooner you stop tracking your sensations with your eyes, the more com fortable and effective your practice will become. As your ability to feel sensations develops, the habit of trying to also them will naturally decrease. For now, simply notice when your eyes begin to move and gently rest them in one position. Focus on scanning the body without engaging your eyes. If this feels challenging, try opening your eyes slightly and resting your gaze on a fixed point on the floor a short distance in front of you while continuing to scan the body. intermediate i ve noticed that my eyes move to the area of the body i focusing on. for example, when i try to fee 1 1.3 book When I do my mindfulness practice, I get very agitated and remorseful. Your feelings of remorse are understandable, and this experi ence is common when difficult emotions from the past resurface. It important to recognize that these feelings are part of the process and may even offer insight into what truly matters to you. However, the key is to avoid falling into self-blame. Using the practice to dwell on blaming 124 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE yourself only agitates the mind further and reduces your ability to accept the past as it was. Last week, during mindfulness of breath practice, you began learning to notice thoughts as they pop up in your mind without perceiving them as parts of who you are, without identifying with them. This marked a shift from seeing your thoughts as part of to recognizing them as transient mental events. The same principle applies to body-scanning. At this stage, you may still find it challenging not to identify with the sensations that arise, as they may feel deeply personal, like parts of yourself. However, as you continue prac ticing, you will develop the ability to observe these bits of the self arise and pass without clinging to them, whether they feel acceptable in the moment or not. This will help you notice these thoughts and feel the accom panying sensations while interrupting rumination and the suffering it cre ates. In turn, this will calm your mind. s also helpful to recognize that strong sensations of guilt can obscure other important aspects of your experience. For example, your deep concern for your children shows how much compassion you have for them. You stayed in a difficult marriage for years because you were afraid of not being able to provide for them on your own. Take a moment to reflect: Could it be that they have always felt the depth of your love for them? How does it feel to remind yourself of these aspects rather than focus on remorse and self-blame? If focusing on these positive aspects feels a bit too challenging, it may be because the co-emerging sensations are too intense to approach with equa nimity. In this case, shift your attention to the breath and practice mindful ness of breath for a short while, or for the entire session, if helpful. Remember, your efforts to reduce anxiety not only benefit you but also positively impact your children, who will notice and feel the effects of your progress. While the past cannot be changed, each moment of practice is a step toward shap ing a better future, for yourself and those you care about. intermediate when i do my mindfulness practice, i get very agitated and remorseful. i can t settle my body, and m 1 1.3 book It s very strange, when I started this practice last week, I began to feel some nausea during body-scanning. Not too much, but enough to feel uncomfortable. Is this normal? Is it possible that mindfulness could be harmful for some people? No, being mindful is not harmful to people, but nausea does happen sometimes, and it passes. It can also happen during progressive muscle relaxation and mindfulness of breath, often as we become more aware of our emotions or worry about them. Remember, nausea doesn last forever, and, like other forms of discomfort, it is a good tool for devel oping equanimity. In fact, you couldn t develop equanimity without dis comfort. Just as we can only learn patience when we are impatient, we can only learn equanimity when we are reactive. WEEK 3: UNDERSTANDING AND REGULATING EMOTIONS 125 s also useful to remember that nausea can arise in the context of a variety of conditions, both mental and physical, including anxiety, depres sion, post-traumatic stress disorder, eating disorders, migraines, infection, food poisoning, or medication side effects, which sometimes occur when medication is taken on an empty stomach. Even dietary changes, such as eating too quickly or consuming spicy or fatty foods, can trigger nausea. If nausea persists outside of meditation and doesn t seem directly related to it, it may be a good idea to consult your doctor to rule out any underlying health issues. s important to understand that the practice of sitting calmly and scan ning the body does not inherently cause nausea. Instead, it may increase your awareness of subtle sensations linked to residual anxiety, which can manifest as nausea. Alternatively, a past memory of nausea might be re triggered during practice. When nausea arises, do your best to remain equanimous, reminding yourself that the discomfort is temporary and not dangerous in and of itself. If the sensations are too overwhelming during meditation, even after focusing equanimously on the four characteristics mass, motion, temperature, and density in the area where it is most intense for about half a minute, then focus calmly on the breath, continuing with mindfulness of breath for the rest of that session. If you find that the nausea increases or other intense experiences arise in the following few days, stop practicing body scanning and replace it with PMR for a few sessions. If you find that nausea has subsided and there is no need for a medical checkup, resume body scanning. intermediate it s very strange, when i started this practice last week, i began to feel some nausea during body-s 1 1.3 book I ve been having headaches during the body-scanning, usually after about 15 20minutes every time I practice. I didn t experience this dur ing PMR or Mindfulness of Breath. I would appreciate your thoughts on what might be causing them. There could be several factors contributing to the headaches. s common for people to put a lot of effort into trying to feel sensations, which can lead to tension headaches. If you find yourself in this situation, take a step back, abandon the strong desire to feel something specific, and simply notice what sensations are arising naturally, if any. s also worth considering whether the headaches come more frequently when you are scanning certain parts of the body. At times, some body sensa tions during our practice can feel similar to those we feel during stressful situations, such as past or present conflicts, painful memories, relationship or work dissatisfaction, and other issues we may not feel prepared to face at this moment, perhaps because of fears that they will be opening a can of worms, so to speak. The emotion we may not yet be ready to feel can still be subconscious, and we therefore can t yet make sense of it. It s natural to automatically, often subconsciously, attempt to suppress these emotions, as they often feel uncomfortable. Has there been any old issue intensifying or 126 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE any new issue arising in your life? Avoiding these sensations during medita tion can create much tension, which can result in headaches. Because this process often occurs at a largely subconscious level, do your best to remind yourself that the headache is simply an unpleasant sensation that will pass in time. Resisting or wishing it away can inadvertently increase its intensity, as tension and worry tend to amplify discomfort. The more relaxed and equanimous you remain during practice, the more likely the headache will dissipate naturally and with greater ease. intermediate i ve been having headaches during the body-scanning, usually after about 15 20minutes every time i p 1 1.3 book I cannot feel ordinary sensations clearly unless I visualize each area of my body. Is there something wrong with my brain? There is nothing wrong with your brain. Where you can t feel a sensation, simply acknowledge this without judgment this acceptance helps maintain a calm and focused mind. Stay with each blank spot for up to 30 seconds, and then move on to the next part of your body, whether or not a sensation becomes noticeable. This approach reduces the ten dency to visualize body parts. If, after three days, you still feel that there is little to no progress, consider switching to Mindfulness of Breath for the remainder of the week. This will help strengthen your ability to regulate attention, making it easier to sustain focus on areas with subtle or faint sensations when you return to body scanning the following week. Finally, let go of any judgments about your ability or performance. Rather than expecting to feel sensations because the audio instructions suggest it, simply notice whether you can feel sensations in a part of the body or not. Your worth is not defined by your ability to perceive body sensations. Skillful practice is characterized by making an effort to feel sensations while approaching your experience without reacting to it or identifying with it in other words, practicing with equanimity.
