Ajahn Cha Practice Background and Rationale As you learn to be more mindful and accepting of body sensations during body-scanning, this chapter will support your understanding and regulation of emotional experiences. You will be able to perceive the deeper nature of body sensations and their central role in emotional experiences. Building on this foundation, this chapter will guide you in minimizing emotional reactiv ity and cultivating curiosity and interest in applying these skills during and outside your meditation practice. Integrating the Wisdom of Impermanence Through Body-Scanning As you continue practicing body-scanning, you may begin to notice something that Heraclitus described around 500 BC: Everything gives way and nothing stays fixed [1]. Similarly, during the same period, the Buddha taught that all things are impermanent, emphasizing that clinging to phe nomena that inevitably arise and pass leads to disappointment and disillu sionment. While you may have encountered these ideas before, perhaps in books or conversations, body-scanning offers a unique opportunity to Week 4: Applying Mindfulness Skills in Daily Life 134 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE experience this impermanence firsthand. Through this practice, you can observe the four characteristics of sensations discussed last week continually arising and passing away. This insight applies to every internal experience we encounter as we scan the body, and we invite you to put this to the test in your own practice! While an intellectual understanding of impermanence can be interesting or even inspiring, it s unlikely to impact your quality of life and well-being meaningfully unless it is experienced directly and repeatedly. Remember the three ways of learning introduced in Week 1: on their own, devotional learn ing and intellectual learning rarely bring about the change we seek. This is why we focus so heavily on first-hand experience. This week, you ll continue exploring the impermanence of body sensa tions, but in silence and with strong determination to cultivate equanimity. As you become more attuned to these sensations, you ll notice that all of them, as well as your need to react to them, are impermanent. Observing this with an equanimous mind helps loosen attachment to fleeting experi ences and reduces your reactivity, bringing greater ease into daily life. Working Skillfully with Craving s examine some of the ways the three forms of craving can manifest dur ing body-scanning. Recognizing them when they arise helps us work with them more effectively when they occur. Craving to Obtain Occasionally, even at this early stage of body-scanning, some people experi ence very pleasant sensations in the body. Since equanimity is still develop ing, the natural response is to crave or cling to these experiences. If pleasant sensations were predictable and permanent, this wouldn t be a problem. Unfortunately, you never know what kind of sensations you will feel in the next minute or during your next practice session. For example, in your next morning practice, you might feel light and pleasant sensations in your arms and legs. However, in your evening prac tice, you may encounter heavy and unpleasant sensations in your neck and shoulders or even pain in your lower back. These, on their own, are not very pleasant to experience, but if you were expecting the morning s pleasant sensations instead, your disappointment would be much worse! This natural variation in sensation can lead to a craving for these pleasant sensations or an aversion to the unpleasant ones. Isn t this something we often experience in daily life, too? Sometimes, craving manifests itself more directly during practice. For instance, you might fantasize about a past event or remember an interaction with someone. Perhaps while scanning your abdomen, you come across a combination of sensations like slight constriction and warm tingling. week 4: applying mindfulness skills in daily life 135 Simultaneously, a pleasant memory co-emerges alongside the sensations. So far, nothing unhelpful has occurred in your practice. It only becomes unpro ductive if you buy into it, immersing yourself in the memory to arouse more pleasant sensations. In fact, revisiting the memory contains an implicit motivation to generate more of these pleasant sensations, perhaps in your belly, which then stimu late sensations in your chest and throat. This can progressively increase your heart rate and blood flow under the skin of your cheeks, leading to sensa tions of increased warmth. Now you are really going for a ride! This is not bad or wrong. It s just another reinforcing event that strengthens the habit of craving pleasant experiences. Since pleasant sensations are impermanent, your craving can never be fully satisfied. Like a perpetually unquenched thirst, craving leads to dissatisfaction. As you continue learning this practice, being caught up in craving is not something to fear but rather something to investigate honestly and learn from. As your equanimity improves, you begin to observe sensations as sen sations and nothing more. By accepting that this is all they are, your desire for them naturally subsides. If the craving for a pleasant sensation persists, focus on the center of the sensation for up to half a minute (about seven breaths), examining it equani mously and impersonally and without making it yours or a part of you. To remain objective and equanimous while staying curious about how nature operates within this mind and body, have a good look at which of the four basic characteristics of sensations is predominant. Is it mass, motion, temperature, or density, or perhaps some combination of these together? Does it stay exactly the same from moment to moment, or does it change? Can you observe its impermanent nature? Can you feel any pleasantness associated with the four characteristics, without suppressing or denying the pleasantness? In other words, can you allow yourself to feel it fully while accepting it just as it is, unconditionally, and without clinging to it i.e., equanimously? Then, shift your attention to the next part of your body and continue scanning. Afterward, acknowledge both your effort and your exist ing strength, as these are vital tools for working with similar experiences in the future. However, don t be surprised if this is occasionally difficult to do. s comforting to remember that you are a student of equanimity and can only do your best, and it s important to remain patient and kind with your self as you learn this unusual and sometimes complex task. Cultivating equanimity takes time! The benefits of applying equanimity to craving extend beyond emotional regulation. Daily experiences accompanied by craving, such as hunger, also arise and pass. In their intermittent fasting method [2], Dr. Michael Mosley and Mimi Spencer explained that the expectation that hunger will intensify during fasting until it becomes intolerable is incorrect. Instead, they found that hunger passes and people can have a productive day on an empty stomach. After all, what is hunger but a constellation of impermanent body 136 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE sensations and co-emerging thoughts? Remaining equanimous and applying right effort (described in Week 2) enables us to let go of catastrophic thoughts and cravings, even for something as fundamental as food. Craving to Avoid (Aversion) Our natural reaction to unpleasant experiences is to avoid them. This instinctive response occurs during body-scanning as well. We may uncon sciously shift our posture, let our minds wander, or even cut a practice ses sion short to avoid unpleasant body sensations. While avoidance may provide momentary relief, it also deprives us of discovering the deeper real ity of all unpleasant experiences: they are impermanent. By learning to just observe these sensations with equanimity, we can discover the powerful relief that arises when we directly experience the transient nature of all sensations. Even the most intense pain, whether physical or emotional, can decrease by about 50% within 30seconds if we accept the moment-to-moment sen sations as they are while feeling them, without reacting with aversion and wishing that they were gone. Our studies have demonstrated this with both chronic pain [3] and acute experimental pain [4]. This 30-second practice, known as the Mindfulness-based Interoceptive Exposure Task (MIET) and sometimes called mindful mapping, has also been shown to effectively manage post-surgical pain following total joint replacement [5] and chronic back pain [6]. You will be learning this Mindfulness-integrated Cognitive Behavior Therapy (MiCBT) method in the Integrating Skills in Daily Life section later in this chapter. When we approach pain-related sensations with reactivity rather than equanimity, there is increased activity in the brain s default mode network, as we are taking the pain personally. At the same time, there is reduced connectivity in brain networks responsible for attention and sensory aware ness, such as the central executive network and salience networks [7]. However, as equanimity improves, we can approach pain-related sensations equanimously without catastrophizing or worrying about them worsening. This reduces the brain s stress response. The brain does not go into a sympa thetically driven alert mode, as it has not detected a threat. Stress hor mones, such as adrenaline, are not secreted, and instead, natural pain-relieving chemicals, such as endorphins, are released. For many, this rapid decrease in pain intensity seems unbelievable at first, yet it s a skill we teach daily and it works! If you encounter strong sensations of pain during practice, focus on the center of the most intense area for about 30seconds (roughly seven breaths) while remaining equanimous, calm, patient, and, most importantly, without identifying with the experience. View it as an impermanent bodily experi ence made of four non-harmful characteristics. If you don t interpret the experience as a potential threat and remain equanimous, you ll likely feel week 4: applying mindfulness skills in daily life 137 significant relief. Remember that you are a student of equanimity and not supposed to be an expert at it, so remaining patient and kind with yourself if the practice seems difficult is important. As you continue this program, the skills you ll be training will also help you achieve a similar result when you encounter emotional pain. Craving to Become A few years ago, at an African short-film festival, one of the films illustrated the frequently endorsed belief that our lives would be better if we were a dif ferent person. It tells the story of a poor family man living in an African jungle village who frequently admires the big city s high-rise buildings break ing the horizon of the bare landscape, dreaming of going there one day to live the big life. One day, while everyone is still asleep, driven by his profound desire, he steals the family s savings to go to the big city, hoping to fulfill his dream of becoming a grand quelqu (French for big somebody But soon after his arrival in the city, despite his keenness to impress with careless spending, to his great disappointment, nobody there takes him seri ously. Deeply disillusioned and with pockets emptied, he sadly and shame fully returns to the village and tells his wife he would never want to be a grand quelqu again. Unsurprised, she replies, You have finally become a grand quelqu Her message highlights the counterproductivity of Craving to Become, and the potential suffering it creates for ourselves and those around us. This very short film beautifully conveys the message that being blind to our true worth and craving to become someone we re not results only in suffering. During meditation, you may also notice becoming more aware of a crav ing to become someone better or different. For instance, a parent might long to be more patient, never losing their temper; a professional might fantasize about becoming a top performer in their field, admired and praised; or you might crave greater self-discipline, imagining the perfect body or impeccable self-control. Perhaps you ve even imagined becoming an excep tional meditator, picturing yourself as a meditation teacher or an enlight ened figure admired by others. While the intention to grow or improve is inherently wholesome, the craving to become someone or something different can create ten sion, attachment, and dissatisfaction. This craving can manifest as an internal pressure that keeps the mind in a cycle of striving for something that isn t present in the moment. Note that this inner drive may have been initially motivated by our early experiences and the core schemas that were formed, such as m not good enough if I don t become some one special, or To be worthy of my parents approval and love, I have to become as good as my sister, or later beliefs, such as that being at the top of a company s or government s hierarchy is what we should strive for if we want to be heard and respected. This dopamine-seeking mode 138 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE prevents us from fully settling into the present experience and delays the cultivation of equanimity. When this craving arises, it s important to recognize it and also become aware of how it disrupts your equanimity. Imagining being different in some ways is often accompanied by strong, pleasurable sensations of craving. These sensations reinforce a future-oriented mindset and perpetuate the belief that you are not enough as you are. Craving, in this sense, distracts you from the truth of the moment the impermanence of sensations and thoughts and fosters the illusion that happiness can only be found in becoming someone different. As in all mindfulness practices, we aspire to cultivate Right Effort. You started practicing Right Effort in Week 2 by monitoring thoughts and men tal states and discerning which to entertain and which to let go. During body-scanning, Right Effort, in relation to craving to become, involves the intention to clearly experience the impermanence of sensations just as they feel in the present moment, without craving for those you experienced in the past or others you wish to experience in the future, and without chasing what feels pleasurable when you think about yourself in the future. The more, and the more clearly, you realize that the craving for a different expe rience itself passes away, the more you cultivate a deeper understanding of how craving to become is maintained, and how impermanent it really is. You are training yourself to let go of the unwholesome craving to become. For example, instead of craving to become a perfect parent, student, professional, or meditator, observe how this craving manifests in the body perhaps as a slight leaning forward or a tightness in the chest. By observing these reactions equanimously, you release the attachment to these sensations, and the craving begins to lose its power. Alongside discerning when Craving to Become is present, and approach ing its manifestations in body and mind equanimously, like a good gardener, you can also plant seeds and tend to young saplings of wholesome inten tions to grow and improve. You can do this by replacing unwholesome thoughts for example, those that co-emerge with unpleasant body sensa tions when you reject yourself with wholesome ones, such as those that let you feel light and at ease in the body when you accept yourself and care about the person you are with a kinder mindset. This will be explored fur ther in Week Body Sensations and Trauma As discussed in Week 2, body sensations can serve as powerful memory cues. When we scan through the body, we are not just connecting with the present moment we may also touch upon memories. For individuals who have experienced trauma, such as abuse, assault, or attachment disruptions in early childhood, body-scanning can sometimes bring these experiences into week 4: applying mindfulness skills in daily life 139 conscious awareness. This is especially likely when the trauma has not yet been fully processed or integrated, meaning that the nervous system contin ues to react to internal trauma-related cues as if the threat were still present. In these cases, trauma memories may resurface through subtle or intense body sensations that trigger automatic emotional reactions, such as fear or anger either suppressing or expressing emotions in ways that may feel out side the person s control. Mindfulness offers a safe way to engage with these experiences and process them effectively, as it focuses on observing moment-to-moment sensations and thoughts with equanimity it is simply a method of being neutrally exposed to thoughts and body sensations that doesn t add or take anything away from our experience. However, how we respond to the memories that may re-emerge is key. This is how this 2,500-year-old meditation practice has been used as a means of purification of the mind, allowing memories and reactive habits learned in the past to reemerge and be neutralized through an accurate under standing of what they are just impermanent thoughts and co-emerging sen sations arising in the present moment. Do you recall the co-emergence effect in relation to spontaneous thoughts during meditation discussed in Week This same principle applies to body sensations: While scanning the body, if you encounter a body sensation that is sufficiently similar to a sensation that you felt in the past, however long ago this might be, the present sensation acts as a memory cue and you might feel the same emotional tone, either with or with out clear understanding of the underlying reason, depending on whether the associated memory becomes conscious a clear flashback or not. The next process is just as important to understand: The way you reacted then may also be the way you are tempted to react now; in other words, your response to this experience may still be conditioned. This could be the fear of a child, the frustration and anger of a teenager, or the disgust or shame of an adult. You may also remember from Week 3 that we don t react to triggers themselves, such as a traumatic event. Instead, we react to the body sensations that co-emerge with our evaluation of those triggers. Hence, the way we eval uate a traumatic memory determines how we react and the level of distress suffering we experience. Therefore, developing a clear understand ing of equanimity, impermanence, and egolessness (the concept of not self is essential for working skillfully with difficult thoughts and sensations. From this understanding, the possible unpleasant experiences encoun tered on this journey are integral parts of this approach to cultivating wis dom and well-being. They are opportunities for desensitization through exposure and not adverse effects of the meditation [8-10]. We will look in more detail at how to address intense sensations effectively later in this chapter, using the Mindfulness-based Interoceptive Exposure Task (MIET). While this approach offers a powerful pathway for healing, working with trauma-related memories requires care and sometimes additional support. If you have experienced severe trauma, we strongly recommend practicing under the guidance of an informed therapist. 140 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE s explore this through the experience of Sarah, a 32-year-old client diagnosed with posttraumatic stress disorder. Like many people who have experienced trauma, Sarah struggled to feel bod ily sensations during emotional experiences, a common symptom of dissociation which is, in its milder form, a reduced ability to perceive what we normally feel, think, or remember. In her case, dissociation had developed as a survival mechanism in childhood after enduring repeated sexual abuse. As an adult, this protective response numbed her awareness of body sensations during nighttime and sexual experiences, but over time, it generalized to other emotional contexts, including making everyday decisions. She had seen several therapists and tried various medications over the years, but with little improve ment, if any. The dissociation led to significant challenges in Sarah s life. At night, she would take freezing showers to feel real, seeking to reconnect with her body and manage intense anxiety. Her disconnection from bodily awareness impacted her relationship with her husband, as she could only recall their sexual experiences if they involved intense, even painful, sensations. Dissociation also undermined her ability to evaluate consequences and make sound decisions, a process closely tied to emotional awareness, which is critical for understanding situations, assessing outcomes, and making decisions [11]. For Sarah, the lack of bodily awareness meant that she was not able to feel her emotions and gauge the consequences of her decisions effectively. She lost mul tiple jobs because she was unable to sense the co-emerging physical and emo tional cues that might have motivated her to complete tasks or address potential consequences. When we evaluate a situation accurately, it is the co-emerging sensation in the body that tells us whether or not it feels right to choose a particular course of action. Through body-scanning, Sarah began to reconnect with her body sensations, accepting them with equanimity and gradually recovering memories. This pro cess allowed her to experience and resolve painful emotions, leading to rapid improvements in her decision-making, relationships, and overall well-being. For example, she regained the ability to recall pain-free sexual experiences with her husband and developed a deeper sense of care for the consequences of her actions. The metamorphosis was surprisingly rapid, despite the emer gence of painful memories. She later mentioned that her improvement had been rapid because she had really understood the importance of applying equanimity to body sensations during the practice in order to allow painful memories to arise and pass in their own right. She was able to resolve lifelong trauma-related symptoms in just a few months. During her last therapy session, she said, You can only be present by feeling the body, feeling that aliveness in your body. Note that Sarah s clear understanding about how to address trauma related memories was central to her success, whereas not having a clear framework for how to work with them could have been associated with a week 4: applying mindfulness skills in daily life 141 worsening of symptoms. Sarah attributed her success to applying three core skills to her experience of painful memories: (1) she remained aware of body sensations and thoughts without avoiding them, (2) she perceived them for what they truly are as just body sensations and just thoughts without identifying with them, and (3) she remained equanimous, knowing that sooner or later they would pass on their own if she did not engage with them. Trauma-Informed Mindfulness: Caution or Empowerment? In recent years, much has been written about applying a trauma-informed approach to mindfulness programs. Given that about 70% of people world wide have experienced at least one traumatic event in their lifetime [12, 13], it is essential to consider trauma when designing and delivering these programs. Often, the focus is on staying within a window of tolerance, aiming to protect individuals from becoming overwhelmed by their emo tional experiences. Techniques such as moving attention to neutral sensa tions (e.g., the feet on the ground), focusing on external sensory stimuli such as sounds, and limiting exposure to potentially triggering sensations are commonly suggested. This cautious approach is prevalent in many mainstream mindfulness programs, particularly those offered outside of clinical settings, such as through apps, YouTube videos, or non-clinician-led mindfulness courses. While these platforms often serve as an introduction to mindfulness for many individuals, they also contribute to a conservative stance on working with challenging emotions. While caution is important, particularly for beginners, an overly protec tive approach inadvertently limits the effectiveness of mindfulness for deeper emotional work. Gradual and intentional exposure to difficult sensations, while allowing ourselves to experience them without reactivity, has been identified as one of the key mechanisms by which mindfulness meditation has its beneficial effects [14, 15]. This is aligned with principles found in cognitive behavioral therapy (CBT) and other evidence-based exposure methods [16]. A balance between equanimity and skillful exposure allows individuals to confront trauma-related emotions safely, leading to transfor mation rather than avoidance. Nearly 40 years of published research [17] have shown that exposure frees us from patterns of emotional avoidance. As Sarah s story illustrates, the combination of body-scanning with equanimity can enable individu als to approach previously overwhelming sensations, leading to profound transformation. Nevertheless, we all have our limits, however flexible they may be. Accordingly, if your emotional experience goes beyond your limit of bearable discomfort and is becoming distressing, stop body scan ning and practice Progressive Muscle Relaxation (PMR) for a few ses sions before resuming body scanning. If you experience persistent emotional distress that includes resenting yourself or others, we recom mend practicing a guided 8-minute-long Loving-Kindness meditation at 142 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE the end of your PMR practice. You can find this meditation on track 27 or in the MiCBT Guide app in Stage 4.1. This will help soothe the wounds of the past. Wisdom in Your Practice Training in mindfulness involves observing the natural process of change in your body and mind without interference. To fully establish mindfulness, it is essential to recognize the universal reality of impermanence across four dimen sions of experience: the body, bodily sensations, mental states, and mental con tent (thoughts, images, etc.) [18]. Developing mindfulness of impermanence while scanning the body equanimously helps you release unhelpful emotions and protects you from the harm caused by reacting to them. With effective training, you can profoundly reduce craving and aversion, even in challenging situations. By experiencing firsthand that everything in your mind and body is impermanent rather than simply hoping or believ ing it to be true you build the mental strength needed to manage difficult experiences. This insight into impermanence is universal and will become clearer as you continue your body-scanning practice. As you attune to the transient nature of sensations, you naturally develop more patience and tol erance, knowing that discomfort will pass. Wisdom develops through this direct understanding of impermanence. Of course, as you begin, this proposition may seem a little far-fetched. You may find yourself instinctively preferring pleasant sensations and avoid ing unpleasant ones just like evolution programmed you to! This is entirely normal. Rather than letting this inclination lead to frustration about slow progress or unrealistic expectations for quick breakthroughs, simply acknowledge and accept this natural tendency. These first weeks of practice are just the beginning, laying an important foundation for well-being and personal growth. Each small step toward your goal is valuable. As Lao Tzu advised, to do great work, we must begin with the small, as the journey of a thousand miles begins with a single step [19]. Training Mindfulness Skills Checking Your Readiness You have now completed your first week of training in mindfulness of body sensations using body-scanning. Congratulations! How was this week for you? Were you able to maintain your commitment to practicing 30minutes twice daily? If so, did you benefit from it? Can you feel sensations in some parts of the body? Were you able to reduce or prevent the reactions that would normally occur when experiencing more intense sensations? Most week 4: applying mindfulness skills in daily life 143 importantly, were you able to shorten the duration of your reactivity, a key measure of progress? If you remained committed to daily practice and had the correct understand ing, you might already be finding it easier to handle sensations, whether pleasant or unpleasant, without judgment. If this hasn t been the case perhaps because practice didn t happen as often as intended that s okay. It s not a problem if you don t let it become one. Sometimes new tasks can be a little daunting, and it takes time to get used to them. The last thing we want is self-blame or other unhelpful judgments that create doubt or a sense of failure. Instead of falling into this trap, simply recommit and start again with the same practice for a few days, with a smile and renewed resolution. Being kind to yourself doesn t cost anything and can carry you a long way when facing challenges. By the fifth or sixth day, did you feel a bit encumbered by the audio instructions and wished you could practice in silence? If so, that s a positive sign. It suggests you re no longer relying on the instructions to guide you or to manage intrusive thoughts. When to Proceed to the Next Step re ready to move on to the next step when you can feel at least 20% of your body surface and have started to manage some of the craving and aver sion reactions when they arise. If this is the case, you can now begin scan ning the body in silence, as outlined below. t wait until you have no thoughts or can feel your entire body before practicing without audio guidance. Intrusive thoughts and blank spots in the body are typical during mindfulness meditation and provide opportuni ties to cultivate equanimity and insight. Encountering these challenges allows you to grow in your practice, as you learn to face unwanted thoughts and unmet expectations. If You Need More Time Delaying the next step when necessary is a wise and productive choice. If ve been practicing daily but still struggle to feel sensations or find your self frequently caught up in thoughts during most sessions, consider going back to the practice of mindfulness of breath until your mind settles a bit. Whether this is for a week or a few days, taking a step back is by no means a waste of time, whereas rushing ahead can be tiring and unproductive. Once you feel more focused with mindfulness of breath, you can transi tion back to this week s body-scanning exercise. If difficulties with focus return when you begin scanning the body in silence (without the audio recording, as explained below), try starting each session with 10minutes of mindfulness of breath without audio guidance. In this way, the first 10min utes will sharpen your mind, so to speak, so that you are more focused when you scan the body for the remaining 20minutes. In any case, keep at it and don t give up! It does no harm to accept our limitations and take a little more time when necessary. Keep in mind the 144 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE classic story of the hare and the tortoise [20]: although the hare was faster, it was the tortoise s consistent, measured effort that ultimately led to success. Unilateral Body-Scanning in Silence with Strong Determination This week, you will be continuing to practice unilateral body scanning, now with three progressive adjustments that will further enhance your produc tivity and proficiency: practicing in silence, customizing your scanning pace, and incorporating strong determination to deepen equanimity. The Practice Introduction to Silent Practice Practicing part-by-part body-scanning in silence brings several unique ben efits, allowing for a deeper and more personalized experience. One of the most profound benefits of silent practice is the opportunity to reprocess memories unhindered by the intrusion of audio instructions. Without the distraction of attention repeatedly moving to external audio instructions, you can allow thoughts, emotions, and memories to surface naturally and observe them with equanimity. Customizing the Pace of Your Practice Practicing in silence lets you move attention from part to part at your own pace. In areas where sensations are harder to feel, slow down and give your self more time to notice subtle details. In areas of no apparent sensation, hold your attention there for up to 30seconds, with equanimity, before moving to the next part. This slower pace supports the development of interoceptive awareness, helping your brain form connections that make it easier to feel sensations over time. In areas where sensations are readily apparent, you can move your atten tion more quickly. This prevents attachment to specific sensations by encour aging you to observe, accept, and then relinquish each experience before moving on. A faster scanning pace also helps keep your focus sharp, reduc ing the likelihood of getting lost in thought or distracted. By the end of this week, aim to complete two to three full cycles through your body during each 30-minute session. If you find yourself scanning a bit more slowly than this, gradually pick up the pace to reach at least two cycles by the end of the week. Conversely, if you find that your attention is natu rally flowing faster than three cycles in 30minutes, slow down by focusing on smaller areas to enhance your attention to detail. Strong Determination Being committed and resolute in achieving your goals in life is indispensable when you encounter challenges, and the greater the challenge, the more week 4: applying mindfulness skills in daily life 145 determination you need to overcome it. In MiCBT, and in the Vipassana meditation tradition on which it is based, sitting in strong determination (adhitthana in Pali) means making a strong commitment to remain immo bile, keep your attention focused on the body, and stay as equanimous as possible during practice. This means sitting completely still while scanning the body, as a way of surrendering to and accepting whatever experience arises. It is probably the greatest skill influencing the effectiveness of our practice, because it makes our equanimity grow, both in our meditation practice and in our daily life. We learn to hold firm, to welcome whatever experience we come across, and to not get involved or tied up with it. We learn to not mind so much. Time can seem to pass quickly when we enjoy ourselves and very slowly when we don t. Similarly, when we practice in silence without auditory instruc tions reminding us of the passage of time, our perception of time can also vary. It becomes a function of our equanimity and ability to keep our attention in the present. For instance, if we experience boredom or aversion to pain sensations, every minute of practice can seem to take a very long time. If we feel peaceful and present in the experience without any craving or aversion, time seems to fly and we feel that the half-hour practice has gone by in just a few minutes. From today, make a strong commitment not to move any part of your body at all during your practice, no matter what happens. Witnessing the experience equanimously neutralizes your usual reaction to it. By the same token, your equanimity toward this experience in the body also neutralizes your learned reaction to all similar experiences, training your brain through neuroplasticity to strengthen adaptive neural pathways that support calm and deliberate responses in daily life [21]. If you encounter body sensations that are intense enough to distract you from continuing to scan, then it s best to work on them for a short while. Focus your attention on the center of the most intense area and hold your attention there with as much equanimity as possible. Dissect it. Examine it in detail without any reactivity whatsoever. Investigate its predominant characteristics, as discussed above. These could manifest as any combina tion of temperature, mass, movement, and density. Keep your attention there, calmly, peacefully, and patiently. After 30seconds of equanimous attention, the intensity of the sensation will likely diminish, and you will be able to scan other parts of the body more easily. If not, stay up to 2minutes on this part, and then move on to scanning other parts. The Stalactites Story Here is a memorable event recounted by Bruno: I will never forget a very good friend s decision to be equanimous, no matter what! About 30years ago, when he became sufficiently curious about this practice, he decided to accompany me and attend an intensive 10-day mindful ness retreat taught by the renowned teacher, S. N. Goenka. During this kind of 146 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE retreat, people practice from 4:30am to 9:30pm, with 6hours of breaks for meals and rest periods. This means sitting on the floor for a total of about 12hours every day. During the first three and a half days, people practice mindfulness of breath and then proceed with various types of body scanning techniques, much as you will learn during this program. Being severely asthmatic and allergic to dust mites, my friend s first course was tough. He had real trouble sitting on the hall s carpet and next to people han dling their woolen shawls. He kept on sneezing and leaving the meditation hall to use his inhaler. I felt very sorry for him and thought that he might drop out of the course, but eventually he made it to the tenth day. Despite his understandable difficulties during the course, he was impressed with the effects that the course had had in his life and proposed to return to the Vipassana Center to attend a second course. I accompanied him and we attended another course together the following year. During this course, I couldn t help being a little too curious about how he was coping. On the fourth day, when body scanning began, the course required making a commitment not to move at all during three of the daily practice sessions, in order to develop greater equanimity. This is traditionally called sitting in strong determination. I anticipated another catastrophe, but being a more experienced practitioner meant that I sat in the front row and closer to the teacher, so opening my eyes and looking at my friend in the back row dur ing a strong determination hour was out of the question. Still, I couldn t help it. I opened my eyes and very slowly and discreetly twisted around to get a glimpse of my friend s situation. He had placed a large towel on his thighs and was sitting straight like a statue of Buddha. His runny nose was absolutely shocking. It looked like two stalactites linking his nose to the towel. He knew what to expect and came prepared with a towel. Come what may, he would not sway! He d remembered what had happened during the last course we attended and this time decided that he would remain immobile and equanimous, no matter what! This story of determination and equanimity has never left my mind. My friend s life has changed a lot for the better since, and I am so grateful for the practice that has been a catalyst for this. I am hoping that this week s practice will also be a determinant of change for you, even if it is a very small change in the way you react to situations. It is now time for you to practice, but please, blow your nose if you need to! In daily life, too, when challenges arise, you ll be able to do just the same as this good friend. Rather than running from a problem, you ll face it more patiently, calmly, and peacefully, without avoidance or anxiety, until it is at least partly resolved. This capacity develops because your practice trains your brain to respond in this adaptive way. Every obstacle you encounter while scanning the body becomes part of your training ground. Without challenges during body-scanning, such as unpleasant sensations, you wouldn t have the opportunity to develop the equanimity needed to week 4: applying mindfulness skills in daily life 147 reshape how your brain handles frustration and distress in daily life. We need weights that are heavy enough to build our brain equanimity muscle ! This is one of the reasons we don t get overly comfortable during practice why we t lean back on the chair or lie down. In this way, your problem becomes your solution: the discomfort itself becomes your teacher, guiding you toward equanimity and freedom from reactivity. Keeping this in mind can reassure and motivate you as you sit in strong determination this week. Skills You Will Learn Exploring Egolessness Through Body-Scanning Practicing body-scanning with strong determination not only enhances mindfulness and equanimity but also serves as a gateway to understanding egolessness, a core principle in Buddhist psychology. Through this practice, we begin to examine our deeply ingrained assumption of what constitutes the self this sense of that we sometimes cherish and other times criti cize, which makes us take things so personally. When we look very carefully, is there really such a thing as a separate, lasting self that exists independently from a universe where everything is in constant flux? Your practice allows you to explore this question with greater objectivity. By remaining still during body-scanning and observing the moment-to-moment flux of sensations and thoughts, you may start to wonder: Where is this Where is this in this ever-changing landscape? Your strong determination to stay immobile and objectively observe the impermanent nature of body sensations and related thoughts supports uncovering a deeper truth about the nature of existence: while there is con sciousness, there is no fixed or permanent self that resides in, or experi ences, these sensations. For this reason, egolessness is often described as not-self or non-self. Insight with Wisdom often develops naturally with age, as life offers countless opportu nities to observe that everything is in a perpetual state of change. Through repeated experiences of impermanence such as shifts in our needs, rela tionships, and abilities we come to accept that life is in a perpetual state of transformation. We then try to adapt and live wisely, in accordance with this reality. When we are younger, we tend to identify more strongly with our emo tions, views, and actions, integrating them into a rigid sense of self. However, research shows that as we age, we generally become less rigid, placing both positive and negative aspects of our identity into a broader and more flexible context [22]. One of the psychological hallmarks of aging is a loosening of attachment to who we are and who we once were a gradual, natural form of wis [23, 24]. But why wait for old age to benefit from this perspective? 148 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE Through mindfulness practice, we can cultivate the conditions for wisdom earlier in life, allowing us to experience greater harmony and peace long before age brings its natural wisdom. Insight Through Training Body-scanning offers one of the most direct avenues for developing wisdom by teaching us to observe and accept the constant change within our own internal experience. Here s how this process unfolds, shifting our relation ship with body sensations and paving the way for insight: 1 Observation of change: During body-scanning, we focus on physical sensations in different parts of the body. Over time, we notice that sensa tions arise, are present for some length of time, and whether prominent or subtle, eventually fade away or shift. This could be the sensation of heat, movement, tingling, or so on each of which comes and goes. Observing this repeatedly makes it clear that sensations are transient they don t last forever. 2 Insight into impermanence: By repeatedly observing that sensations are constantly changing, we come to a realization that nothing about these sensations is fixed or permanent. This understanding goes beyond the ory; it is a direct experiential insight. We have confirmed it in our own experience! For instance, an intense, unpleasant sensation might initially feel unbearable, but as we maintain equanimity, we notice it diminishes or transforms, demonstrating its impermanence. 3 From identification to observation: Normally, we tend to identify with our sensations and emotions. If we feel anxious, we might think, I am anx ious, fully identifying with that state as if it is who we are. However, rec ognizing the impermanence of sensations and thoughts allows us to step back from this identification. Instead of thinking, I am anxious, we can observe, There are sensations of anxiety present, without merging them with our sense of This shift transforms our relationship with sensa tions from one of identification to one of mindful observation. Thought after thought, seeing thoughts only as thoughts, and sensa tion after sensation, seeing sensations only as sensations, the same insight is applied: This is not mine, this is not part of me, it arises and passes away. Reacting to it will only maintain my habit of reacting, which will maintain suffering. From moment to moment, session after session, we learn not to iden tify with mental, emotional, and physical experiences, no matter what they are. As far as we are able to remain equanimous and objective, we observe bits of self arising and passing. We notice the impermanence of what we usually call and We develop insight as we realize, My thoughts arise and pass; my sensations arise and pass; my pain arises and passes; my attitudes arise and pass; my resentments and cravings arise and pass; my views and values arise and pass. As we week 4: applying mindfulness skills in daily life 149 witness this impermanence directly, we cultivate a profound wisdom: everything changes, and we can find freedom in accepting this. By letting go of identifying with fleeting phenomena, we release ourselves from the suffering tied to the transient constructs of and finding freedom in the wisdom of impermanence. The understanding and effort to integrate egolessness into our lives is central to a deepening of well-being and personal growth. Without increas ing our ability to let go of attachment to who we are and who we should our views, cravings, and self-image would not be able to change, and we would stay imprisoned by our sense of who we are. How can we grow and change if our attachments remain the same? Integrating Skills in Daily Life Cultivating Equanimity Toward Sensations Now it s time to bring the skills you ve developed on the cushion into your daily life, applying awareness of body sensations with equanimity to all situ ations. This involves paying attention to your internal experience from the moment you wake up until you fall asleep at night. For example, practicing equanimity toward sensations right now while reading this book would mean being aware of and feeling sensations in your body without needing to change what you are feeling. While reacting to sensations may feel instinctive, making an effort to respond equanimously in various situations throughout the day helps integrate the wis dom gained from meditation into your everyday life. This approach lays the groundwork for aligning your behavior with your values, which we will explore in Week 5. For now, the aim is to remain aware and equanimous toward body sensations and their accompanying thoughts as often as possible. One possible exception to this is during sexual intimacy. In these situa tions, maintaining equanimity might lead to confusion or awkwardness, as sexual experiences typically require some level of desire to unfold naturally. While reducing reactivity can still enhance these moments allowing you to be more attuned to your partner s needs and fostering deeper connection complete equanimity might not serve this context well. Outside of intimate situations, do your best to sustain an attitude of awareness and equanimity toward your internal experience throughout the week. Mindfulness-Based Interoceptive Exposure Task To help you integrate equanimity toward body sensations and regulate emotions during stressful or painful moments, the Mindfulness-based Interoceptive Exposure Task (MIET) provides a structured, practical 150 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE approach. This brief, evidence-based exercise takes just 30seconds and guides you to observe sensations in the body through the four distinct char acteristics discussed in the previous chapter mass, motion, temperature, and density rating them before and after feeling them with equanimity. s walk through the steps of the MIET: Steps of the MIET 1 Identify the area of strongest sensation: During a distressing moment, scan your body to locate the area where the sensation feels strongest. 2 How intense is it? Take a moment to note how intense or distressing the sensation feels, rating it from least to most intense. 3 Observe the four characteristics: How are each of the four characteris tics being experienced within this area of most intense sensation? For example, how cool or warm, how tight or loose, how still or moving, or how light or heavy does it feel? 4 Apply equanimity: With an attitude of unconditional acceptance, observe the predominant characteristics of the sensation for about 30seconds (approximately seven to nine breaths), without judgment or reactivity. 5 Observe the four characteristics again: Now, after 30seconds of equani mous attention, re-examine how each characteristic feels now. Has it changed? Is it looser or more constricted? Warmer or cooler? 6 How intense is it now? Note to yourself how intense or distressing the sensation is now, from least to most intense. The MIET extends the skills you re practicing during body-scanning with strong determination into everyday life situations. By using the MIET each time a stressful situation arises, you train your mind and body to feel, accept, and let go of sensations without habitual reactivity in real time you train to regulate emotions as they arise. Note that the MIET is not a practice to suppress emotion on the contrary. Because emotions are composed of body sensations, and the MIET guides you to pay attention to them with unconditional accept ance, you learn to feel and accept emotions more fully, rather than sup pressing them. Nor is the MIET practiced to prevent actions. By training you to prevent reactivity, it allows you to choose the most proactive decision or action. To get started, listen to the Introduction to the Mindfulness-Based Interoceptive Exposure Task (MIET) (Track 10 on MP3 or Meditations Stage 1.4 on the MiCBT Guide app). This 3-minute track provides essential background information. Then, use the The Mindfulness-based Interoceptive Exposure Task guided practice the first few times (Track 11 on MP3 or meditation Stage 1.4 on MiCBT Guide app). This 6-minute track will walk you through the MIET step by step, helping you become confident in using the technique. week 4: applying mindfulness skills in daily life 151 Recording Your Practice To maximize the benefits of your MIET practice, use the Mindfulness-based Interoceptive Signature Scale (MISS) to systematically track your progress. You can find the MISS on the MiCBT Guide app (under Assessments in Stage 1.4) or use the paper form provided. Recording your 30-second equa nimity practices this week offers several key advantages: 1 Visually recording stress responses: By visually documenting your sensa tions, you map how emotions and stress manifest in your body. This helps identify recurring emotional patterns, or interoceptive signa tures, training you to observe stress responses with greater precision. 2 Tracking equanimity over time: Rating the intensity and characteristics of sensations before and after equanimity practice creates a clear record of your progress. This tangible evidence highlights your growing ability to engage with challenging sensations skillfully. Here s how to document your MIET practice: 1 Rate initial intensity: As soon as possible after a distressing event, mark the intensity of the area of strongest sensation or associated distress on the Intensity Before scale. 2 Rate initial characteristics: Place a dot along each of the four scales mass, motion, temperature, and density to represent how each quality feels initially. For example, if a sensation feels very heavy, mark near Heaviest on the Mass scale. 3 Draw a before equanimity line: Connect these four dots with a solid line, which visually represents your initial sensory experience. 4 Rate characteristics after equanimity: After 30seconds of equanimity practice, mark how each characteristic feels now. For example, if the heaviness has lessened, place the dot closer to Neutral on the Mass scale to reflect this change. 5 Draw an after equanimity line: Connect the new dots with a dotted line to graph the sensory changes. Comparing the solid before line to the dotted after line shows how sensations shifted through equanimity practice. 6 Record intensity after: Lastly, mark the Intensity After scale to capture the intensity of sensations, or associated distress, after your MIET prac tice. Any difference between the before and after ratings captures the impact of equanimity on your experience (Figure 4.1). MIET and Emotional Regulation When practicing and recording the MIET this week, it s important to keep in mind that the intensity of distress in daily life situations can vary greatly. Some events, like missing a bus, may be associated with mild frustration, while others, such as a car accident, may bring about extreme distress. The 152 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE examples that follow showcase how the MIET can be applied across a range of emotional and physical experiences, whether mild or intense. As you read these examples, remember that you ll be using the MIET in the context of your own unique emotional landscape, and even small improvements in equanimity can lead to significant relief over time. As represented by the solid line, the sensations of heat in his face and hands and the feeling of agitation through his body felt over whelming. After applying equanimity for 30seconds, the dotted line reflects the marked reduction in intensity of these sensations. With regular practice, the brain gradually rewires itself to handle stress and regulate emotions more effectively. As the brain s executive func tions, particularly in the prefrontal cortex, become more adept at calming overactivation of the amygdala an area central to fear and stress responses [25] we react less in situations that previously would have over whelmed us. This spontaneous equanimity, without much effort on our part, can be very surprising! Through his regular use of the MIET, he found himself remaining calm in a situation that would typically trigger an outburst, reflecting the deep, spontane ous impact of equanimity training on even longstanding emotional reactivity. The MIET and Pain One of the most remarkable effects of the MIET is its effectiveness in man aging both acute and chronic pain. While the MIET has been an important emotion-regulation technique within MiCBT since its inception in 2001, it was first developed as an independent method for pain reduction in 2012. Our initial study showed that practicing the MIET can reduce chronic pain by approximately 50% within just 30 60seconds [3]. Participants with chronic pain lasting anywhere from 6months to 20years caused by conditions such as arthritis, disk damage, whiplash, migraines, and fibromyalgia reported a 50% decrease in pain intensity after just half a minute of practice. Even more strikingly, these results persisted over two and a half months of participants practicing the MIET on their own. Importantly, they also experienced substan tial decreases in depression, anxiety, and stress alongside their pain reduction. 158 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE We found a similar effect in immediate pain reduction on experimentally induced pain. University students who practiced the MIET could manage acute pain for longer than those who used distraction as their coping method [4]. Since then, the MIET has been increasingly used to assist people with chronic pain. For instance, patients who learned the MIET before joint replacement surgery reported significant pain reduction [5]. Similarly, a team at the University of California, San Francisco, showed comparable results in a study involving individuals with chronic low back pain. Participants used a phone app to practice the MIET over an 8-week period, which resulted in significant reductions in pain levels and the extent to which pain interfered with their daily lives. Neuroimaging from the study showed clear changes in areas of the brain that manage acute pain [6, 26]. Together with our clinical observations, these findings suggest that prac ticing the MIET trains specific areas of the brain, enhancing the ability to manage painful and emotional experiences equanimously. Measuring Progress with Equanimity The difference between the Intensity before and Intensity after ratings, as well as the gap between the dots on the Before Equanimity line and the After Equanimity line, illustrates the change in your experience within just 30seconds. Provided you remain focused on your experience during this time, you can be confident that this change reflects your equanimity skills. Without equanimity, stress hormones like adrenaline and norepinephrine (noradrenaline) typically take longer than 30seconds to dissipate especially if the stressful event is still ongoing. The more adept you become at main taining non-reactivity, the greater the soothing effect you ll experience within just 30seconds of exposure to intense sensations. Figure 4.6 is from a middle-aged professional who experienced significant stress and anger during repeated phone discussions with his wife about set tlement issues. Each recorded experience was about the same unresolved matter. By the end of Week 4 of the program (the same point you are approaching), he reported feeling remarkably relieved despite the settlement issues remaining unresolved, thanks to his steadily improving ability to reg ulate his emotions through equanimity. Frequently Asked Questions intermediate i cannot feel ordinary sensations clearly unless i visualize each area of my body. is there somethin 1 1.3 book For the past few days, during meditation, I ve been obsessing about roast pork. I can hear the pork ribs crackling in the oven, and I can even smell the exquisite aroma in my mind. And I just can t help it, after my meditation, I have to cook something. Cravings, especially for familiar things like food, are common during body-scanning and can be easily explained. Remember how we discussed in Week 2 that spontaneous thoughts are likely informed by our recent and frequent thoughts, or previous emotionally charged experiences? To revisit these ideas, the frequency effect refers to habitual thoughts that arise because of their prominence in daily life. For instance, if you often think about food, these thoughts are more likely to arise during meditation, especially during moments when your concentration dips. The co-emergence effect, on the other hand, happens when body sensa tions during meditation resemble sensations linked to past experiences. For example, if you consciously or subconsciously feel a pleasant body sensation similar to one you ve felt when you were craving roast pork in the past, it acts as a memory cue, triggering the memory of craving roast pork. But this is only a memory of craving. It s not craving in and of itself it only becomes craving if you react to the memory by engaging with it, effectively making the past your present. During body-scanning, rather than focusing on the sensory memories themselves such as the image of roast pork or hearing it crackle redirect your attention to the pleasurable sensations that co-emerge with the memo ries. For example, when a pleasant memory of the smell of roast pork arises, shift your attention to the co-emerging pleasant body sensations and feel them equanimously, rather than focusing on the smell. Continue to feel the pleasant sensations, but without attachment to them or craving more. Just observe them with genuine curiosity for a little while, like a scientist observ ing the mechanisms of an experiment and collecting data. Which of the four characteristics is predominant: mass, motion, temperature, or density? Or is 164 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE it perhaps a combination of these? See how impermanent they are when you remain aware and equanimous. The trap of being attached to sensations that are changing all the time will become clear because of past attachment to them, we crave them when they are not present, and we remain unfulfilled. Through your practice of awareness and equanimity during meditation, you can learn to neutralize your cravings, which in turn will assist you in addressing craving-related behaviors in your daily life, like emotional eating, with greater freedom and understanding. intermediate for the past few days, during meditation, i ve been obsessing about roast pork. i can hear the pork 1 1.4 book I still can t feel many sensations in my face when I scan, so I have been imagining the shape of my nose, cheeks, and ears to try to feel more. Is it okay to do that? It s very common to need more time to feel the whole body. When we can t feel certain areas, the brain often automatically compen sates by visualizing those parts this occurs spontaneously. The brain is remarkable in its ability to problem-solve. When one function isn t fully developed, like sensing certain body parts, another function, such as visu alization, will come to the rescue. When you scan the body, you activate nerve cells contained in the soma tosensory cortex, a strip of the brain responsible for processing sensations. Another region, the insula, becomes involved when scanning sensations linked to emotions. If the neural connections between some groups of cells in the somatosensory cortex and insula aren t yet well-developed, there may be parts of the body that are still difficult to feel. For example, if the neurons that allow you to feel your ears aren t sufficiently connected with each other, it might be hard to feel your ears. When this happens, the visual cortex will activate to help you imagine their shape. However, relying on visualization strengthens the neural pathways for seeing rather than feeling the body. If our goal was simply to become more attentive and concentrated, more focused in the present moment, then visu alizing parts of the body and using verbal cues, like mantras, would cer tainly be helpful and welcome during our practice. But in MiCBT, our purpose is very different. We have a very strong rationale for training our selves to feel the body. We train to feel body sensations to cultivate equanim ity, not just concentration. In this way, we learn to not react with more craving and aversion toward sensations associated with emotions. Through this, we regulate both attention and emotions. If this resonates with you, do your best to just notice the blank spots when you scan the body and prevent craving or aversion from arising. Pay atten tion there for 30seconds to a minute, or until you feel a sensation. When you move, do so smilingly, so to speak, without impatience, disappoint ment, or frustration. This is part of normal practice and is not a reason to feel defeated or incompetent. week 4: applying mindfulness skills in daily life 165 For now, accept that this is how it is at this moment, and that this, too, is impermanent. Over time, you ll likely be able to feel every part of your body, except areas like hair or the outer nails. In the bigger picture, not feeling sensations when you scan the body is an opportunity to work on your patience and equanimity, so that you ll be able to do the same in daily life when things don t go as planned. intermediate i still can t feel many sensations in my face when i scan, so i have been imagining the shape of my 1 1.4 book I have serious pain in my knee when I practice, even though I sitting on a chair, not even cross-legged on the floor! I injured my knee at the gym about 5years ago. Do you think the body-scanning is making it worse? What should I do about it? Body-scanning doesn t create pain; it only reveals what is already there. Think of it like washing dirty clothes the dirt comes out, but it was in the clothes, not caused by the soap. Similarly, body-scanning is like soap; it allows you to notice existing sensations, but it doesn t cause them. The intense sensations you re feeling during practice were already there, or came up during practice, but not because of the practice. It s common to feel the physical memory of old injuries when you start body-scanning, and this tends to decrease quickly over time. However, if you re concerned about an underlying physical issue, it s always a good idea to consult your doctor. In the meantime, remember our discussion regarding the four basic char acteristics of body sensations. Since what we call pain is simply composed of intense sensations, it can be addressed with equanimity. Focus your atten tion on the most intense spot in your knee, staying with it as if you re in the calm eye of a storm, patiently and equanimously, for up to 30seconds. Which of the four characteristics is most predominant? Perhaps constric tion? Or heat? Observe what happens when you genuinely allow and wel come the experience, rather than resisting or resenting it. If you find it difficult to tolerate the sensation, it may be because you identifying too closely with it, believing that this is your sensation and therefore your body, and, consequently, your pain. Even if you re very committed to practicing in absolute immobility, gritting your teeth and sweating it out while resenting or hating the pain will only increase fear and aversion, and your mental state will be anything but equanimous. It s like trying to break a stone with an egg; it simply doesn t work! Remember that equanimity needs to be applied to all reactive habits, including unhelpful thoughts. Recognizing that this is just a thought or this is just a sensa tion helps minimize your identification with the experience, making it eas ier to remain equanimous even when it is very intense. Ensure that you relax into the four basic characteristics (mass, motion, temperature, and density) of the intense sensation and let those characteris tics just be. Do your best to feel and accept them unconditionally. If the pain becomes too intense, then have a rest by moving your leg a little or have a small break from the practice and return to it a little later. 166 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE By using intense sensations like pain to develop equanimity, you put the difficult experience to good use. It becomes an opportunity to develop patience and determination, and can lead to rapid changes in daily life. You turn potential suffering into happiness capital, and even pain can become acceptable when you know it s not wasted and may even benefit you in the future. intermediate i have serious pain in my knee when i practice, even though i sitting on a chair, not even cross-leg 1 1.4 book I have problems falling asleep since I started scanning the body. Can I use my practice of mindfulness of breath to fall asleep? I also have strange dreams. Do you think they are related to my practice? Let s talk about your dreams first. Do you recall what we dis cussed about co-emergence? It describes how body sensations and thoughts are interconnected, often triggering one another. This connection makes it easier to understand how your dreams arise, and how it is that they often reflect the emotional tone of what you felt dur ing the day. For example, if you ve been worrying in the back of your mind about getting an injection tomorrow at a medical appointment required for traveling, your body-scanning practice may bring awareness to the body sensations associated with that anxiety. Once you re asleep, if these sensa tions are still present, they can trigger anything stored in memory that involves similar types of sensations, influencing the content of your dreams. The dream itself can take the form of anything stored in your memory any scenario, event, or person that you read about, heard about, or watched on the news or in a movie, at any age that has become associ ated with similar sensations, even if seemingly unrelated to your current anxiety. In the example of being afraid to get an injection, the dream might involve someone wanting to poison you or chasing you down the street with a sword. This reflects how the mind connects bodily sensations with stored memories that involved similar sensations, weaving them into the fabric of your dreams. s important to note, however, that this connection isn t always straight forward. Sometimes, we might encounter very strong experiences in daily life and yet not remember any dream at all, or we might dream about some thing completely unrelated. In general, you can observe a link between what ve been feeling recently and the emotional tone of the dream. Within the context of your practice, sudden changes in dreaming are often signs of memory stimulation or increased awareness of your current emotions, offer ing insight into co-emergence, even during sleep! You also say that it s not been as easy to fall asleep this week. Most people report feeling more relaxed in daily life and having more energy by this stage of the program. This may be contributing to not needing as much sleep this week. There is also the possibility that your increased awareness of sensations when you are relaxing in bed before falling asleep is accompanied by more week 4: applying mindfulness skills in daily life 167 co-emerging thoughts. One thing that often helps to calm your mind and ease into sleep is to use your subtlest possible breath to reduce mental and emotional activity. If you want to give this a go, this is how: (1) Turn your alarm clock away so you re not tempted to check the time. This reduces unnecessary worry. (2) Then, keeping your eyes closed, focus all your atten tion on the small spot below the nostrils above the upper lip, a spot no big ger than the size of a fingertip about 1cm2 . (3) Gradually reduce the volume and depth of your breathing so that you can barely feel the faint touch of your breath on that small spot. Your breath should be so subtle that any less and you wouldn t notice it at all. This results in decreased heart rate and the probability of agitation. When you reach that threshold of awareness, your breathing naturally becomes very shallow, and may occasionally pause on its own. Because you are still focusing on the task and your focus is so narrow, the inhibition pathways in the brain stay engaged, preventing the typical mind-wandering that usually kicks in when your mind is not busy and can keep you awake. intermediate i have problems falling asleep since i started scanning the body. can i use my practice of mindfulne 1 1.4 book I m still not 100% clear about why I have to scan the body. I read that mindfulness is just being in the present moment, so we can live our lives more fully. Why can t I just focus on my breath, like I was doing a week ago? I really liked doing that. It really calmed my mind. It s wonderful that mindfulness of breath has helped calm your mind. This technique is a reliable way to rest your mind when needed and can always serve as a supportive practice. However, mindfulness of breath is just one part of the larger picture in this program. Body scanning adds another dimension to your practice by training inter oceptive awareness your ability to feel and observe sensations throughout your body. For this program to offer you maximum benefit, it s essential to recognize that scanning the body is also scanning through our past, the storehouse of our important memories. Many memories have decayed to the extent that we can only experience them through their corresponding body sensations, without even knowing that what we are currently feeling in the body is the remains of a memory. Most sensations we feel are conditioned by past experiences. By observing these sensations equanimously during scanning, you prevent conditioned reactions, effectively freeing yourself from habitual responses tied to the past. Repeated across the whole body, over time, this process can also help you release unhelpful views and values that potentially limit your well-being and growth. Another critical reason for practicing body scanning is that it sharpens your ability to discern between wholesome and unwholesome thoughts. Recognizing helpful from unhelpful thoughts without feeling their immedi ate consequence in the body would require so much mental analysis that it would be unrealistic to attempt this with every thought that enters the mind. The most practical, and accurate, way of discerning whether a particular 168 STAGE 1 PERSONAL STAGE thought is helpful or unhelpful is to feel its immediate bodily consequences, becoming sensitive to any sensations that co-emerge. This practice allows us to more readily discern our motivations and inten tions, empowering us to change them when they are likely to contribute to suffering. We can use this clarity to develop wisdom, thoughtfully assessing what is important to do or say in certain contexts and recognizing when it is better to refrain. With this discernment, we can choose to be more patient or refrain from performing verbal or physical actions that could be harmful to ourselves or to others. Mindfulness is a prerequisite for wisdom, and alongside wisdom arises com passion. When mindfulness is cultivated as the right mindfulness, as described by the Buddha, and not simply attentiveness devoid of discernment, it reveals the universality of suffering. Rather than seeing suffering as something unique to ourselves or others, we come to understand it as a shared human condition. Consequently, it helps you accept your own and other people s suffering, even when it manifests as unawareness or reactive emotions. Coming back to your original question, if you were to only practice mindfulness of breath, your ability to detect subtle sensations in the body would not be as well developed. This means you might only notice the emergence of intense sensations once they have become too overwhelming to prevent reactivity. However, developing the ability to detect, understand, and let go of these subtler sensations is crucial, because some of them rep resent the early manifestations of emotions. By detecting and observing these subtler sensations with equanimity before they intensify, you can pre vent emotional reactivity from arising in the first place. This is also why ll start to practice more advanced scanning techniques in the coming weeks, including methods like sweeping attention throughout the entire body rapidly. These practices will further enhance your ability to perceive increasingly subtle sensations and recognize early cues of distress. intermediate i m still not 100% clear about why i have to scan the body. i read that mindfulness is just being in 1 1.4 book When you talk about not identifying with sensations or thoughts, I kind of understand what you mean, but I m still confused about who or what I am then. If there s no or then who is speaking to you right now? Isn t that me? If I am not my or my then what am I? This is a bit confusing. You are right, this can be confusing. The idea of egolessness, or not having a fixed sense of self, is often best explored through your own direct experience in meditation practice. However, some explanations can be helpful to clarify what we mean by and why it s important to examine it. The refers to the part of the mind that creates a sense of personal identity. It s not something you need to get rid of, nor is it something you can pinpoint although we have a good idea of which brain areas are involved. The is a sense of self that we construct moment by moment by identifying with what we perceive, think, feel, and do (you may recognize week 4: applying mindfulness skills in daily life 169 these as the four components of the co-emergence model introduced in Week 3: sensory perception, evaluation, body sensation, and reaction). This means that we are constructing and modifying our sense of self all the time, by identifying with our experiences of the body, such as its appear ance, or through attachment to certain tastes, smells, sights, sounds, and tactile sensations. We identify with the thoughts that arise in the mind, including our beliefs and imagined futures, as well as the memories of events that shaped our lives. We identify with emotions through the sensations we feel in the body. We also identify with our actions, including routine behav iors and our ways of reacting. Over time, this ongoing identification builds a story that becomes the narrative of or We also extend this sense of self to include the people, possessions, and roles we value. When we think of something as mine, we also perceive it as being a part of including my family, my culture, my best friends, my career, my football team, my political party, etc. This can lead to strong attachments, as we naturally want to protect and preserve what feels like part of our identity. Problems arise when this constructed sense of self is challenged whether by loss, change, or conflict. Because nothing is permanent, disruptions to this sense of self are inevitable like when we lose a possession that we val ued greatly, or the ending of a relationship, or experiencing an unwanted change in circumstances. It can feel like a part of has been lost, and in extreme cases, people may feel they can t live without specific people, things, or roles they have come to associate with their identity, saying that I can live without him, or If I can t be with her and the kids, I might as well die. Can you see how attachment to an illusory self creates suffering? By iden tifying so closely with what is impermanent, we set ourselves up for disap pointment and distress. Recognizing that our sense of self is something we create and maintain, rather than being something permanent or inherent, can help us approach challenges when they arise with greater equanimity. If this still feels unclear, don t worry. These are complex ideas that become much clearer as you continue to develop your mindfulness practice and dis entangle each thought and each body sensation from your sense of self. For now, it suffices to just observe that they arise and pass in their own right, and therefore cannot be parts of a fixed self. intermediate when you talk about not identifying with sensations or thoughts, i kind of understand what you mean, 1 1.4 book With the Mindfulness based Interoceptive Signature Scale (Figure 4.1) that I m using this week to record those four characteristics, how do I fill it in if there is an issue during my work meeting tomorrow?
