Joseph Campbell Practice Background and Rationale In Stage 2 of Mindfulness-integrated Cognitive Behavior Therapy (MiCBT), ll focus on addressing avoidant behaviors that limit your ability to act with confidence and purpose. This week, we begin by exploring how habit ual avoidance develops, its impact on your well-being and relationships, and the rationale for the exposure tasks that we ll be starting this week. By approaching challenging situations with equanimity, you ll start to reverse avoidant patterns and build confidence and resilience toward pursuing meaningful goals. Decreasing Avoidance and Freeing Yourself to Welcome to Stage 2 of MiCBT, where you ll be applying the skills devel oped over the last 4weeks to specific situations that you typically avoid. Whereas Stage 1 focused on exposing yourself to the internal dimension of your experience, Stage 2 will support you in applying equanimity while you safely and confidently confront external situations that you habitually avoid. This will allow you to encounter body sensations that you wouldn otherwise have the opportunity to experience in meditation, offering a Week 5: Regulating Behavior Overcome Avoidance 176 STAGE 2 EXPOSURE STAGE valuable and convenient way of developing greater levels of equanimity. This exposure not only helps with reducing emotional reactivity but also builds self-confidence. Avoidance behaviors are rampant in daily life. For instance, people might rely on alcohol to avoid feeling awkward in social settings, procrastinate to escape the risk of making mistakes, stay in unhealthy relationships to avoid insecurity, lie to avoid embarrassment or conflict, overspend on personal appearance to avoid criticism and rejection, or withdraw socially to avoid difficult conversations. This is far from an exhaustive list, but it highlights how pervasive avoidant behaviors can be and how they shape our lives. Take a moment to reflect: What are some of your own avoidant behav iors? These behaviors often stem from avoiding discomfort rather than aligning with healthy values. In Stage 2, you ll be applying your mindfulness skills to daily situations that you tend to avoid to your detriment or that of others. Within a couple of weeks, situations you ve avoided for years will feel easier to address, encouraging you to take healthy risks rather than let ting fear dictate your choices. The Nature of Avoidance Avoidance is neither inherently good nor bad it depends on the context. Sometimes it s adaptive, steering us away from genuine danger, pain, or fail ure. However, it becomes problematic when it s maladaptive and signifi cantly limits our lives. For example, it may be necessary and adaptive to work overtime for a while, but if it becomes a habitual way to avoid spend ing time with family or housemates, it could eventually disrupt relationships and well-being. Similarly, it may be necessary once in a while to decline a social invitation from friends. We may have a genuine need for rest! For example, when exhausted from single parenting, wanting a rest on the weekend does not constitute maladaptive avoidance. But what if regularly avoiding social inter actions was due to a fear of being judged? Over time, our social network would shrink, leading to loneliness and reinforcing feelings of inadequacy. Avoidance is rooted in basic survival mechanisms: we seek pleasant expe riences and avoid unpleasant ones. For instance, one person may avoid pub lic speaking, while another seeks it out for the thrill of appearing in public! It all comes back to how the mind evaluates the situation, what meaning it gives to it. Reflecting on the example of public speaking, the first person may associate it with a fear of criticism and subsequent rejection, whereas the second expects praise and subsequent acceptance. Avoidance can range from mild (e.g., avoiding chores) to severely impair ing, such as fear of leaving the house (in agoraphobia), sharing painful expe riences with a trusted therapist (in complex trauma), eating specific foods (in eating disorders), or confronting distressing thoughts (in OCD). Avoidant behavior can sometimes feel reassuring. Take, for example, someone who always keeps their pantry full out of fear of scarcity. Though week 5: regulating behavior to overcome avoidance 177 comforting in the short term, this behavior can lead to overstocking and waste. In the same way, we might justify avoiding social situations by telling ourselves we simply enjoy our own company, masking deeper fears of embarrassment or rejection. What Are We Really Avoiding? s take a closer look at anxiety. When you avoid situations where you might feel stressed or anxious, what are you truly avoiding? It may be help ful to revisit the co-emergence model of reinforcement, as it is highly rele vant here. Every time we anticipate that we might feel anxious in a particular situa tion, even just the thought of it can elicit co-emerging unpleasant sensations in the body. If we don t recognize this pattern, we might mistakenly assume that the situation itself is the cause of our anxiety, leading us to avoid it entirely. The immediate relief we feel from avoiding the situation reinforces the belief that avoidance is helpful. This belief is strengthened each time we avoid the situation again, making it less obvious that what we are actually avoiding are the unpleasant body sensations that arise with anxiety not the situation itself. Understanding Values in MiCBT Sometimes people believe they are not avoiding anything, only to discover that they are sidestepping key values in their lives. In MiCBT, values are understood as the guiding principles that help orient our actions toward what truly matters to us, so long as they contribute to our own or someone else reduction of suffering or promotion of happiness. Examples of wholesome values include acceptance, accountability, competition, courage, duty, fitness, generosity, gratitude, perseverance, self-reliance, and trust. Recognizing the importance of values is not unique to MiCBT. For example, in Acceptance and Commitment Therapy, identifying key values and following valued directions is an essential aspect of the treatment approach [1, 2]. Values serve as personal guides, and although they represent deeply held principles or desired directions in life, they may sometimes become entangled with avoidance, insecurity, or the need to fulfill certain fears or unspoken social expectations. In these cases, even constructive values like independence, kindness, or discipline might lead to actions that ultimately hinder our personal fulfillment. For instance, someone might value independence. However, if valuing independence is driven by a fear of intimacy, vulnerability, or rejection, pur suing this same value may be used to justify avoidance of meaningful rela tionships, leading to isolation and loneliness. Similarly, someone may deeply value physical fitness or achievement, but if exercising masks a fear of inad equacy or rejection, it might lead to compulsive behaviors that detract from overall well-being. Note that the value itself is not inherently flawed; instead, the issue lies in how it is used as a shield against deeper insecurities. 178 STAGE 2 EXPOSURE STAGE Sometimes, avoidance can hijack values to defend against perceived threats, leading to patterns that hinder personal growth. Bruno recalls one of his clients who was referred for severe chronic depression and anxiety that she had been experiencing for the previous 42years: After about 40years of various psychiatric treatments, from a long string of antidepressants to several courses of electroconvulsive therapy, the softly spoken 80-year-old knocked at my door, pushed by her last hope. She had buried her need for learning, art and joyful activities the day she became farmer s wife, as she put it. Despite the obvious success of the farm, she had traded-off her strong inter est in study at university and the pursuit of a scientific or academic career. Playing her piano became a fantasy. Even sharing her opinion about the activities and problem-solving strategies at the farm had to be tempered to prevent the risk of being ignored or ridiculed. She learned to avoid painful situations, one after another, from small issues to more fundamental ones, to the extent that life seemed purposeless and her sense of self-worth shrank after each criticism she received. She had lost her voice and progressively set aside much of what she valued, just because That s what we had to do in my situation, she said. Her ensuing grief seemed unshakeable and no antidepressant was going to do the trick. The cause of her established unhappiness had never been addressed until she started facing her avoidance and related emotions through MiCBT. As she started the program, she gained a better understanding of verbal abuse and available external supports. Fortunately, things began to change. As she also discovered that her verbally abusing husband had a mental health condi tion that explained his behavior, she reduced her habit of taking his comments personally and began to stand up for herself. Her confidence improved and avoidance was not to remain her co-dependent companion. Her elderly neigh bors can now enjoy the soft sound of her old piano, the modest notes breaking the lonely silence of her small retirement village apartment. Those whom she befriends are often rewarded by her warmth, compassion, and joy. Thus, choosing carefully what valued direction to follow requires a careful and sincere interpretation of our behavior. It requires mindfulness, which is why Stage 2 is taught after Stage 1, once we are skilled at looking inward. In summary, Week 5 marks a pivotal stage in MiCBT, where the practice of facing everyday avoidances becomes a foundation for deeper, value driven change. Applying equanimity to minor tasks we usually avoid may seem simple, yet it builds resilience against habitual avoidance and instills a greater sense of self-confidence. Overcoming these day-to-day discomforts allows us to more consistently live in alignment with our values. Each time you consciously face a discomfort with equanimity, you re increasing your capacity to engage with life on your own terms. This commitment to engage, rather than avoid, grows over time, and you ll no longer be bound by week 5: regulating behavior to overcome avoidance 179 patterns of avoidance that previously held you back from pursuing mean ingful goals. Instead, you can move forward with confidence, cultivating life that genuinely reflects wholesome values that reduce suffering and increase life satisfaction. Training Mindfulness Skills Checking Your Readiness Congratulations on completing your second week of practicing unilateral body scanning in silence and in strong determination. How did you find the experience this week? Were you able to keep your commitment to practice 30minutes twice daily? If so, did the more intense practice with complete immobility bring noticeable benefits? You may have noticed that thoughts tend to intrude more when you practice in silence were you able to let go of them and return your focus to bodily sensations fairly quickly? Could you feel sensations in most parts of the body, and could you reduce reactiv ity to sensations or the lack of them? With regular practice and the correct understanding, you ve likely begun to notice progress. Maybe you re letting go of intrusive thoughts more quickly than last week, feeling more sensations across the body, and even experiencing reduced reactivity in daily life. If these benefits haven t emerged yet, perhaps related to difficulties in maintaining consistent practice, be assured that it s simply a matter of time. Recommitting to the necessary frequency, duration, and accuracy of practice will allow you to experience the beneficial effects of your practice in daily life. When you reflect on your experience with the Mindfulness-based Interoceptive Exposure Task (MIET), were you able to observe and record the four characteristics of sensations during stressful situations? Has your capacity to let go of distress within 30 seconds improved? Feeling these characteristics with equanimity a bit-like tuning into the elements of nature within the body provides a solid foundation for this week advanced exposure skills. Looking at your recordings of the MIET from this last week, has the After Equanimity (dotted line) moved progressively closer to the neutral point on each scale over the course of the week? This shift toward neutral typically reflects increased equanimity. You can also quantify equanimity another way: look at the Intensity Before and the Intensity After scales at the bottom of each Mindfulness based Interoceptive Signature Scale (MISS). If there is an increasing difference between the two scores, then you are increasingly able to recover from stress within 30seconds. The best way of quantifying equanimity with this form is to combine both patterns of change and derive an overall impression. 180 STAGE 2 EXPOSURE STAGE When to Proceed to the Next Step re ready to move forward if you can sense approximately 80% of your body, even if some thoughts still arise or certain areas remain difficult to feel. At this stage, you can begin symmetrical body scanning with the audio instructions. If You Need More Time If you ve been practicing consistently but still find it challenging to feel sensations throughout, consider continuing last week s method for a few more days. Inconsistent practice isn t a problem unless you let it discourage recommit with purpose and begin again, focusing on the same prac tice for a bit longer. Approach this with a clear resolution to prioritize your well-being and personal growth, recognizing that the benefits ripple out others as well. Postpone reading ahead until your practice catches up; aligning your reading with your progress will enhance clarity and deepen your understanding of each exercise. Symmetrical Scanning The Practice This week, you ll transition to symmetrical scanning, a method that involves attending to both sides of the body simultaneously as you scan. Begin at the top of the head, still scanning downward part-by-part in 2 3in. (5 8cm) diameter areas at a time, but now focusing on both the left and right sides of the body at the same time. For example, instead of scanning one cheek and then the other, direct your attention to both cheeks simultaneously, moving symmetrically through the body. Once you reach the tips of the toes, reverse direction and scan symmetrically back to the top of the head, main taining equanimity even if you cannot feel both sides equally. It may be tempting to visualize a line running down the center of the body, separating the left from the right. However, as emphasized in an earlier chapter, mindfulness involves attending to the reality of the present moment as it is rather than creating visual or mental imagery. It s enough to know roughly where the middle of the body is, from the top of the head to the groin, without actively imagining it. This keeps the practice focused on directly experiencing sensations as they arise. At first, you may notice your attention moving in a zig-zag pattern, where you become aware of one side slightly before the other. As you con tinue to practice over the course of the week, this will diminish, and you find it easier to scan both sides in unison. Skills You Will Learn Advanced scanning methods have been taught in the Burmese Vipassana tradition for a very long time. These techniques train the mind to detect very subtle changes in the body and cultivate deep levels of awareness. With a week 5: regulating behavior to overcome avoidance 181 greater ability to feel subtle sensations, practitioners can sometimes experi ence levels of blissful states that are well-documented in traditional teach ings. However, while such experiences may occur, they are not the purpose of including advanced body scanning in the MiCBT program. Very pleasant experiences have the potential to trigger much attachment and craving, which can become counterproductive and detract from the practice s signifi cantly broader goals. In MiCBT, the primary aim of advanced scanning methods is to become very effective at detecting subtle cues of distress early in the emotional sequence, when they are still manageable. By noticing these sensations before they intensify and evolve into emotions, you gain the opportunity to respond consciously rather than being swept away by escalating frustration, anger, or anxiety. You might also begin to notice that the same mental state such as calmness appears in multiple life contexts, whether in medi tation or during dinner with friends! Consider Adam, a middle-aged man who sought help a few months ago for severe anxiety and low self-worth. Underneath his drive to overachieve lay a belief that he was failing unless he constantly exceeded his own limits. This craving for unrealistic goals even manifested in his driving, where he found himself consumed by road rage if he couldn t be the fastest on the road. Adam discovered a surprising parallel in his meditation practice. On days when he rushed through body scanning skipping any areas he couldn immediately feel he noticed that his driving was similarly impulsive and aggressive. Conversely, when his practice was calm and deliberate, he found himself driving with greater composure. As Adam s practice deepened, he became increasingly aware of the underlying mental states that shaped his reactivity or equanimous composure. He realized that his inner experiences and external actions were not driven by separate mechanisms, but instead represented the same underlying processes. As discussed in Week 4, research supports these experiences, showing that daily body scanning produces neuroplasticity in key areas of the brain, such as the somatosensory and insular cortices, which are essential for processing bodily sensations and emotions [3, 4]. Through consistent practice, neurons in these areas form stronger connections, improving your ability to detect subtle sensations across the body even outside of formal practice. This heightened awareness supports adaptive responses in daily life, as we will explore in detail below. With symmetrical scanning, attention passes through each part of the body more frequently during a 30-minute session than with unilateral scan ning of the same duration. This repetition provides more opportunities for desensitization. As you consistently bring equanimity to each area of sensation offering an equal interest to sensations, whether they are pleas ant, unpleasant, or neutral your nervous system gradually reduces the intensity and frequency of learned reactivity. Repeated attention to areas 182 STAGE 2 EXPOSURE STAGE where sensations initially seem faint or unclear also strengthens neural connections in those regions, fostering neuroplasticity. Over time, this process enhances your ability to perceive previously vague sensations more clearly. Symmetrical scanning also lays the groundwork for the next stage of practice, partial sweeping, which you ll begin next week. By developing skill of perceiving both sides of the body simultaneously, you are preparing for the rapid and detailed perception of sensations that partial sweeping entails. Integrating Skills in Daily Life Magnifying Equanimity through Exposure This step marks a significant milestone in your MiCBT journey, where you begin applying mindfulness skills to challenging situations in daily life. Until now, your focus has been on cultivating mindfulness within yourself. You used these skills to regulate your thinking processes and emotions. Now it time to apply them to regulating your behavior. Meditation, no matter how well-practiced, is not a cure-all. It won resolve every problem, even for those who devote their lives to it as nuns or monks. The true value of mindfulness lies in applying its skills in everyday life. This is how it fulfills its purpose: reducing suffering, fostering well being, and supporting personal growth. The exercises below will help you harness the stability you ve been devel oping through mindfulness to face situations that you might typically avoid. By doing so, mindfulness and equanimity become tools you can depend on during moments of discomfort or stress particularly when avoidance is not an option. In essence, this is about transforming avoidance into an opportu nity to further cultivate your equanimity! Identifying and Ranking Avoided Situations To skillfully address ingrained avoidant habits, it s essential to reflect non judgmentally on areas of your life where avoidance may be holding you back and where greater confidence could make a difference. Over time, these habits can become deeply familiar coping mechanisms, subtly shaping your daily choices and affecting your well-being and growth. When avoid ance becomes entrenched, it s easy to lose sight of your genuine needs and goals. Take a moment to ask yourself sincerely: What might I be neglecting to my detriment or to that of others? Imagine what it could feel like to direct your life according to what truly nourishes your well-being and sense of purpose! What situations or activities do you find yourself repeatedly steering away from? Begin by brainstorming 10 12 scenarios that typically trigger week 5: regulating behavior to overcome avoidance 183 distress, and assign each a percentage of discomfort you d expect if you faced it today. Use a scale where 0% represents no distress at all, and 100% represents the highest level of distress. To help you rate situations more accurately, refer to the Subjective Units of Distress (SUDS) Rating Guide in Table 5.1 below. If social anxiety leads you to avoid family gatherings, you might note: Attending family events: 60%. At this stage, we re just brain storming, so write down all the items that come to mind; you ll select spe cific ones later. Here s an example of brainstormed situations, each with a distress rating: Starting a conversation with a stranger: 50%. Scheduling a medical appointment: 45%. Driving on a busy highway: 60%. Giving a presentation at work: 85%. Submitting a creative project for feedback: 55%. Talking to a family member about a past disagreement: 95%. Reaching out to an old friend after losing touch: 65%. Being in a crowded place: 90%. Not snacking in the evening: 75%. Table 5.1 Subjective units of distress (SUDS) rating guide. Level Description 0 Calm and at ease; feeling completely comfortable. 10 Mild trace of unease without desire to withdraw from the situation. 20 Mild discomfort or tension with a desire to withdraw from the situation. 30 Noticeable anxious thoughts, yet able to resist avoidance. 40 Moderate worry or distraction, but able to remain engaged. 50 Markedly unsettled, with a strong urge to retreat or escape. 60 Substantial anxiety with rising emotional and physical tension. 70 High anxiety with decreased ability to think clearly and manage responses. 80 Strong panic with disrupted focus and physical sensations of distress. 90 Intense emotional overload, accompanied by feelings of helplessness and inability to cope. 100 Extreme distress that severely impairs functioning; the worst you can imagine feeling. 184 STAGE 2 EXPOSURE STAGE Trying a new hobby or activity on my own: 35%. Declining an invitation to a social event: 40%. Sharing a personal opinion in a group discussion: 25%. Note that some of these situations include not doing things, such as refraining from eating at a certain time of day or not attending a social event. As you can see, sometimes we avoid not doing things! How can avoid ance manifest in these ways? Consider the example of not snacking in the evening: in this case, eating may serve as a distraction from unpleasant sen sations like tiredness. Similarly, saying to attending a social event might be a way of avoiding the unpleasant sensations that arise when setting a boundary, such as feelings of guilt or fear of rejection. The next step is to select five situations from your list that reflect an incre mental range of distress and are particularly important for you to address. Ensure that these five situations are ones you can realistically encounter over the next 3weeks. For instance, if you have a fear of flying but no flights planned within this timeframe, it s best to choose another scenario with a comparable level of distress that you can address now. Begin with a situation that causes about 25 30% distress. This level of discomfort is ideal s enough to trigger the co-emerging sensations necessary for effective exposure, but not so overwhelming as to feel unmanageable. Avoid situations that cause very low distress, such as 15%, as these may not generate sufficiently strong co-emerging sensations to effectively challenge avoidance patterns or foster meaningful skill development. Starting with manageable scenarios allows you to build your skills gradu ally and effectively. To structure your list, aim for a range of distress levels, progressing incrementally from less challenging to more challenging situa tions. While your choice of situations is flexible, try to avoid selecting mul tiple situations with the same level of distress. Here s a general guideline for organizing your list: Situation 1: Expected to cause between 20% and 35% distress. Situation 2: Between 30% and 50% distress. Situation 3: Between 50% and 70% distress. Situation 4: Between 70% and 80% distress. Situation 5: Between 80% and 100% distress. Before filling out Table 5.2 or entering the information in Stage 2.1 of the MiCBT Guide app, take a moment to review the example in Table 5.3 for guidance on how to structure your entries. Record your five chosen situa tions, along with today s date and how distressing each would feel to do today. It is best to record and rate all situations on the same day, as this provides a clear baseline for measuring your progress on Days 7 and 14, when you ll be re-rating these five situations again. week 5: regulating behavior to overcome avoidance 185 Equanimity During Exposure Over the next 2weeks (or longer if necessary), you ll be exposing yourself to the five situations you ve listed, while feeling the co-emerging sensations with equanimity. We ll be taking a structured approach, starting with imagi nal exposure visualizing these challenging scenarios before approaching them in real life. This method offers several key advantages: it provides a safe environment to explore the co-emerging sensations, allows you to remain in control and adjust the intensity as needed, and helps you build confidence incrementally. ll be using the bipolar exposure method to help you practice observ ing and feeling co-emerging sensations with equanimity. Remember that it the sensations we fear and avoid, not the situations themselves! After prac ticing bipolar exposure four times over the course of 2days, you ll transition to applying these skills in real-life exposures, equipped with greater confi dence and equanimity. The Bipolar Exposure Method The bipolar exposure method is a unique MiCBT technique that is unrelated to bipolar disorder. This approach helps you prepare to face avoided situa tions in real life by using guided visualization to encounter them first in your mind. By imagining both the worst-case and best-case scenarios of a feared situation, you can observe the bodily sensations that co-emerge without reacting to them. This dual exposure is designed to neutralize both aversion and craving, fostering a balanced and calm approach to real-life challenges. Exposure to a feared situation in imagery while being equanimous increases our sense of self-efficacy, which provides the confidence we need to over come avoidance and face the situation in real life. Table 5.2 Subjective Units of Distress (SUDS) form. Day 1 Day 7 Day 14 Situation or activity Date Distress rating Date Distress rating Date Distress rating Table 5.3 Subjective Units of Distress (SUDS) form with examples of distressing situations. Day 1 Day 7 Day 14 Situation or activity Date Distress rating Date Distress rating Date Distress ratingGoing to the grocery store Nov 19th 25%Going out with friends (rather than making excuses) Nov 19th 35%Taking the elevator when there are strangers in it Nov 19th 50%Making small talk with colleagues during lunch Nov 19th 70%Being close to large dogs Nov 19th 90% week 5: regulating behavior to overcome avoidance 187 Bipolar exposure is structured as a three-step exercise lasting 11 13min utes, practiced immediately after your 30-minute mindfulness meditation session. Figure 5.1 outlines the process. Here s how it works: 1 Visualizing worst-case scenarios (first 5 minutes) Begin with the first situation on your list. For the first 5 minutes, imag ine the worst-case scenarios that could occur if you were to encounter this situation in real life 2days from now. Keeping your eyes closed will help you feel sensations more clearly. While visualizing these scenarios, notice the co-emerging sensations in your body, such as constriction or heat, without reacting to them. Do your best to remain equanimous, not identifying with them, and instead, perceive them for what they are: just sensations in the body composed of mass, motion, temperature, and density. Allow them to arise and pass naturally, reminding yourself that they are transient. Note that since you have already learned to prevent reactions toward body sensations during your meditation, reactivity to a specific worst case scenario might take much less than 5minutes to neutralize, and you will need to imagine several additional worst-case scenarios during the 5-minute exposure in order to continue to generate co-emerging sensa tions. Over the 5minutes, the habitual tendency to avoid the sensations is being progressively desensitized by your equanimous attitude replacing the usual avoidance with accepting the sensation as it is. 2 Calming the mind (1 minutes) After visualizing worst-case scenarios for 5minutes, keep your eyes closed and shift your focus to the breath at the entrance of the nostrils. Practice mindfulness of breath for at least 1 minute, allowing your mind to settle. If the situation you visualized was particularly stressful, extend this step to 2 or 3minutes as needed, especially for more challenging situations in the weeks ahead. 5 minutes visualization of worst-case scenario 5 minutes visualization of best-case scenario Mindfulness breath Apply equanimity throughout the exercise 1 minutes rest Figure 5.1 Representation of the bipolar exposure procedure. Cayoun (2011)/ 188 STAGE 2 EXPOSURE STAGE 3 Visualizing best-case scenarios (last 5minutes) Next, visualize the best-case scenarios related to the same situation. Picture positive outcomes vividly and observe any pleasant co-emerging sensations, such as warmth or lightness. Remain equanimous by observ ing these sensations without clinging to them or becoming attached to the I, me, mine narrative. Focus on the four basic characteristics of sensations mass, motion, temperature, and density bringing equal interest to each. If a single best-case scenario takes less than 5 minutes to desensitize to, imagine additional scenarios to complete this step. Keep in mind that unpleasant sensations often arise more readily than pleasant ones, as our brains are wired to prioritize survival. If you find it challenging to sense pleasant sensations during this practice, keep practicing patiently. It might take one or two practices before you are able to feel pleasant sen sations with the best-case scenarios. You might have noticed that it s much easier in daily life to switch from feeling good to feeling bad than to switch from feeling bad to feeling good. This applies to our experience during bipolar exposure, and it should not alarm you when you switch from the unpleasant scenarios to the pleasant ones. Your ability to make this transition will improve with training. Switching focus in this way develops cognitive flexibility, which is a benefi cial skill in itself. You may wonder why we shouldn t indulge in the pleasant sensations that arise during the best-case scenarios. The answer lies in an insight rooted in Buddhist psychology: reacting with attachment to pleasant sensations rein forces craving a fundamental source of suffering. Craving fosters depend ency on achieving the best-case scenario, creating expectations for it to materialize in real life. When reality doesn t meet these expectations or falls short of the ideal, disappointment and dissatisfaction inevitably arise. By practicing equanimity, we can engage with and fully feel pleasant sensations as they occur, without clinging to them. This approach weakens the cycle of craving, cultivating a more balanced and liberated response to whatever unfolds. Integrating this understanding into daily life can lead to a more peaceful life. Bipolar Exposure Versus Standard Exposure Therapy If you have been in therapy in the past and were asked to undertake stand ard exposure therapy, you may have had to stay exposed well past the peak of the emotion before your fear response could be extinguished, which can often take up to 30 minutes. During this typical prolonged exposure, the focus is usually on the external object of avoidance (as in phobias) or on emotional experience (as in trauma memories). This gives plenty of opportunity for identifying with the experience and reacting with distressing emotions during the exposure. This complicates and delays the expected week 5: regulating behavior to overcome avoidance 189 results, and the dropout rate can reach up to 40% for individuals treated for post-traumatic stress disorder [5]. In contrast, bipolar exposure uses 5 minutes of interoceptive desensitiza tion per scenario type, twice daily over the next few days, allowing a shorter and gentler approach that preserves a sense of safety as you begin your exposure. The focus is uniquely on body sensations that compose emotions while not identifying with them and accepting them as impermanent phe nomena. This method of exposure is based on the co-emergence model of reinforcement [6] that we discussed in Week 3, which explains how and why reinforcement occurs at the level of body sensations. Since anxiety limits our ability to notice how this is taking place in the body [7], increasing our abil ity to feel body sensations during emotional experiences while being equani mous during exposure helps us address avoidance of the fear experience at its root. As we will discuss in some detail next week, bipolar exposure ena bles us to desensitize the fear signature in the body, rather than limiting desensitization to a particular avoided situation, which results in a progres sive reduction of reactivity to fear experiences in general. Nonetheless, research also shows that interoceptive desensitization alone can be insufficient for some individuals and for treating some mental health symptoms [8]. In line with this observation, combining interoceptive and exteroceptive desensitization is the most thorough and effective way of reducing avoidance. This means that being exposed to avoided situations in real life is also necessary. As explained below, the same desensitization of the fear signature in the body continues during real-life exposure to the avoided situation. Hence, following bipolar exposure, you also need to practice vivo exposure to ensure that desensitization occurs in the relevant con text, and to get a genuine boost of confidence. The In Vivo Exposure Method After completing four sessions of the bipolar exposure exercise tonight, tomorrow morning, tomorrow evening, and the following morning be ready for what is known as vivo exposure. In vivo is the Latin word for in the living, meaning in a real-life situation. In addition to every 30-minute body-scanning practice you have done so far, the four bipolar exposures have prepared your nervous system to remain equanimous toward sensations associated with fearful expectations, and there is no need to con tinue practicing it with this particular situation. Your practice of bipolar exposure will have further trained inhibition pathways in your prefrontal cortex that you can activate using equanimity, and they will serve you well when you face actual situations. You are ready to deliberately approach your first scenario in real life. When engaging with Situation 1, s important that you remain mindful of the most obvious co-emerging sensations while remaining equanimous with them. Be willing to feel them as they are, however pleasant or unpleasant they may be! As you learned in the previous 2weeks, the 190 STAGE 2 EXPOSURE STAGE sensations are not and are not yours. Observe them without judg ment, acknowledging them as transient phenomena, arising to just pass away. Unlike with bipolar exposure, the time for in vivo exposure is not limited to 5 minutes the longer the better. Now that the fear has been partly desensitized with bipolar exposure, in vivo exposure is not likely to be as daunting as it was previously. Just like with bipolar exposure, it is beneficial to repeat in vivo exposures several times, ideally until any associated distress is equal to or less than about 5%. You may find that while some sensations remain intense, any associated distress has decreased and no longer prevents you from facing the situation. This is where your confidence begins to improve noticeably. Continue to immerse yourself in Situation 1 as often as possible. For instance, if Situation 1 on your form is avoiding the grocery store, make a point to visit it regularly while maintaining awareness of body sensations with equanimity, until it s no longer an issue. Similarly, if you ve been avoid ing a certain colleague or neighbor in situations where no immediate con flict exists (interpersonal conflicts will be addressed in Week 7), do your best to say a few words to them each morning. Remember that the most impor tant part of this training is maintaining equanimity toward body sensations throughout these interactions, until meeting this colleague or neighbor no longer triggers reactivity. Referring back to our description of the co-emergence model in Week this mindfulness-based in vivo exposure helps establish an equilibrium state in your nervous system in situations where you would usually experience anxiety and the disequilibrium state it creates. Through this kind of expo sure, you not only build confidence but also cultivate a deeper equanimity that is typically accessible only through intensive mindfulness retreats within the Vipassana approach utilized in MiCBT. Scheduling Bipolar and In Vivo Exposures This Week After completing bipolar exposure to Situation 1 four times over 2days and beginning in vivo exposure, proceed to Situation 2. Start bipolar exposure to Situation 2 after each meditation practice for the following 2days, then transition to in vivo exposure, which will be 4 or 5days from now. For instance, if your Situation 2 involves a fear of socializing with friends, like in the example on Table 5.3, you would begin bipolar exposure for four sessions, starting two evenings from now, after having completed bipolar exposures for Situation 1. At the same time, you would be starting in vivo exposure to Situation 1 daily or as often as possible. This staggered approach allows you to prepare for the next situation through imagery while actively addressing previous ones in real-life settings, fostering rapid progress and sustained motivation. Continue this pattern until you ve addressed all five situations on your list. While completing these exposures within 2 weeks is possible, logistical constraints may occasionally require adjustments. For instance, if Situation 3 week 5: regulating behavior to overcome avoidance 191 involves riding an elevator but you lack access to one and no comparable substitute with a similar distress level is available you can temporarily skip to Situation 4 and return to Situation 3 when feasible. These adjustments may extend Stage 2 of MiCBT slightly, but they are part of the process. Take the time you need while maintaining momentum to maximize the benefits of the exposure practice. The Benefits of Exposure with Equanimity By desensitizing our reactivity to co-emerging sensations using bipolar exposure, we cultivate equanimity toward sensations that we typically avoid. It may become increasingly apparent that our avoidance of external situations is largely due to the body sensations associated with them, rather than the situations themselves. Can you see how this relates to the way we process information, explained through the co-emergence model of rein forcement discussed in Week 3? If you re not sure, it could be helpful to return to it in the book, as it helps understanding how our behavior takes place and is maintained over time. The explanation of schemas in Week 3 is also highly relevant to under standing avoidant behavior. As you may recall, maladaptive schemas are deeply ingrained, unhelpful core beliefs often formed during childhood. These schemas are experienced emotionally when activated later in life, often operating subconsciously, unless the distress is overwhelming. We tend to avoid situations that trigger our most painful schemas. For example, children who are discouraged from freely expressing their opinions and emotions may develop a belief that they need to please others, seek approval, or suppress their needs to feel worthy. This can result in avoid ant behaviors in adulthood, such as prioritizing others needs over their own or feeling guilty about focusing on their personal goals. This can be illus trated by one of our recent clients, a very amiable individual who was very kind and eager to please, struggling to give himself permission to spend money renovating the house because it seemed selfish to him. On his list of avoided situations, he included researching renovation costs and planning the project with my partner (50% distress) and informing my parents and siblings about the renovation plans (85% distress). Not surprisingly, his partner was immensely grateful when he eventually overcame his need for approval and his fear of disappointing others, particularly authority figures. Similarly, children who are not encouraged to develop a sense of auton omy and competence may grow up relying heavily on others to complete tasks, believing they are incapable of achieving their goals independently. This can lead to feelings of inadequacy and feeling like an imposter even with successful achievements. One client with this schema included going out shopping by myself (45% distress) and leaving my job where I am taken advantage of (90% distress). When we do exposure to these situations while remaining equanimous to the body sensations associated with these beliefs, the belief diminishes. 192 STAGE 2 EXPOSURE STAGE the sensations become less intense, the beliefs lose their hold, making it easier to respond consciously rather than reactively. Once we recognize that we are primarily avoiding the bodily consequences of our beliefs, there is little stopping us from facing any uncomfortable situation with growing confidence. Trust in your ability to make this effort, as it will strengthen your capacity to handle challenges across all areas of life. You can finally outgrow child hood hurts and misunderstandings you can begin to offer yourself free dom from the past. Within the framework of this program, this newfound confidence will also prepare you for the next step, Stage 3, which we will discuss in 2weeks. Gaining these insights by completing desensitization to the five situations on your list is an essential part of your progress. This Week s Practice Symmetrical Body Scanning For this scanning method, pass your attention on both sides of the midline of the body, as symmetrically as possible. You will need to use audio instruc tions. However, since symmetrical scanning is a more advanced scanning method and you now have sufficient understanding of how body scanning is practiced, the instructions are shorter about 13 minutes leaving you more than half the 30-minute session to practice in silence. Aim to complete at least three body cycles (down and up) per 30-minute session by the end of the week, but not much more than five. As you have done previously, continue to make an effort to prevent clinging to thoughts. Also continue to meditate in absolute immobility and with consistent effort to accept your experiences just as they are, unconditionally. Frequently Asked Questions intermediate do i have to record all my emotions with the miss this week? my god you have no idea i will need 50 1 1.4 book How fast should I scan my body with symmetrical scanning? Move at your own pace. When you encounter blank areas in the body places where sensations aren t noticeable t interrupt your flow. Continue scanning through the whole body symmetrically to complete the cycle you started. Afterward, return to each blank spot one at a time, focus ing equanimously for about 30 60seconds on each area. If a sensation arises during this time, observe it equanimously, noticing how even blank spots can change, and then shift attention to the next blank spot and do the same. If no change occurs, don t be disappointed or you will lose your equanim ity. Instead, accept that this is not the time for it to change. Not getting the result you expect in meditation while staying equanimous will be of great benefit when you can t get what you want in daily life; you will be able to remain calm and equanimous. intermediate how fast should i scan my body with symmetrical scanning? 2 2.1 book I can t feel some body parts symmetrically. What should I do? That s very common. Don t expect to feel both sides perfectly symmetrically for the first few days. There will be a lead between sides when scanning certain areas of the body, causing your attention to quickly shift back and forth from one side to the other very rapidly to catch up. This happens because sensations are easier to feel symmetrically in some areas of the body and more difficult in others. This is to be expected. As you maintain your commitment to practice for 30minutes twice daily, it will only take a few days before new and stronger neural connections are made in the relevant brain areas and you are able to feel the whole body symmetri cally, without having to flick your attention quickly from side to side. Remaining immobile and equanimous will help. intermediate i can t feel some body parts symmetrically. what should i do? 2 2.1 book I avoid many things due to my OCD, but anything dirty, like dusty things, is my biggest problem. Sometimes, the language we use can shape how we view our situation. If you say to yourself that the cause of your three-year workers compensation claim is that people were rude to you and took advantage of you for so long, then the problem doesn t seem to have anything to do with you. It can even seem like the problem lies entirely outside of your control. At the same time, we can be curious about why you waited until the situation became unbearable before addressing it. Could it be that you re avoid ing taking healthy risks? Avoidant behavior isn t just about the things we t do, it can also be reflected in the choices we make. If you re struggling to identify situations that you avoid, consider asking someone close to you, like a partner, sibling, or friend. They might offer insights into your behaviors or decision-making that you hadn t recognized as avoidant. intermediate i really can t think of anything that i avoid. my problem is with my boss and the other staff who su 2 2.1 book I wonder why we should not allow ourselves to enjoy the pleas ant sensations produced by best-case scenarios in the last 5 minutes of the bipolar exposure. Enjoying pleasant situations as they arise in daily life is very important for our well-being, and we should enjoy pleasant life experi ences! Your training teaches you to remain equanimous during meditation or bipolar exposure while feeling sensations, with a genuine willingness to experience them as they are, whether pleasant, unpleasant, or neutral. It not about numbing our experience. Attachment is the problem! Here s how it works. When you enjoy a pleas ant sensation, over time, you soon develop a preference for it over other ones that s how memory of learning works. This can lead to becoming week 5: regulating behavior to overcome avoidance 199 attached to feeling pleasant experiences and disappointment when they not present. If you let the habit of attaching to pleasant sensations occur during bipolar exposure, you re likely to have this in mind when you exposed to the unpleasant situation in real life. Consciously or subcon sciously, you might hope or even expect that the experience will be easy or pleasant, or at least not as unpleasant. The bipolar exposure technique does not teach you to develop preferences. It teaches you the skill of not being so affected by either unpleasant or pleasant experiences, so that whatever the result of your in vivo exposure, you won t be disappointed. It teaches you equanimity in real life without being detached from life. However, this can be a little confusing when the underlying rationale is not fully clear, so let s have a closer look at it. If the exercise was for the purpose of just overcoming fears that are specific to what you avoid, then it would be technically appropriate and beneficial to enjoy pleasant sensations in the body. But the bipolar exposure method in MiCBT seeks to go far beyond this limited positive outcome. It is a method that aims to desensitize you from fear altogether not just a fear of specific situations. This is done by cultivating a more equanimous mind, a mind that is less affected by the ups and downs of daily life. Partly for this purpose, bipolar exposure requires us to think about pleasant scenarios in order to obtain pleasant co-emerging body sensations. We then prevent any judgment and other reactions to these pleasant sensations, without clinging to them, understanding that they will pass. In daily life, this allows a more flexible way of evaluating situations and reduces our habitual likes and dislikes. This results in a better ability to enjoy situations, even when they are not to our liking. If I recall, one of your SUDS lists for exposure was avoiding social situa tions because you don t believe that socializing will be satisfying, even though you are feeling lonely, right? When you imagine the best-case scenarios, for example, seeing your best friend at a party you reluctantly accepted to attend, do your best to feel the apparent pleasant sensation and be as objective as possible. Rather than liking or disliking it, try to step back and observe care fully if the predominant sensation is principally manifesting with one or more of the four characteristics you learned about (i.e., mass, motion, temperature, and density), where pleasantness and unpleasantness are not as relevant. Do your best to process the pleasant sensations only through the sensory perception part of the mind, which simply notices, and steer away from activating the evaluation part of the mind, which judges with likes and dislikes. It means that you use pleasant sensations as tools to cultivate equa nimity, rather than using them to feel good. This is the impartial attitude toward experiences we are now needing to learn outside the standard medi tation context. In 2weeks from now, we will be starting a body-scanning method called sweeping to specifically decrease the habit of craving. intermediate i wonder why we should not allow ourselves to enjoy the pleas ant sensations produced by best-case s 2 2.1 book I think I m starting to really get the whole avoidance thing, during bipolar exposure, I don t understand why I need to visualize the 200 STAGE 2 EXPOSURE STAGE best-case scenarios as well, since that s not what I am avoiding. Also, can you explain why it s necessary to visualize the best-case scenarios last rather than the worst-case ones? When we face situations in real life that we ve been avoiding, the outcomes can vary. Things tend to go well, but sometimes they don There is no guarantee either way. While we often expect some degree of anxiety, we might also expect things to go well, building an expectation of success. But what happens if things don t go as well as we hoped? The disappointment could be difficult to manage, especially if we haven t yet learned to be with pleasant sensations with equanimity. Therefore, during bipolar exposure, we also visualize the best-case sce narios in order to allow pleasant body sensations to co-emerge while remain ing equanimous toward them. With practice, the attachment to pleasant outcomes decreases, and we are less likely to feel disappointed if real-life exposure doesn t go as expected. This also reduces any potential sense of failure and discouragement that can come from unmet expectations, allow ing us to continue with the practice. As for why we visualize the best-case scenarios last, and not first, this has to do with the processing of expected outcomes in memory. Do you remem ber our discussion of the recency effect during mindfulness of breath in Week 2? Our most recent experiences tend to be remembered more readily because brain cells that produce that memory are more readily activated. This applies to bipolar exposure as well. If we visualized the worst-case scenarios last, that would be what we remembered most between bipolar exposure sessions, and we might not be as confident to perform exposure in real life. By ending bipolar exposure with the pleasant scenarios, we leave ourselves with a positive impression of the forthcoming real-life exposure, which boosts our motivation and confidence. intermediate i think i m starting to really get the whole avoidance thing, during bipolar exposure, i don t under 2 2.1 book I wonder if I could reduce my cigarette smoking using bipolar exposure? Yes, you certainly can! The key is to take it step by step. Start by identifying the contexts in which you smoke the little things that make smoking feel like a personal ritual. You can then use bipolar exposure, fol lowed by real-life exposure, to change up your routine, and in this way reduce craving and attachment to the things that make smoking a personal habit with which you identify. Try smoking a different brand, using your other hand to smoke, and smoking in places that aren t your usual spots. You might also experiment with not smoking while drinking coffee or alcohol or being around other people. The idea is to make smoking feel less habitual and less enjoyable. If this makes sense to you and you are interested in giving it a trial, start with the easier changes first. In the second half of the week, break down attachment to remaining contexts, such as not smoking during the times of week 5: regulating behavior to overcome avoidance 201 day you usually would. After four sessions of bipolar exposure for each context change, implementing the change in real life won t be so daunting, especially since you are not even decreasing the number of cigarettes yet. In the second week, use bipolar and in vivo exposure to gradually reduce how much you smoke. For the first half of the week, delay smoking until the craving signature the pattern of body sensations has changed, and the intensity of unpleasant sensations has subsided. This will be facilitated by your increasing equanimity. Only then smoke a cigarette. If you think this would defeat the purpose of smoking, we would certainly agree with you. That s exactly what you can do this week: defeat the purpose of smoking! In the second half of the second week, do bipolar and in vivo exposure to decrease the number of cigarettes by about 50%. During the third week, do exposure to smoking a maximum of two ciga rettes during the times when the compulsion to smoke is the strongest often morning and evening for most people. Then, in the last 2days of that week, use bipolar exposure to prepare yourself for not having any cigarettes in the following (fourth) week. Make up your mind that those cigarettes in the third week will be the last for the rest of your life. Think of this as build ing renunciation skills rather than abstinence skills. You are choosing to quit, choosing to refuse the craving, rather than feeling deprived. This mind set shift can be very empowering. During the fourth week, apply strong determination to not react to unpleasant body sensations, just like you did in every session that you prac ticed in absolute immobility last week. This demonstrates the relevance of having practiced this way. In case there are other addictions you would like to let go of, the method just described can be applied to just about anything you are addicted to, including dopamine-seeking behavior, since much of addiction involves our psychological attachment to the body sensations associated with the substance or behavior. intermediate i wonder if i could reduce my cigarette smoking using bipolar exposure? 2 2.1 book My partner keeps telling me that I avoid visiting her parents on weekends like we used to, but frankly, it s just because I d get angry with them and end up saying what I really think. So, I prefer to stay home and spare my partner the embarrassment. I don t think I m avoiding anything by not wanting to hurt her feelings, though. It sounds like you care a lot about your partner s feelings and her relationship with her parents. But why is it that you feel you can t visit them without reacting with anger? What is it about those visits that s hard to handle equanimously? Sometimes, we avoid situations where we might face embarrassing or invalidating comments, or where we feel disrespected or uncomfortable. In these situations, it s the unpleasant sensations we feel that we re really trying to avoid. That s when 202 STAGE 2 EXPOSURE STAGE ll dive into mindful interpersonal skills and how to handle difficult conversations effectively. So, if visiting your partner s parents means address ing unresolved issues, then it might be best to revisit this in 2weeks, when we start learning mindful interpersonal skills. On the other hand, if you could visit them while observing sensations associated with anger while maintaining equanimity without needing to address the underlying issues directly this could be valuable exposure practice for this stage.
