Virginia Woolf (portrayed) in The Hours, screenplay by David Hare (2002) Practice Background and Rationale Understanding the relationship between past experiences and present reactivity is a crucial step in the Mindfulness-integrated Cognitive Behavior Therapy (MiCBT) program. This chapter builds on the foundational skills you have developed so far, helping you deepen your insight into the underlying causes of your thoughts, emotions, and behaviors. By now, it is likely that you ve noticed how memories and reactions can be triggered by subtle cues in your environment, mind, or body, often without conscious awareness. These automatic reactions are rooted in patterns formed by earlier experiences, which continue to influence how you interpret and respond to the world around you. In Week 6, you will explore how this conditioning can reveal the intercon nectedness of your experiences. By identifying the link between cause and effect, you will gain a clearer understanding of how your emotional and behavioral patterns have been shaped and how they can be shifted. This week also highlights the transformative power of recognizing the role body sensations play in maintaining these patterns, offering you new tools to work with deeply ingrained beliefs and responses. Let s explore this phenomenon of cause and effect more closely, beginning with how it manifests during meditation practice. Week 6: Improving Self-Confidence 204 STAGE 2 EXPOSURE STAGE Noticing Causes and Effects Law of Effect: Thorndike and the Buddha Edward Thorndike was one of the first behavioral scientists to study how new skills are learned. In his 1898 article describing a study on animal behavior [1], he described precisely how cats and other animals learn through consequences. He observed that a behavior (such as solving a prob lem) followed by a pleasant consequence (such as receiving food) is more likely to be repeated, while a behavior followed by an unpleasant conse quence is more likely to cease. Thorndike called this the Law of Effect. As a result, the consequence of a behavior also becomes the cause for the next behavior, and so on. This pioneering observation in Western psychology led to the development of what is known as learning theory and to the birth of behaviorism nearly 120years ago. About 2,400years earlier, Siddhartha Gautama, better known as the Buddha, discovered the same law through mindfulness meditation, which we now refer to as the Law of Karma [2], sometimes called the Law of Cause-and-Effect. Both the Buddha and Thorndike observed that behavior changes because of its consequences. However, they understood this at very different depths, and their interpretations of this principle led them to differ ent insights and applications. While Thorndike s work laid the foundation for modern behaviorism, the Buddha s deeper exploration through mindful ness meditation revealed how these principles could transform one s entire life. Capturing this natural law in every moment of our life requires special skills mindfulness skills. By remaining mindful of our daily mental, verbal, and physical actions, we shape future consequences; in fact, we reshape our future altogether. Your future is therefore shaped in this very moment. Each moment is connected to the past, as it is the source of our current experiences. Our current reactions are consequences, or effects, of past causes. By chang ing our reactions, we can alter the impact of the past, and in this way inter rupt the cycle of cause and effect. In Eastern terminology, we would say that we are changing our karma. Regardless of the causes contributing to a current unpleasant experience, your decisions and actions, grounded in awareness and equanimity, can change and halt the perpetuation of suffering. Metaphorically speaking, no matter what seeds have been planted in the past, they can only grow if the soil is fertile [3]. For a seed of anger to grow into an angry reaction, the soil (your mental state) must promote reactivity to body sensations. This applies to all negative emotions. However, what happens if the soil can no longer support its growth? What happens if you are equanimous with body sensations when the first signs of sadness, fear, or craving arise? By neutralizing learned reactivity, you are changing what grows in the garden of your destiny. You stop nourishing unwanted seeds week 6: improving self- confidence 205 and begin planting and nurturing the ones you choose. Even after a short while, you will notice a difference in your garden: weeds aren t growing quickly or as abundantly, while the good seeds you ve planted begin flourish. A Life-Changing Insight Do you recall our discussion in Week 2 about how memories can arise spontaneously, triggered by cues in the environment, mind, or body? By now, you may have experienced this phenomenon in your own meditation practice. For instance, while meditating, you may notice that the sound of a dog barking immediately evokes a memory of a past experience with a dog or sensations in the body accompanied by a co-emerging memory of an event where a similar sensation occurred. It s important to recognize that these memories arise automatically there is no self that chooses to remember them. Instead, this automatic recall arises from memory traces in the brain that were formed during the original events, reactivating when triggered by internal or external stimuli. In this context, a cause refers to the original event or condition that cre ates a specific memory, while an effect is the resulting experience or response that arises when those memories or sensations are reactivated. As your insight deepens with meditation practice, you start to see how intercon nected all experiences are, challenging the common impression that events and sensations are isolated in space and time and independent of each other. Over time, your direct experience will reveal a foundational understand ing: all things are interconnected, in one way or another. Every experience is both an effect of past causes and a potential cause for future experiences. You will observe that everything you experience is conditioned by some thing else. This recognition that nothing arises independently becomes central to understanding how past perceptions shape your present experi ence and how your current mental state conditions your future interpreta tions of external events. Observing this intricate interplay allows you to perceive that your present experience is but the effect of a past cause, and may become the cause of a future effect when the conditions are sufficiently conducive for it to emerge. Like thoughts, sensations come and go, depend ent on past causes and current conditions, mostly outside of our control. Integrating this simple but profound insight alone can change your life. Jack, a veteran grappling with chronic post traumatic stress disorder (PTSD), provided a poignant example of causes and effects. Haunted by witnessing a close friend trigger a landmine, he lived in a state of constant hypervigilance. Back home, he avoided open spaces by walking close to walls in public places, ensuring potential threats could only come from one direction. Sudden loud sounds, such as a car backfiring, triggered an automatic ducking reflex, as if a past explosion was occurring in the present. 206 STAGE 2 EXPOSURE STAGE His mindfulness practice allowed him to observe how these reactions were conditioned by his wartime experiences. He traced his present reac tions back to their origins, noticing how certain sounds reactivated body sensations linked to his wartime experiences. As Jack s insight grew, he began to notice three particular phenomena: when a current trigger (such as a sound or another stimulus) arose, a cause established in the past (body sensations connected to the meaning previously assigned to that sound) pro duced the same effect (ducking behavior) he had performed in the past. In psychology, this cause effect phenomenon is known as a conditioned response. As this understanding of causes and their effects these condi tioned responses became more deeply integrated in his daily life, he gradu ally learned to move through the world with less fear. Training Mindfulness Skills Checking Your Readiness Congratulations! You have completed your third week of practicing mind fulness of body sensations using symmetrical body scanning. Were you able to maintain your commitment to practicing 30minutes twice daily with accurate understanding treating all experiences as impermanent phenom ena that could be potential sources of suffering if you cling to or avoid them? If so, you are likely noticing a reduction in reactivity during daily life perhaps you re finding that emotional responses feel less intense or resolve more quickly. This shift reflects that your brain is beginning to rewire itself, forming new or stronger beneficial connections. These observable changes aren t merely subjective impressions. Research shows that as you develop greater equanimity and focus, your brain s activ ity patterns adapt. First, there is a reduction in default mode network activ ity as the central executive network becomes more effective at regulating thoughts and emotions [4]. Second, parts of the amygdala an almond shaped structure deep within the limbic system tend to decrease in size, a change associated with reduced stress [5]. Together, these changes make low mood and anxiety less likely to recur. When to Proceed to the Next Step You do not need to feel every part of the body perfectly before moving on. A good guideline is that once you can feel about 80% of your body sym metrically, you re ready to begin the next technique, known as partial sweeping. If You Need More Time If you have been practicing regularly but are not quite feeling 80% of the body yet, continue with symmetrical scanning for a few more days, in silence week 6: improving self- confidence 207 and without audio instructions. And please postpone reading this chapter until you are ready to start partial sweeping. Aligning your reading with the meditation technique you are practicing will enhance clarity and deepen your understanding of each exercise. Partial Sweeping The Practice Partial sweeping is the second advanced body-scanning method. Like sym metrical scanning, it involves feeling both sides of the body at once, but this time you will sweep your attention smoothly and continuously through large sections of the body. Begin at the top of your head and sweep your attention downward, feeling as many sensations on the scalp and face as possible at once. After scanning the head, move down through the neck and throat, and then con tinue symmetrically down both shoulders, arms, and hands all the way to the fingertips. From there, move to the torso, the buttocks, and finally the lower limbs. When sweeping down the legs, begin at the top of your thighs and sweep slowly and progressively down to the tips of your toes in one single movement of attention, feeling as many parts as you can. If sweeping both sides of the body at the same time feels too challenging at first, begin with sweeping one side at a time until you can do both together. Just a note about the trunk, or torso. Because the trunk is a larger area, s best to divide it into sections initially. For the first 2days, sweep attention down the front of the torso from the base of the throat to the groin one continuous movement. Next, sweep down the back of the torso from the base of the neck to the buttocks in the same manner. Finally, sweep down the sides of the torso, starting at the armpits and feeling both sides simultaneously as you move toward the hips. After practicing this for 2days (i.e., four sessions), begin combining the front and back. For instance, sweep down the chest and upper back together, then the abdomen and lower back. For the last 3days of the week, combine sweeping the front, back, and sides of the torso together in one continuous fluid motion. After reaching the toes, scan the body back upward, returning to the top of the head in the same manner. Aim to complete as many body cycles as possible during your 30-minute practice. Sometimes our mind can get so busy that we are frequently distracted and can only do two or three body cycles. Other times, we can easily do six or more body cycles. Regardless of how many cycles you complete, the most important factor is practicing with strong determination resolutely maintaining stillness and equanimity while guiding attention through the body. As you learn this new method, it is also possible to be surprised by sud denly being unable to feel as much of the body as you did with symmetrical 208 STAGE 2 EXPOSURE STAGE scanning. This is a normal variation in practice that sometimes occurs and should not be a cause for alarm; like everything else, it will also pass! And as with previous forms of body scanning, you ll naturally experience areas where sensations feel unclear or absent. That s okay and to be expected. t interrupt your flow of attention; simply note these areas of decreased sensation. Continue sweeping through the entire body, completing two or three full cycles. Then go back to these unclear areas and survey them sepa rately, part by part, before resuming sweeping. If your sweeping pace is very slow taking, for example, 5 10minutes per cycle begin by completing one full body sweep. Then, survey any missed areas part by part before resuming the sweeping practice. Sweeping through larger sections of the body is naturally accompanied by more co-emerging thoughts, making it easier to get caught up in thinking, which in turn can slow the pace of scanning. Although this may feel surpris ing and even discouraging especially after having been more focused in previous weeks it does not indicate a decline in your skill. Rather, it reflects the relationship between body sensations and co-emerging thoughts. When you were scanning the body part by part, you were feeling sensations in small areas of about 5 8cm in diameter. In contrast, sweeping through large sections of the body results in feeling many more sensations, which, in turn, can result in more co-emerging thoughts. When you find this happening during your practice, instead of feeling discouraged, remember that these thoughts are also impermanent, without taking them personally. Skills You Will Learn Have you noticed that some of the body sensations you feel during practice are becoming subtler? This occurs partly because the areas of your brain responsible for sensing the body the somatosensory and insular cortices are forming new neural connections and strengthening existing ones. As a result of this neurological adaptation, you can detect more subtle sensations, including pleasant ones, in areas where you previously felt little or where sensations initially seemed more salient and solidified. You may have already noticed gentle, pleasant tingling in certain areas and wondered what this might mean. This is entirely normal and actually a positive sign, indicating that you are practicing effectively. If you have yet to encounter these subtle sensations, don t be concerned; they will likely emerge with continued practice. These subtler sensations can arise in your practice in two common ways at this stage: (1) as very early cues of emotional experiences before any clear emotion has fully formed and (2) as mild yet distinctly pleasant, even pleas urable, sensations. Both of these occur because sweeping attention over large areas of the body mirrors the wave-like flow characteristic of various emotions, such as anger, sadness, joy, and pleasure. As we discussed in an earlier chapter, the ever-shifting nature of body sensations can be understood through the lens of the four elements earth, week 6: improving self- confidence 209 water, fire, and air. Among these, the water element embodies the character istic of density that you ve been observing during your Mindfulness-based Interoceptive Exposure Task (MIET) practice. It can manifest as fluidity: a cohesive, wave-like quality that emerges as you sweep attention through increasingly subtle sensations. Instead of viewing the body as a solid struc ture, you can begin to experience it as composed of flowing, constantly changing subtle sensations, often described as vibrations. Recognizing this fluid quality can help you observe impermanence while strengthening your capacity for equanimity when these vibrations arise, calmly witnessing both pleasant and unpleasant sensations as part of a natural, ever-changing ebb and flow of experience. Wave-Like Sensations as Emotional Cues When you use sweeping techniques, emotions that commonly arise in wave-like patterns are more likely to co-emerge. These emotions often co emerge with thoughts, because the mind tries to assign a cause to these apparently random feelings. This doesn t mean you will always recall spe cific memories of when you previously felt those emotions. Instead, you might suddenly feel warm, passionate, sad, angry, or joyful for a few sec onds or minutes without knowing why. By maintaining equanimity, you neutralize any reaction to these sensations, allowing them to subside more easily. Over time, this can lead to noticeable benefits, including reduced anger, sadness, or shame in daily life, and an enhanced capacity to experi ence joy and overall well-being. Wave-Like Sensations as Triggers for Craving Sweeping techniques can sometimes trigger craving because moving attention through subtle, diffuse sensations often feels like a pleasantly flowing, wave-like energy. If you pause to examine the deeper nature of these sensations for a moment, you may observe that the tingling we feel in various parts of the body results from countless subtle sensations arising and passing extremely rapidly. The pleasant wave you experi ence is a product of attention flowing through these transient, imperma nent sensations. Now that you have acquired a good degree of equanimity, managing both pleasant sensations and any associated craving has become more feasible. As we discussed in the past weeks, one benefit of remaining equanimous during body scanning is that it allows body sensations to serve as memory cues for similar sensations felt in the past. Consequently, when you sweep through certain parts of the body, pleasant sensations can serve as memory cues for very pleasurable past events or anticipated pleasurable experiences you have been thinking about recently or frequently. These might include feeling loved and cared for, romantic encounters, sexual experiences, getting a large sum of money, or times when you used substances to alter your mood. You may notice this effect more strongly during the sweeping en masse practice in the following week. 210 STAGE 2 EXPOSURE STAGE Under the Spell of Craving During the last 3days of a 10-day intensive mindfulness meditation retreat in the Vipassana tradition used in MiCBT participants learn to sweep attention through the body, as explained above. Practicing for 12hours each day, it s not uncommon for participants new to the prac tice to become caught up in intensely pleasurable memories and start crav ing similar experiences in the future. This is understandable, as fluid, wave-like sensations can easily bring up cravings from the past. However, this can become problematic, as prolonged craving reactions increase the activation of reactive parts of the brain and hinder the develop ment of equanimity. Although you are only practicing 30minutes twice daily, and intense cravings are therefore less likely to occur, here is an anec dote that illustrates the relationship between sweeping and the stirring of craving, as recounted by Bruno: About 36years ago, several months after I started practicing this meditation technique, one of my friends, a young and passionate French baker, decided to try it as well. He enrolled in a 10-day intensive retreat with me, where we practiced between 10 and 12hours daily, cross-legged on the floor. Upon his return, the first thing he did was to contact an ex-girlfriend, take cocaine and ecstasy, and have sex for a whole day! He later explained that during the retreat s last 3days, as he began sweeping through the body, he was overwhelmed by cravings and fantasies. All he could think about was returning home and having sex with someone. Although he was able to manage the typical discomfort of prolonged sitting, pleasure was too hard to resist. His equanimity went out the window and he gave in com pletely to the sensations. During our post-mortem discussion of his experience, it became clear that he hadn t understood the rationale for equanimity or its role in handling crav ing for impermanent experiences. He humorously attributed his compensatory extremes to the famed romantic tendencies characteristic of the French, yet the underlying principle applies broadly: without recognizing the fluid, imperma nent nature of these sensations while applying equanimity, it s easy to be swept away by craving s powerful currents. Integrating Skills in Daily Life Applied Practice As in the previous 2 weeks, you are encouraged to bring your awareness of body sensations and equanimity into daily life, especially in stressful situa tions. Doing so helps maintain and strengthen the beneficial neural connec tions associated with interoceptive awareness and equanimity, while also facilitating the new body-scanning method you ll learn this week partial sweeping. week 6: improving self- confidence 211 Bipolar and In Vivo Exposure How did filling out the Subjective Units of Distress (SUDS) form (Table 5.2) go for you? Many people find it difficult to remain objective about their own avoidant behavior, so don t worry if it wasn t easy. And what about applying bipolar exposure to your first two or three situations on the SUDS form? Were you able to visualize both the worst- and best-case scenarios while remaining equanimous for four sessions, and then face the situation in real life? If so, you re ready to address the remaining, more challenging situ ations. Although you may feel a bit intimidated these are often issues that have been avoided for quite some time it can also be exciting to resolve them skillfully, usually in a relatively short period. From Avoidant Behavior to an Avoidant Self As we discussed previously, painful life events can lead to habits of avoid ance. Over time, avoidance promotes unhelpful brain plasticity, which can generalize to other stressful situations. Consider the example of a teacher who was traumatized in his workplace. The school environment felt threat ening to him, leading first to avoidance of the school itself. As his avoidance became established both psychologically and neurologically, he began avoid ing students outside of school as well. This meant that he would not go out at certain times of the day. Progressively, his avoidance spread. Eventually, he avoided leaving his home, just in case he encountered anybody remotely related to the initial environment in which the trauma occurred the school. Over time, his avoidance generalized to the wider context of work itself, leaving him fearful of starting any job he anticipated could be stressful. So what was he truly avoiding? What is stress, at its core, made of? Indeed, it comes down to unpleasant bodily sensations! Sometimes the link between cause and effect is readily apparent, and we can understand the underlying reasons for our avoidant behavior. More often, though, the initial causes are long-forgotten, buried under a rubble of memories, and we start to identify with the avoidant behaviors. For example, we might say I don t like eating in public or I only visit familiar places, but we rarely question where these preferences originated. Over time, the avoidant behavior becomes a part of who we are, influencing our decisions and shaping our identity. We become avoidant. For example, children who were reprimanded for asserting their views and rewarded for not expressing discontent are likely to grow up avoiding conflict, seeing this trait not as a learned behavior, but rather as part of who they are. These patterns of avoidance can become further entrenched when tied to self-worth such as feeling good about oneself because of the ability to avoid confrontation and maintain harmony. When these children become adults, they may describe their difficulty expressing their perspective by say ing, m not confrontational like my colleague or I let my partner talk to the neighbor about their loud music. 212 STAGE 2 EXPOSURE STAGE The Generalizing Effects of Exposure Just as unhelpful behaviors can generalize across various aspects of our life, helpful behaviors can also generalize. Reducing avoidance and anxiety in one area naturally decreases avoidance across similar situations. For instance, if you become less anxious about asking for help from a family member, then you re also likely to feel less anxious about asking for help work. Even more powerfully, generalization can occur across seemingly unrelated areas, particularly through the exposure techniques you learn in MiCBT. For example, if you learn to be less anxious about physical pain through exposure exercises, this can translate into reduced anxiety in seemingly unrelated areas, such as being assertive or starting a new job. Why does this happen? The key lies in desensitizing your response to the body sensations associated with fear, not just to the specific situations themselves. As you ve been exploring in your own experience, experiences of fear share certain bodily characteristics what we call the interoceptive signa ture of fear. These sensations might include movement, constriction, or heat, as discussed in Weeks 3 and 4. When equanimity reduces your fear of experiencing these sensations in one context, your fear of similar sensations in other situations is also decreased. In other words, exposure with equa nimity desensitizes you to fear itself, not just to fear in the situations you previously avoided. Progressively, you become less avoidant because there is less fear of fear! This was such a striking realization for a middle-aged woman struggling with chronic depression, severe anxiety, and excessive alcohol consumption. Drinking between 4 and 8l of wine nightly, she eventually required hospitali zation. While in hospital, she attended the weekly MiCBT group on the unit. After several weeks, she was discharged and continued attending as an outpa tient. In her sixth week of MiCBT, she was using the bipolar exposure exer cises to address anger-provoking situations with her partner. During the session that week, she shared an experience that made everyone laugh out loud: As you know, I have a bit of OCD too and dirt freaks me out. Well, saw my towel on the bathroom floor and knew that it was one of the kids who left it there. When I picked it up, I saw a pubic hair on it and it didn bother me! I just shook it off, as if it wasn t a big deal, put it back on the rack and moved on. Normally, just seeing the towel on the floor would have been enough for me to be anxious and scream at the kids and scare them to death! I just couldn t believe it. Her entire way of generating anxiety and the anger that followed was changing from within. The most notable aspect of this transformation was that her nervous system was progressively generalizing improvements in her management of anxiety to how she managed anger as well. Her equanimity during exposure to one high arousal state (anxiety) led to generalization equanimity to another high arousal state (anger). This illustrates how week 6: improving self- confidence 213 equanimity can generalize from one challenging emotional state to another. Remarkably, she was unknowingly neutralizing her obsessive fear of dirt without directly exposing to dirt itself. A similar pattern emerges in clients coping with chronic pain. They sys tematically report that as they desensitize to pain sensations, they also find it easier to handle difficult emotions. Keeping this in mind, our work this week will continue to focus on exposure. We will use unpleasant sensations during exposure as tools to desensitize ourselves from all fear reactions. The more you can remain equanimous with unpleasant body sensations, the more efficient and generalizable the desensitization will be. If you learn to smile at a particular fear during practice, you will be more likely to smile at other fears in daily life, because they all share a similar pattern of bodily sensations. One of our clients at this stage of the program recently described it, pointing to their body, This method is amazing! It s like seeing this house on fire and a firefighter putting out the fire immediately! Measuring Progress with Desensitization After a week of exposure practice, you may be starting to notice changes in your anxiety and resistance toward previously avoided situations. Tracking this progress by recording changes in distress levels as percentages can be especially helpful. To do this, use the SUDS form from Table 5.2 that you began filling out last week. Let s build on the example from Week 5 to see how this works (refer to Table 6.1). For instance, in Situation 1, going to the supermarket and other shops was anticipated to cause about 25% distress last week. Now, the expected distress has been reduced to just 5%. Similarly, in Situation 2, accepting invitations from friends to socialize previously came with an expectation of 35% distress, which has now decreased to 10%. As the form shows, the distress levels for Situations 4 and 5 even though not yet addressed through bipolar or in vivo exposure have also decreased. As discussed earlier in the chapter, this is due to the generalization of desen sitization. These changes highlight a common outcome: as you practice equanimity during exposure, your confidence in facing previously avoided situations grows in tandem. Now, return to your own form. Record today s date for each of your five situations, then re-rate and document your current expected level of distress for each one, even though you probably had time to practice exposure for only two or three of them. This Week s Practice Partial Sweeping This week, focus on sweeping your attention smoothly and continuously through large sections of the body. When sweeping through the limbs, feel both sides simultaneously. With the torso, begin with the front, back, and then sides for a few days, and gradually combine them over the course of the week. By the last few days, aim to be sweeping the entire torso in one fluid motion. If you encounter areas where sensations are faint or not felt at all, note them without interrupting the flow of your attention. After completing two or three full body cycles, revisit those areas part by part. You may find that maintaining equanimity with pleasant sensations can be more challenging than with unpleasant ones. Allow yourself to feel pleas ant sensations while developing equanimity toward them. This will train you to appreciate and enjoy life s pleasant experiences more fully, without clinging to them or feeling dissatisfied when they pass, while also strength ening your ability to remain balanced and peaceful in their presence or absence. I just can get it out of my mind. This is a common question! Most of us aren t used to accepting thoughts as just thoughts and letting them pass, whether this is during meditation or while practicing Right Effort, even though research shows that suppressing thoughts is not efficient, and usually not effective [6]. Would you like to try a short exercise to see why this might be? s give it a try! Lean back in your chair and close your eyes. Now, imagine a police officer knocking at your door and handing you a letter to appear in court. Take half a minute to picture this as vividly as you can Now, switch your attention to your breath and try not to think about the event at all. re laughing because you ve realized how difficult it is to stop thinking about something once it s already in your mind. This is partly because in order to block a thought, you have to first think about it! This keeps activating the week 6: improving self- confidence 219 same brain cells, which maintains the thought rather than getting rid of it! The harder you try to block it, the more it sticks around. The key is to remain equanimous and allow those neurons to deactivate in their own time. Otherwise, the only other option is to mask the thought by distracting yourself with an even stronger thought. For example, you might think about a fun memory from your last holiday to temporarily push away thoughts about the court summons. While this might work for a moment, it s not always practical especially in situations where distrac tions are hard to find like when you re in bed trying to fall asleep. In these cases, unwanted thoughts often persist, leading to rumination. You may have experienced this pattern yourself! Your question also highlights an important observation: when we try to block a thought but remain unaware of the body sensations that arise with it, the thought doesn t truly go away. Instead, it remains tied to the body sensations. Since the sensations are part and parcel of the thought, when one arises, the other does too. If we re unaware of the sensations, it becomes harder to approach them equanimously, making the associated thoughts even more difficult to release. So, when an unwanted thought arises, try this instead: focus your full attention on the body sensations that come with it. Stay with those sensa tions quietly, peacefully, and equanimously. As the emotional intensity of the thought decreases, you ll notice that the thought itself becomes less persistent. Attempting to block persisting, troubling thoughts won t make them go away; it can even make them more persistent. But acknowledging, under standing, and accepting the associated body sensations is a more effective way to let go of these thoughts. intermediate i m still not clear on why i can t just block my negative thoughts when they come up. i know you ve 2 2.2 book I found part-by-part scanning easier because I didn t have so many thoughts. Remember our earlier discussions about not getting attached to impermanent mental states? This situation is a perfect example. When our experience changes, it s easy to feel dissatisfied if we become attached to a particular way of experiencing things. This is an important reflection on impermanence not just in meditation, but in life as a whole. Think about changes in your career or relationships; disap pointment often arises because we want things to stay the way we prefer. The same principle applies here. Now, let s address why you re noticing more thoughts during sweeping. Hopefully, this explanation will help you let go of the idea that you 220 STAGE 2 EXPOSURE STAGE regressing. When you practiced part-by-part body scanning, your focus was narrower and more defined. This is because you were feeling a smaller num ber of sensations at a time, so there were fewer sensations for thoughts to co-emerge with! With sweeping, however, you re covering much larger areas of the body in a given period, and this naturally brings up more sensations. As a result, more thoughts are likely to co-emerge with those sensations. This is normal and is why most people experience the return of persistent, intrusive thoughts when they practice using sweeping techniques. The key is to recognize that these thoughts are not a problem. They arise because of the increased flow of sensations being observed s simply a natural part of the process. Rather than feeling frustrated, focus on staying equanimous toward both the thoughts themselves and any expectations that it should be different. Given that mindfulness is about gradually becoming more aware of the breath, thoughts, and body sensa tions in the present moment, I have a question about the bipolar exposure and in vivo exposure techniques. I was just wondering why you chose to include them in MiCBT when meditation alone is so beneficial. week 6: improving self- confidence 221 There s no doubt that Vipassana meditation alone can address fears and reduce avoidance, especially when practiced intensively and with clear understanding. However, this process can take considerable time, particularly when avoidance behaviors are deeply ingrained or tied to clinical levels of anxiety. Here s a way to think about it: since you began practicing Vipassana med itation, have you completely overcome your avoidant behaviors? Be honest with yourself are there still situations or experiences you avoid because they make you anxious, even though you know it would be beneficial to face them? If the answer is yes, this highlights why exposure is included in MiCBT. For example, if someone has a fear of public speaking, they could medi tate in isolation for an entire year and still feel unable to face an audience when they return. Relying solely on sitting on a cushion with closed eyes will not do the trick! To transfer the skills we cultivate in meditation to real world contexts, particularly those where it s more difficult to apply them, we need focused, targeted practice. In such cases, deliberate desensitization through exposure with equanimity becomes essential. Exposure helps us confront what we ve been avoiding while practicing equanimity in real-life situations not just during meditation. The Buddha taught this to monks in his own and most interesting ways. For example, he would ask monks who struggled with letting go of per sistent sensual cravings to meditate in a cemetery and carefully observe the reality of the body, especially its impermanent, decaying nature [7]. This, indeed, is a form of exposure! This wouldn t have been suggested if meditation alone were sufficient to address craving. If you re interested to read more about the relevance of exposure in the Buddha s teachings, Bhaya-bherava Sutta, also known as the Discourse on Fear and Terror, [8, 9] can be easily found on the Access to Insight website. In this sutta, the Buddha recounts his experiences of confronting and overcom ing fear and terror during his early meditative practices in secluded for ests. Here is a passage of this teaching, slightly shortened, in case you find it interesting: The thought occurred to me: What if on recognized, designated nights such as the eighth, fourteenth, and fifteenth of the lunar fortnight I were to stay in the sort of places that are awe-inspiring and make your hair stand on end, such as park-shrines, forest-shrines, and tree-shrines? Perhaps I would get to see that fear and terror. So at a later time [ ] I stayed in the sort of places that are awe-inspiring [ ] And while I was staying there a wild animal would come, or a peacock would make a twig fall, or wind would rustle the fallen leaves. The thought would occur to me: Is this that fear and terror coming? Then the thought occurred to me: Why do I just keep waiting for fear? What if I, in whatever state I m in when fear & terror come to me, were to subdue that fear & terror in that very state? 222 STAGE 2 EXPOSURE STAGE In MiCBT, we practice approaching aversive stimuli with equanimity toward the sensations they evoke, which has been shown to be more effec tive than standard exposure therapy alone [10]. This doesn t conflict with Vipassana meditation. Instead, it makes good use of it! There is an even more important reason why exposure is included in MiCBT: to further increase overall levels of equanimity. This is particularly important because only a small number of people seeking therapy attend 10-day intensive Vipassana meditation retreats, where deep equanimity is typically developed. By Week 4 of MiCBT, most practitioners are able to sit for 30 minutes with strong determination focused on the body, immobile, and equanimous. Once this level of skill is achieved, the development of equanimity naturally slows down, as we ve already built enough equanimity to accept thoughts and sensations and let them pass more easily. However, to continue cultivating equanimity, we need to introduce more challenging contexts just as muscles grow stronger when training with progressively heavier weights in a gym. Exposure introduces new, anxiety-provoking situations that generate just the right intensity of body sensations needed to continue developing equa nimity to a greater extent. This added challenge promotes further brain reorganization and helps reduce the risk of relapse into unhelpful habits or mental health conditions. In this way, exposure not only complements Vipassana meditation but also enhances its transformative potential. intermediate i recently completed a 10-day retreat at a vipassana meditation center and have been practicing dail 2 2.2 book Can you clarify what I m supposed to do during the 1-minute rest period during bipolar exposure am I just supposed to focus on the breath? Bruno A. Cayoun and Andrea D. Grabovac. If we could read the secret history of our enemies, we should find in each s (sic) life sorrow and suffering enough to disarm all hostility. Henry Wadsworth Longfellow Practice Background and Rationale This chapter marks the beginning of Stage 3 of your Mindfulness-integrated Cognitive Behavior Therapy (MiCBT) journey, where mindfulness skills are applied to interpersonal relationships. In this section, you ll explore how interactions with others influence your mental and emotional states, identi fying patterns in your reactions to others emotions and the role co-emerging body sensations play in these reactions. Developing equanimity to more subtle and refined sensations during sweeping en masse body scanning this week will support you in remaining equanimous during interpersonal interactions. Developing Interpersonal Mindfulness The main purpose of Stage 3 of MiCBT is to help you develop equanimity during interpersonal interactions, especially the more challenging ones. By bringing a mindful presence and equanimity to your relationships, you strengthen your ability to communicate effectively, even when others are emotionally reactive. A wonderful consequence of remaining equanimous in Week 7: Improving Relationships with Interpersonal Mindfulness 228 STAGE 3 INTERPERSONAL STAGE these moments is the ability to notice and understand others suffering more clearly, enabling genuine empathy. Stage 3 also addresses interpersonal avoidance, particularly the tendency to withdraw from communicating or interacting with others when uncom fortable emotions arise. Unlike situations like driving over a high bridge or attending to household chores, where the environment itself does not respond to you, interactions with others introduce layers of unpredictability and emotional complexity. This inherent uncertainty can challenge even skilled communicators, as it may trigger feelings of vulnerability to criti cism, rejection, or conflict particularly when interacting with those who play significant roles in your life. To avoid this discomfort, you might side step interactions entirely, and avoidance may seem like a safer choice. However, by cultivating equanimity and understanding, you can overcome these tendencies and build effective communication skills that deeply enrich your relationships. For those recovering from anxiety or depression, these skills are particu larly valuable, as these conditions often isolate individuals, weakening social connections and increasing the risk of symptom recurrence. Strengthening these connections through interpersonal mindfulness can support both recovery and the prevention of relapse. Stage 3 consists of two interconnected steps. In the first week, you ll prac tice maintaining equanimity in response to others reactions, strengthening your nonverbal assertiveness. In the second week, you ll build on this foun dation to develop verbal assertiveness, enabling you to express your experi ences and needs effectively. Many people find this empowering, as it fosters emotional resilience and a deeper understanding of others perspectives qualities that naturally lay the groundwork for cultivating compassion, which will be the focus in Week 9. This week, you ll begin by applying equanimity in moderately challenging social situations. This approach will help you discern where your responsi bilities lie when others are distressed or upset, reducing unproductive feel ings of guilt, fear, and avoidance. It is worth a reminder here that not all avoidance is unhelpful avoiding a situation is a prudent choice when it carries genuine risks to your safety or well-being. Our Relationship with Others Our social environment has a profound effect on our well-being. Research consistently shows that interpersonal challenges are closely linked with clin ical levels of anxiety, depression, and even suicidality, often triggered by experiences of rejection or feeling wronged by others [1]. Early experiences of rejection in childhood can make us more vulnerable to this. The effects of emotions are bidirectional; our emotions affect others, just as theirs can affect us. For example, consider the impact of expressing gratitude versus being critical of a colleague in a work setting; each choice significantly shapes future interactions. WEEK 7: IMPROVING RELATIONSHIPS 229 As social beings, we naturally share what we feel, often without realizing it. This interdependence means that our emotional states, whether positive or negative, ripple through our social circles. When we are depressed or unmotivated, it inevitably affects our relationships. Over time, friends may withdraw, and even family members can eventually tire, leading to a gradual crumbling of our support network. This dynamic also applies when those closest to us are unwell, especially when they are the people we care most deeply about. Their struggles can pro foundly affect us emotionally. This interplay of emotional influence extends not only to negative mental states but also to positive ones. When we experience happiness, those around us notice our smiles come more readily, we engage more warmly, and we are more likely to forgive minor faults creating a fulfill ing supportive social environment. Joy is contagious, often inspiring creativity and optimism in others, and serves as a reminder that happiness is attainable. The way we influence and are influenced by our relationships is central to improving and sustaining well-being. Without extending mindfulness skills to our interactions, our progress may be limited, as unresolved patterns in communication and social insight can perpetuate unhelpful habits. This is especially significant because our sense of self-worth is deeply tied to our rela tionships with others. Below, we ll review key research findings that highlight the importance of this aspect of our life and its connection to well-being. Our Brains in Social Contexts Research consistently demonstrates that two key areas of the cerebral cortex are activated when we think about ourselves and our social environment: the posterior cingulate cortex and the medial prefrontal cortex (mPFC) [2]. The mPFC, located just behind the forehead, plays a particularly significant role in social contexts, supporting our ability to understand others emo tions, generate empathy, and maintain social bonds [3]. Embedded within this region are mirror neurons specialized cells that allow us to sense and mirror others feelings, often without conscious effort. These neurons enable us to imitate or predict behaviors [4, 5]. Mirror neurons make us yawn when others do and feel hungry when others eat! They also help athletes predict their opponent s movements and allow us to emotionally connect with a book or movie. Importantly, mirror neurons enable us to mir ror the emotions of those around us whether joy, stress, or sadness and play a crucial role in developing empathy [6]. Together, these brain regions and mirror neurons shape both our empathetic responses and our emotional reactivity, influencing how we relate to and interpret others actions. The impact of our social environment on the brain is evident in studies of early adversity. For instance, researchers studied nine-year-old children who had been institutionalized in impoverished orphanages in Eastern Europe and Asia. They found that children adopted after 15 months of age had significantly larger amygdalae than those adopted 12 months earlier [7]. Similarly, another study examined 10-year-old children whose mothers had 230 STAGE 3 INTERPERSONAL STAGE experienced frequent depression since their birth. These children also had larger amygdalae compared to children whose mothers had not faced such challenges [8]. These enlarged amygdalae represent the brain s adaptation to early life stress creating a hypervigilant emotional processing system that continues to generate heightened anxiety and emotional reactivity even years after the children were placed in safe environments. These findings highlight how early social experiences shape brain pathways, which, in turn, affect our interactions with others from childhood through adulthood [9]. While early social experiences leave lasting imprints on the brain, our abil ity to navigate social interactions continues to evolve throughout life. The way our brains process and interpret the preferences, thoughts, and emotions of others plays a critical role in our social relationships. A fascinating study explored the neural mechanisms that enable us to make intuitive assessments about others [10]. Researchers asked participants to look at photos of stran gers for 3seconds and guess their preferences for various items based on minimal facial information. Those participants who were better able to guess strangers preferences were those who had greater activity in the dorsomedial prefrontal cortex, a brain region involved in understanding others mental states and taking their perspective. Conversely, participants who relied on their own preferences when guessing showed greater activity in the ventro medial prefrontal cortex, which is linked to self-referential thinking. In other words, participants were less accurate in understanding others when their thoughts were more centered on judgments based on their own values and beliefs. The good news is that mindfulness meditation reduces both self-referential thinking the evaluation component in the co-emergence model and ventromedial prefrontal cortex activity, suggesting that mind fulness may support more balanced and accurate social perception [11]. Encouragingly, mindfulness meditation has also been found to reduce the volume of the amygdala, with associated decreases in perceived stress after just weeks of practice [12]. This reduction in emotional reactivity enhances our capacity for social connection and provides a neurological foundation for developing improved communication skills. Studies focusing specifically on mindfulness and communication have shown that mindfulness training enhances people s ability to connect and communicate effectively [13, 14]. In line with this evidence, cultivating a deeper understanding of others and hon ing mindful communication skills will be central to this stage of your journey. Training Mindfulness Skills Checking Your Readiness You have now completed your fourth week of mindfulness training focused on body sensations, using the partial sweeping technique. How has the practice been for you? Are you able to direct your attention through entire sections WEEK 7: IMPROVING RELATIONSHIPS 231 of the body in a continuous flow? Can you feel subtle vibrations throughout some areas of the body? Do these sensations sometimes feel like electricity or energy? It can feel remarkable to discover this heightened sense of aliveness. When to Proceed to the Next Step At this stage, you might notice subtle tingling sensations as you sweep your attention through the body. While a free flow of attention throughout the entire body might not be present yet, you might be experiencing it in spe cific, discrete areas. Generally, sensations in the limbs are easier to feel when learning partial sweeping. The trunk often needs a little more work. You are ready to move to the next body-scanning method, sweeping en masse, when you can consistently feel approximately 80% of the body during partial sweeping. If You Need More Time If you re not yet able to feel sensations in about 80% of the body during a 30-minute practice, continue with partial sweeping for a few more days. Focus on areas like the trunk to refine your ability to sweep through them in a single, fluid movement of attention. After two or three sweepings, revisit any blank or unclear areas separately, maintaining your attention on each of these spots for up to 30 seconds. Exposure to Avoided Situations How did your bipolar and in vivo exposures go this week? Were you able to address the last two or three challenging situations from your Subjective Units of Distress (SUDS) form? If so, congratulations! You are now ready to move on to the next step the interpersonal stage of MiCBT. That said, as noted in the last chapter, logistical challenges can sometimes make it difficult to address certain situations. You may have only managed to work through one, or perhaps none, of the higher SUDS items this week. If that s the case, then it is best to postpone the next step of this program for a few days, or even a week, to give yourself time to work through the remaining avoided situations. There can be so much benefit to bringing equanimity to experiences where you anticipate intense feelings. If your scanning ability with partial sweeping has progressed to the point where you are ready to move on to sweeping en masse, feel free to begin practicing this new technique while continuing bipolar and in vivo exposure to the remaining situations on your list. Sweeping En Masse The Practice The third advanced body-scanning method used in MiCBT is sweeping masse. Unlike partial sweeping, which involves scanning the body in sections, sweeping en masse requires you to move your attention through 232 STAGE 3 INTERPERSONAL STAGE the entire body in a single, uninterrupted motion from the top of the head down to the tips of the toes, and then back up from the toes to the top of head. Of course, there may be some blank spots where you can t feel sensa tions. This is to be expected, especially for the first few days. These blank spots offer a great opportunity to cultivate equanimity. As you sweep, sim ply notice any areas where sensations are absent without interrupting the flow of your attention. Just like last week, you don t need to pause, as many of these blank spots may fill in with sensations after two or three sweep ing cycles. After two or three body cycles, survey any remaining blank spots using part-by-part scanning. You might find that sensations that were too subtle to feel during sweeping are relatively easy to feel with part-by-part scanning. If you encounter a particularly persistent blank spot, one that remains sensationless despite focusing on it equanimously, keep your atten tion on it without craving or frustration, patiently and equanimously, for up to half a minute, and then move to the next blank spot. Once you have surveyed all the blank spots separately using part-by-part scanning, resume with sweeping en masse for two or three cycles of the body. Once again, after two or three cycles of sweeping two if you are slower at sweeping or three if you are faster survey each area that remains blank. As the week progresses, in addition to going back to any blank areas, start to also go back to areas that are not as clear or detailed as others. Skills You Will Learn The primary purpose of sweeping en masse in MiCBT is to further develop our skills of investigation and equanimity. We become adept at detecting sub tle and nuanced cues of potential emotions, enabling us to interrupt habitual reactions before they take hold. We also learn to proactively manage unful filled cravings with acceptance and insight, remembering that they will pass. In addition, it s common to experience an increase in the number of thoughts during sweeping en masse. This phenomenon is closely tied to the bidirectionality of co-emergence, where thoughts and body sensations con tinually influence one another. For instance, a personally relevant thought often triggers co-emerging body sensations. Likewise, a particularly notice able or familiar body sensation what we might call salient can give rise to a thought, even if you re not consciously aware of the sensation itself. Since sweeping en masse involves scanning larger areas of the body more quickly, it s natural for more thoughts to arise during the practice. This increase provides even more opportunities to practice equanimity while not identifying with thoughts! Blissful Experiences As you continue practicing sweeping methods regularly, you may encounter experiences that are quite different from how practice has been thus far. For example, you may experience the very rapid arising and passing of very WEEK 7: IMPROVING RELATIONSHIPS 233 subtle, pleasant body sensations, sometimes felt as waves of sensations. Practice can start to feel effortless, powerful, and like a rollercoaster ride of radiating energy! With the increased clarity of sensations, it may even feel as though you have a front-row seat to impermanence itself. In the early stages of this practice, this pleasant sense of flow and fluidity in the body can vary from small patches of tingling sensations to waves of enjoyable sensations that spread throughout the body as your attention moves in a sweeping fashion. As you become increasingly at ease with sweep ing en masse and the movement of attention naturally accelerates, unhin dered by intense sensations or blank spots, you may experience a more pronounced sense of feeling joyful or euphoric, even a feeling of the body being weightless or floating. These experiences may be felt in both mind and body as blissful and incredibly tantalizing. Reflecting on the four character istics of body sensations introduced in Week 4, the predominant one during these moments is density, which can be experienced as a sense of diffusion of solidity and radiating energy that seems to flow throughout the entire body. It is crucial, however, to recognize and understand these experiences for what they are. When misunderstood, we might mistakenly believe that this is the ultimate goal of meditation that we ve finally achieved the state ve been hoping for! Such a misunderstanding can make it difficult to remain equanimous if we feel flowing pleasurable body sensations during sweeping en masse. It is very tempting to identify with the experience and give it special value. We might find ourselves believing that it adds some thing to who we are. Sometimes, we might give in to craving the pleasant sensations for fear of missing out. Isn t this a pattern we often fall into with pleasurable experiences in daily life? From this craving, greed can arise. That said, this doesn t mean we cannot enjoy pleasurable moments in life. On the contrary, these are part of life and need not be feared or avoided. What matters is how we relate to them. By understanding them for what they transient phenomena arising and passing away we can enjoy them fully without becoming attached or letting them disrupt our equanimity. Experience of Dissolution As your skills develop further, you may also come across an experience tra ditionally called dissolution, or bhanga in Pali [15, 16]. During bhanga, the usual sense of solidity within the body seems to dissolve. When you keep your eyes closed and remain still, it can feel as though the physical bounda ries of the body are fading, merging seamlessly with the surrounding space, leaving no clear sense of solidity anywhere. It can be deeply peaceful and, at the same time, very pleasurable. Occasionally, these experiences can evoke anxiety in some individuals, particularly those who feel as though they are disappearing. This is because our sense of self is experienced through feeling sensations in the body, which make us feel present and real [17]. The more we identify with body sensations, the more we can feel that we are losing our sense of self 234 STAGE 3 INTERPERSONAL STAGE during bhanga. Yet, when we open our eyes or move our body, the familiar sense of solidity returns. Whether you find the experience of bhanga euphoric, anxiety provoking, or somewhere in between, the key is to remain as equani mous as possible. Accept the experience just as it is, unconditionally, without trying to prolong or eliminate it. Recognize that, like all experi ences, bhanga is temporary. By staying open-minded during mindfulness meditation, even unusual experiences like bhanga become simply more sensations to practice with! As remarkable as bhanga can seem, it does not indicate that one has achieved enlightenment. While it represents an important milestone on the path toward such an achievement [18, 19], enlightenment comes much later but that is a topic for another book! However, experiences of bhanga can provide profound insights and shifts in perception along the way. To illustrate this, here are two examples of individuals who shared their experience of bhanga. Example 1 A therapist participating in a MiCBT professional training course was prac ticing ardently and had a dissolution experience during her sixth week of training. I think I would stay in meditation 24/7 LOL I wanted to share with you as I am sure that you will have an understanding of this experience. My partner would think I had lost the plot. I know I didn t make it up or imagine any of it; it just was Kind regards, One contented MiCBT student; -) After receiving an encouraging reply, she wrote the following: Thank you for your in-depth reply and explanation. It is confirmation for me that it was a real experience and is nothing but positive and encouraging, not just for the experience, but to know after years of belief that we are all one and that there is so much more to our world, life and being than our form and our belief system and that life is to be honored and lived in each moment all things that I try to teach my family and my clients and now I know that this is true. The change in me over the last month has also brought changes within my children. My last two meditation sessions since my email to you have not been so extraordinary, but trust me, I hear your voice in my head, reminding me to stay equanimous and to not crave or expect bliss and I am doing just that. If I never experienced that feeling again, it wouldn t matter. It was so profound for me that I will be always grateful for that alone. I feel a sense of lightness this week. After traumatic younger years and a lot of work on myself, I finally feel so much clearer. I don t kid myself that there is no more stuff I need to let go, but I will embrace that as it presents itself. I do feel that something as big as a realization of the need to love myself more is a big step. I do feel truly blessed and take this opportunity to thank you for the work you do and the guidance that you are giving us on this journey. The sense of dissolution we experience can last for seconds, minutes, or even hours. For this therapist, it offered a profound and tangible sense of egoless ness, affirming her previous belief that there is no fixed and permanent Having this kind of experience firsthand can transform our way of thinking about ourselves and life in general. We find it easier to let go of less important matters and focus our efforts on what truly matters. However, the depth of insight that arises, and the ways in which it transforms our thinking and behavior, remain unique to each person. 236 STAGE 3 INTERPERSONAL STAGE Example 2 The following excerpt comes from an email written by a young woman with a history of complex trauma who was determined to work through her chal lenges. She had completed the 10-week MiCBT program the previous year. The email has been shortened with permission: m not sure if you will remember me or not, I did the MiCBT program with you last year (with regard to my complex PTSD). I tracked down your email as I wanted to share with you an experience that I had, because I thought you may be interested. Last Sunday, I had the most intense and profound experience/realization during meditation: I went into a state of pure consciousness it was formless and expansive. It was like being in my own breath. I was the breath and everything else or nothing else. I had the sudden realization that THERE IS NO ME. I saw the illusion of self. There is no me and there is no everything else. There is just the one thing. That one thing ties everything together, or rather s behind everything we see and experience. It was beyond peace, love or any emotion. Even to feel an emotion like love or gratitude was to feel a separation from what truly is How is it possible to give gratitude, appreciate nature or condemn another when I am that gratitude, that nature, that other too? I felt intensely energetic (I felt like I was buzzing) and intensely aware (I was in that awareness). I didn t want to bring myself out of the state back into the room, just wanted to dwell in the experience of it, yet also felt completely overwhelmed. After all, we do live in the material world and man is not a camel.
