It makes me feel lighthearted and that I should treasure every moment not because it passes, but because it was there to experience in the first place. These are the kinds of beautiful and affirming experiences that can some times arise during practice. They can help challenge deeply held maladaptive beliefs, offering transformative insights and be highly motivating and inspire WEEK 7: IMPROVING RELATIONSHIPS 237 intensive, dedicated practice. As you can imagine, attachment to any experi ence during practice can slow your progress. In an email reply, she was reminded of the risk of becoming attached to needing these kinds of experiences in her practice. She responded: You are right; there is that part of me that wants to chase the experience! But I realize that it is not how things work. It does make me want to change my life though. Things seem different. If you experience bhanga, think of it as a very important marker of the skills you have developed with determination and effort. It signals that your practice is progressing well, so keep going, while remaining equanimous, as your journey continues. Further Along Your Stages of Insight After bhanga, people can still encounter profound experiences, sometimes creating strong emotions. These experiences are absolutely normal you are not regressing! As our insight deepens, we may pass through phases where the mind begins to let go of illusions. These are not signs of failure or dan ger; they are signs of awakening [16, 19]. Like clouds parting to reveal the sky, these knowledges gently dissolve the habits that keep us bound. There are four main types of typical new realizations that can be made just before or after bhanga: 1 The mind begins to recognize the fleeting nature of all experiences. This can bring a sense of vulnerability not as panic, but as a quiet realiza tion that clinging to things offers no reliable peace of mind. Some people can experience existential fear after the realization that their life is indeed impermanent. 2 Another common realization is that habitual patterns and attachments lead to dissatisfaction. It s not about despair, but about understanding why freedom from attachments matters. 3 Realizing this, we feel naturally less interested in chasing fleeting pleas ures that leave us disenchanted once they have passed. This isn t apathy, just the beginning of inner peace. 4 As wisdom deepens, the desire to break free from suffering becomes clear and intentional. This marks a turning point toward deeper determination to meditate, change unhelpful habits, and improve our way of life. As explained below, learning to cultivate equanimity and loosen attachment to your transient sense of self including thoughts, emotions, and especially bodily sensations can make the journey ahead feel like each day is a blessing! 238 STAGE 3 INTERPERSONAL STAGE Transforming Painful Memories with Equanimity Another very important effect of feeling increasingly nuanced sensations with equanimity is the gradual transformation of our relationship with past experiences and events. How does this work? When we feel sensations in the body when scanning, some of them will be accompanied by co-emerging memories, thoughts, images, and so on. Some of these memories and thoughts will still carry an emotional charge. A habitual response might be to start thinking about the event, such as I wish I hadn t arrived late, That really hurt me, or How could my father do this to me? Instead, by continuing to observe both the sensations and the thoughts equanimously without reacting or identifying with them, thereby prevent ing the potential adverse effect of rumination during meditation [20] the mind learns that even challenging memories can be experienced within a context of safety and acceptance. This approach aligns with recent research on memory reconsolidation, a neurobiological process in which reactivated memories become open to modification. In more detail, when memories, including difficult or traumatic ones, sur face alongside physical sensations, meeting them with equanimity allows for them to be reprocessed. Instead of reinforcing distress through avoidance or reactivity, the memory is updated with the new emotional experience of calm, safety, and a willingness to fully feel the experience in the body in that moment, as it is. As painful memories including their co-emerging body sensations are repeatedly met with equanimity, they become progressively deconditioned. Over time, the grip that past experiences hold on the present is softened and, ultimately, released [21, 22]. Equanimity provides the opportunity for new emotional learning. This process of memory reconsolidation can lead to significant symptom reduc tion by altering the emotional learning associated with trauma and other challenging experiences [23, 24]. The Sense of Self as a Process of Change As your mindfulness skills continue to develop, you may notice that as thoughts quieten and the mind becomes calmer, co-emerging body sensa tions also become fewer and less intense. Because this decrease in thoughts includes those that are self-referential those and thoughts the sense of self likewise becomes less prominent. This is because our sense that this is real is very much conditioned by body sensations that accom pany these kinds of thoughts. Recognizing this is a key insight in the cultiva tion of egolessness: the self is not a fixed entity, and our sense of self need not remain static. Accordingly, instead of bracing against change, we can embrace it confidently, as change is the only constant! The sense of who you are is a dynamic process, and experiencing this firsthand in your meditation practice can help you be more flexible in your views. This makes it easier to maintain equanimity even during potentially intense experiences like bhanga. Indeed, one of the purposes of practicing WEEK 7: IMPROVING RELATIONSHIPS 239 more advanced body-scanning techniques in the Vipassana tradition used in MiCBT is to reduce our attachment to a fixed and limited sense of self. Sweeping en masse enables us to witness the profound reality of our mind and body: thoughts and sensations co-emerging and passing away moment to moment, with incredible rapidity. The deeper you look, the more you see the paradoxical omnipresence of impermanence in the phenomenon that we call myself. Every moment reveals the reality of change, and in that change, the illusion of a fixed dissolves. This insight directly relates to the importance of developing interpersonal mindfulness a way of engaging with others grounded in this understand ing of the self as fluid and ever-changing. Imagine how your relationships might transform if you remained mindful that others may not yet have expe rienced this clarity about the impermanent nature of self. Their reactions may stem from their own reactivity to body sensations, particularly those that relate to their sense of self. Might this understanding allow you to take things less personally? Integrating Skills in Daily Life Experiential Ownership Feeling empowered in interpersonal interactions begins with taking respon sibility for your inner experience. Experiential ownership is an equanimity based approach that helps you own your inner experience, understand others experiences, and engage in challenging situations with clarity and empathy. Feeling Empowered If you recall the co-emergence model of reinforcement, co-emerging sensa tions arise as a consequence of our thoughts, beliefs, and interpretations of events. Nobody else has the power to put the queasiness of anxiety in your stomach or the heat of anger in your chest! And yet, when caught up in the intensity of interpersonal interactions, it often feels as if others are making us feel a certain way. For example, if we struggle with self-doubt and believe we are likely to fail, a comment from an authority figure about a mistake might feel deeply personal. This can trigger intense body sensations and lead to defensive or self-blaming reactions, whether expressed outwardly or con tained internally. In such moments, we tend to react impulsively to the sen sations we feel, rather than responding with intention. When we attribute our internal discomfort to external causes, we place our sense of well-being in the hands of others or external circumstances. This can leave us feeling disempowered a victim of outside influences and erodes our confidence in social situations. However, recognizing that our emotional reactions are tied to body sensations co-emerging with our 240 STAGE 3 INTERPERSONAL STAGE thoughts, particularly those rooted in schema-related beliefs, can transform our perspective. When we recognize our dissatisfaction as arising from inter nal factors such as attachment to our expectations or schema-related beliefs co-emerging with body sensations we regain a sense of control and responsibility for our responses. This shift in perspective enables us to understand that, while our initial reactions may sometimes feel automatic, how we engage with them is ultimately within our control. There are, of course, some exceptions where our actions can influence another person s initial physical sensations or emotional state. For example, if we are shouting at or threatening someone, our behavior is likely to trig ger a survival response in them. The brain is wired to react to threats, creat ing sensations like fear or anxiety to help ensure safety. In these cases, we are contributing to the sensations they experience, even though they are still responsible for how they interpret and react to those sensations. The prac tice of experiential ownership is intended for situations where we are not intentionally adding to another s experience in this way a topic which we explain in detail in Week 9. By taking responsibility for our thoughts and related body sensations and approaching them equanimously, we can remain present with our experi ence during interpersonal interactions without reactivity or avoidance. This allows us to set mature and productive emotional boundaries. In short, we our experience while disowning the experience of others hence the term experiential ownership. We feel empowered and no longer need to assign blame. Instead, we understand that it is up to us to respond equani mously to our internal experience. The Art of Seeing Suffering An important aspect of experiential ownership is developing insight into the dynamics of emotional reactivity specifically, how and why people appear to be reacting to others. With experiential ownership, our under standing of suffering progresses from perceiving suffering as individual and situation-specific to perceiving it as universal and shared. Consider how we think about catching a cold: we understand its symptoms as a universal response to a virus, something almost everyone experiences at some point. We don t see a cold as unique to any one person s a common occur rence. In the same way, suffering can be understood as a consequence of unawareness, rather than as something uniquely personal. This is the art of seeing universal suffering because we see it accurately, equanimously, and empathically. In Buddhist psychology, this understanding aligns with the first and second Noble Truths. Why noble? Because developing a clear under standing of them alleviates one s suffering with the first we clearly under stand that suffering is a universal condition, and with the second we clearly understand the cause of it. Together, they lay the foundation for insight and freedom from reactivity. WEEK 7: IMPROVING RELATIONSHIPS 241 Through the practice of experiential ownership, you may begin to notice how the reactions of others often arise from their own unrecognized body sensations and thoughts. Without insight into this process, they may unknowingly identify with these sensations and react to them, while believ ing they are reacting to perceived triggers, just as you may have done before starting your training. This lack of awareness is a shared human condition, and unless we learn to observe our experiences objectively, it leads to suffer ing for all involved. Once this universal nature of suffering is unveiled, it becomes easier not to take things personally. Instead, you can remain equan imous, recognizing, ve been there too. And when you find it possible to be empathetic toward the other person, it can deepen your own sense of connectedness. The co-emerging sensations that naturally accompany this empathy can bring a sense of ease to your experience. The four steps of experiential ownership outlined below provide a structured approach to apply this understanding in real-life interactions. They emphasize that emotional reactions ours and others arise from internal body sensations, not directly because of the person with whom we are interacting. By practicing these steps, you can further develop skills to remain calm, centered, and empowered in the face of interpersonal tension. In Week 8, we will build on this foundation with seven assertiveness statements for setting clear and effective interpersonal boundaries. Choosing Appropriate Situations for Exposure To train this method effectively, we need to practice it in situations where there is at least the potential for interpersonal tension. This doesn t mean creating conflict deliberately experiential ownership is not about provok ing or upsetting others. Rather, it s intended for situations where tension is expected or where someone is already reactive toward you, perhaps because of a past conflict, mutual dislike, or a history of avoidance on either your part of theirs. Interpersonal discomfort can also arise in situations where you need to proactive. For example, you might experience interpersonal discomfort when reminding a child it s bedtime, saying to a loved one unaccus tomed to being refused, or interacting with someone you would typically avoid. The key is to select situations in which both you and the other person are likely to feel some level of unease. If such situations are not readily or consistently available in the coming week, you can also work with situations where others around you are expe riencing tension with each other. For this option to be effective, the individu als involved should matter enough to you that witnessing their interaction creates unpleasant sensations in your body, too. Note that this backup option may not be as effective as direct interpersonal exposures involving you directly. It is equally important to recognize when the practice of experien tial ownership is not appropriate. While this method is universally 242 STAGE 3 INTERPERSONAL STAGE applicable since everyone is ultimately responsible for their own thoughts, feelings, and reactions it should not be used in situations that could com promise your safety. For example, it is not suitable to use with individuals who display aggressive behavior or in the context of violence or abuse. In situations involving a person associated with your own previous trauma, engaging in experiential ownership may unintentionally trigger anxiety or painful memories, making it challenging to perform the practice effectively. Please consider these factors as you read the method s description below. Similarly, if the other person has been using alcohol or other substances, this may indicate that they are not in the best mental or emotional state for a meaningful or constructive interaction. Always prioritize your safety and boundaries. Postpone the discussion until they become calmer or sober. With these important safety considerations in mind, let s now look at the actual practice. Using Bipolar and In Vivo Exposure After identifying interpersonal situations for exposure, start by practicing experiential ownership using bipolar exposure for two to four sessions, just as you did during Stage 2. This approach helps you to gradually desensitize your response to co-emerging sensations through imaginal practice first. Once you feel ready, face the situation in real life (in vivo), using the four steps below. However, if you feel confident and don t need to use bipolar exposure beforehand, then you can go directly to in vivo exposure. Aim to engage in in vivo exposure for at least three situations this week. However, with experiential ownership, the more practice, the better! This will help you establish a solid foundation for next week s mindful assertiveness training. The Four Steps of Experiential Ownership Step 1: Taking Responsibility for Your Experience Once you find yourself in a tense interpersonal situation, the first step in experiential ownership is to take responsibility for your internal experience. Start by doing a very quick body scan, sweeping your attention through your entire body in just 2 or 3seconds. Your sweeping en masse practice this week enables this rapid scan, as sweeping en masse allows you to notice sensations throughout the whole body quickly. As you scan, bring equanim ity to the area where sensations are most intense, ideally within just a few seconds to prevent aversion from taking hold. Remember, equanimity means a genuine willingness to feel the sensa tions exactly as they are, without trying to change or ease them. This isn about reducing discomfort; it s about fully experiencing these sensations without resistance or judgment. If this seems familiar, you re right! We applying the Mindfulness-based Interoceptive Exposure Task (MIET) welcoming the four characteristics of sensations without taking them personally! WEEK 7: IMPROVING RELATIONSHIPS 243 Next, while remaining equanimous, take full responsibility for your internal experience take ownership of what you re feeling. No one can throw a bucket of butterflies into your stomach when you re anxious or a flush of heat into your cheeks when you feel angry or embarrassed. No one can make you feel anything not even the warm, expansive, light sensa tions that come with love. Rather, you feel loved because you perceive or believe that you love this person. Recognizing this can be liberating, as it highlights that your inner experience is your own, and you have the power to work with it. Others don t have that power. Only your mind can evalu ate incoming information and generate these sensations often rooted in underlying beliefs or habitual thoughts and so only your mind can be responsible for them. In other words, body sensations co-emerge only with your thoughts. Understanding this can be very helpful because it allows you to take full responsibility for your inner experience, rather than attributing your dis comfort to the other person s actions or influence. If your sensations, feel ings, and thoughts were solely caused by someone else, you d have to wait for that person to change in order to feel better. Instead, by owning your experience, you empower yourself to respond to what s happening without needing the other person to change their attitude. Step 2: Recognizing the Other s Experience The second step in experiential ownership is to acknowledge the other person s experience both as it appears to them and as part of a universal human pattern. By recognizing the other person s reactivity to body sensations, and its true cause unawareness re also able to see the broader, shared reality of human suffering. You are genuinely witnessing this person s experience of universal suffering. This step requires intentionally setting aside our self-focused interpretations and personal reactions to create space for a clear and empathic understanding of the other s experience. Begin by directing your full attention to the other person and conduct a brief body scan of their possible sensations. Observe their body language, facial expressions, tone and pitch of voice, and choice of words. These nonverbal and verbal cues can often suggest what sensations they might experiencing. For instance, raised shoulders might indicate tightness or tension in the shoulders, neck, or upper back; flushed cheeks could suggest warmth or heightened emotional arousal; and a tight, higher-pitched voice might point to stress or agitation. Our brains are naturally equipped to pick up on these emotional cues thanks to mirror neurons, which enable us to intuitively sense what others may be feeling. Another reason you can often infer what sensations they may be feeling is because we ve all been there ve all experienced unpleasant physical sensations in challenging interactions at one time or another. 244 STAGE 3 INTERPERSONAL STAGE Of course, keep in mind that these observations are only educated guesses, and you may sometimes misread the other person s experience. The main purpose here is to train yourself to recognize another person s suffering when they show reactivity, even if their behavior is rude or their assump tions misguided. In that moment, their experience is very real to them, and they may be suffering because they are unaware of how much aversion to unpleasant body sensations drives their reactions. Without this insight, they remain caught in their reactions, unable to take responsibility for their own thoughts and feelings. At this stage, your goal goes far beyond winning an argument. You training your dorsomedial prefrontal cortex to activate by consciously try ing to understand others, while also teaching your ventromedial prefrontal cortex to deactivate by quieting unhelpful judgmental thoughts especially those involving should and shouldn Over time, with consistent prac tice, this neural coordination becomes more automatic, making this approach easier and more natural. Step 3: Relinquishing Responsibility for the Other s Experience The third step is to relinquish any sense of responsibility for what the other person feels, allowing them to own their own sensations and experiences. Just as you are responsible for the sensations that your thoughts generate in your body, they are responsible for the sensations their thoughts produce in their body. Their reactions arise from their own internal processes patterns of thoughts, co-emerging sensations, and reactions shaped by their unique life experiences and beliefs. Recognizing this sets a healthy boundary rooted in empathy. You may have a sense of how they feel and understand that their behavior reflects a reaction to their own inner experience, even if they aren t yet aware of it themselves. They may not realize how their thoughts and physical sensa tions contribute to their discomfort. Remember that their feelings belong to them; you don t have the power to create butterflies in their stomach, make their heart race, or cause tension in their chest only they do. Remaining patient and kind is, therefore, an essential part of this practice. By embracing this perspective, you move away from self-focused or judg mental thinking and resist taking their behavior personally. Their responses are not about you, but rather stem from their own co-emerging thoughts and sensations. With this understanding, you can accept their feelings for what they are, recognizing the source of their suffering unawareness of what they are truly reacting to without creating additional tension or reac tivity within yourself. Seeing their suffering clearly, without taking it on yourself, enables you to maintain an equanimous and empathetic presence. In doing so, your mind remains clear and open to viewing the interaction from multiple angles. The other person is then more likely to feel heard and respected because you didn t react to their reactivity or try to change how they feel. Instead, you WEEK 7: IMPROVING RELATIONSHIPS 245 are meeting them with patience and understanding. Keeping this in mind can help you stay tolerant and kind, appreciating your own growth and capacity to respond with balance and empathy and perhaps taking a moment to count your blessings along the way! Step 4: Offering a Helpful Response Once you feel emotionally steady grounded in your own experience, tak ing responsibility for it, and letting go of responsibility for theirs find this approach both empowering and solution-oriented. From this stable foundation, you can choose how to respond whether by remaining silent or by expressing yourself in a respectful, constructive way. Sometimes, a simple, clear statement is enough. You might say, Yes, I can see what you mean, or I understand. In other contexts, you may choose to expand your response. For instance, you could say something like, This is getting very tense. Could we discuss it tomorrow or later today? or I can see that this isn t easy to talk about, but I m glad you shared how it affects you. In a work setting, you might observe that the discussion is not going to be productive at the moment and suggest putting concerns in writing, for example: Would you be willing to put your thoughts in writing so I can understand them better? However, in certain situations and with particular individuals, it may be wiser to refrain from speaking at all. In the end, choose a response that hon ors both your own well-being and the potential needs of the other person. Example of Scenario with Experiential Ownership Imagine an emotionally charged interaction: your partner, who sometimes self-medicates their anxiety with alcohol, comes home later than expected and smells of alcohol. They hold longstanding core beliefs of being incom petent, rooted in past experiences of feeling unworthy. You ask how they feeling and whether anything happened during their day. In the context of their schema, your partner becomes defensive, saying they feel criticized and accusing you of trying to control them. This situation presents an opportu nity to practice experiential ownership. s go through the four steps: 1 Scan your body and take ownership of your experience: Scan your body quickly and notice any unpleasant sensations such as tightness in your chest, heat in your face, or a sinking feeling in your stomach. Acknowledge that these sensations co-emerge with your thoughts and take full respon sibility for them take ownership. This act of ownership is empowering; it allows you to break free from the instinct to blame your partner for your discomfort. Even if you feel their behavior is unfair, you can choose to engage with your own sensations equanimously, remembering that you have the skill to manage them and that they will soon pass. This under standing provides a sense of control and supports a proactive response. 246 STAGE 3 INTERPERSONAL STAGE 2 Acknowledge your partner s experience of suffering: Observe your part s body language, tone of voice, and choice of words perhaps they are fidgety, speaking with heightened emotion, or avoiding eye contact. Imagine the sensations they might be experiencing, such as tension in their shoulders or a racing heart, with the clear understanding that these are expressions of their suffering; they wouldn t react this way if they were peaceful or happy. Hold in mind that the suffering you see is univer sal suffering, arising from people s unawareness that they re reacting to how they feel, and not necessarily because they are deliberately intending harm. 3 Relinquish responsibility for their experience: Remind yourself that your partner s expressions stem from reactivity to unpleasant sensations, shaped by their own history not from you. While accepting that their experience may be challenging for them, let go of any sense of responsibility for what they feel, as their emotional experience is the consequence of their own thoughts. This perspective allows you to remain equanimous and empathetic, recognizing the expression of their suffering without assuming responsibility for it. 4 Respond mindfully and kindly: Offer a simple and easily understood comment, if appropriate. You might simply say, m not trying upset you; I just wanted to understand because I care or want to talk about it? These responses prioritize understanding while maintaining your boundaries. At times, the most helpful response may be to remain silent and listen with equanimity and genuine interest. Practicing these steps in real-life interactions strengthens your ability respond to others reactivity with mindfulness and equanimity. Over time, as your brain s dorsomedial prefrontal cortex becomes more engaged, these skills will become second nature. With this in mind, your best to implement experiential ownership as often as possible this week. This Week s Practice Sweeping En Masse As you begin your sweeping en masse practice this week, you might find that it takes around 10 15minutes to complete one full body cycle from head to toes and back to the head, which translates to about two to three cycles during a 30-minute session. This slower pace is perfectly normal and gives you the time to develop steady, equanimous attention, even if sensa tions feel faint or progress seems slow at first. Over time, with consistent practice, you might find that your attention flows more fluidly, completing 15 full body cycles in the same amount of time. WEEK 7: IMPROVING RELATIONSHIPS 247 Regardless of your pace, each body cycle is an opportunity to refine your equanimity by meeting sensations whether pleasant, unpleasant, or neutral with the same accepting, nonreactive awareness, and a willingness to feel them just as they are. Also, notice any thoughts, such as I should be scanning faster or m doing so well, and recognize them as just thoughts, transient mental events that you can observe without identifying with them or engaging with them. The mind is quick to build a sense of self around perceived progress or its absence, but these thoughts are just as imperma nent as the sensations you re observing. Applying Your Skills: Experiential Ownership Challenging Interactions This week, focus on using experiential ownership to navigate and ease dif ficult interpersonal situations. The key is to remain mindful and equani mous with your own discomfort while observing what the other person may be experiencing. Your acceptance of the other person s mental state might also help defuse any tension present. Treat this as an experiment: your task is to test whether interpersonal challenges can be diffused simply by not adding to them. Collect the data without attaching expectations to specific outcomes. Do your best, observe what unfolds, and aim to practice experiential ownership as often as possible at least three times this week. If helpful, you can use bipolar exposure to prepare before engaging in real-life situations. Frequently Asked Questions intermediate my last situation on the list is my fear of contacting my daugh ter after 17years of us not talking. 2 2.2 book When I do the sweeping en masse practice, should I just skip the parts where sweeping is not happening? I m asking this because at times my attention seems to go directly from sweeping my arms and hands to sweep ing my legs and automatically skips the buttocks. Should I check to see if sweeping can happen for my buttocks and genitals, or go straight to where the sweeping seems to be taking me? Yes, sometimes it can feel as though your attention naturally bypasses certain parts while you re sweeping through the body. Often, attention is more drawn to areas where the sensations are more obvious. However, it s important not to skip over areas like the torso, genitals, or buttocks simply because they may be harder to feel at the moment. Neurologically, the most probable reason for which your attention takes to specific parts is that neural networks in the somatosensory cortex and insula associated with feeling those parts are now more densely and effectively connected than those representing other body parts. Don t take the path of least resistance. Move in the same order and teach your brain the pattern of activation that you want to establish. So, sweep from head to toes and back up to the head again two or three times without stopping to address the blank spots, and then go back to surveying any blank spots separately, part by part. Then return to sweeping through the entire body for another two or three cycles, and repeat the process of addressing blank spots separately, and so on. intermediate when i do the sweeping en masse practice, should i just skip the parts where sweeping is not happeni 3 3.2 book When I go back to part-by-part scanning after two or three sweepings, do I need to scan the whole body part by part or can I still sweep the parts that sweep easily? Or should I really break down the scanning for every part of the body? When you go back to part-by-part scanning after two or three sweepings, focus only on the specific areas where you didn t feel sensa tions as readily, or in as much detail. You don t need to sweep through the rest of the body while doing this more targeted part-by-part scanning. Once you have addressed all the unclear areas using part-by-part scanning, then return to two or three cycles of sweeping en masse. intermediate when i go back to part-by-part scanning after two or three sweepings, do i need to scan the whole bo 3 3.2 book Regarding the bipolar exposure method, the scenario I m imag ining often doesn t feel real, in that it seems to be missing the emotional part of the experience. Is this a problem, or does it mean I am actually doing it correctly, I mean, with sufficient equanimity? 250 STAGE 3 INTERPERSONAL STAGE This is fairly common, and it can happen for several reasons. As you suggest, one possibility is that you ve already developed a significant level of equanimity. If so, the scenario may no longer provoke noticeable emotions and their associated arousal, and you ll likely be able to face the situation in real life with increased confidence. Another reason could be difficulty evoking emotions using imagination. Some people find it challenging to evoke strong feelings when visualizing future events, even though they may readily recall emotions associated with past experiences. If this describes you, try imagining the scenario happening in the next hour or two, which might make it feel more immediate and real. To help create these sensations, ensure you genuinely believe you will actu ally be facing the situation in the next few hours. s also possible that the situation you selected isn t as distressing as you initially thought. For instance, if you rated your third situation as causing 50% distress but it actually only causes 35% distress, it won t generate much arousal because you ve already exposed and worked through your second situation and reduced your distress at that 35% level. If this is the case, choose a more challenging situation to work with. Alternatively, your brain may have learned to avoid feeling certain types of emotional discomfort, which is quite common. Visualizing a situation that could evoke these strong sensations may result in your brain inhibiting these emotional signals in the body, making it harder to feel them. If this is the case, this particular situation may need to be rated more highly on your list and addressed later, once your equanimity has grown, as the sensations associ ated with it are potentially much stronger than you initially realized. In the meantime, choose a different situation with a similar distress level. For exam ple, if you had initially rated this situation as 60% distressing, but it s actu ally 80%, choose another 60% situation to work with. Working with this new item will still produce the growth in equanimity you are looking for. intermediate regarding the bipolar exposure method, the scenario i m imag ining often doesn t feel real, in that 3 3.2 book One of the situations I ve been avoiding is chatting with my boss at work. Heraclitus Practice Background and Rationale As we continue with Stage 3 of Mindfulness-integrated Cognitive Behavior Therapy (MiCBT), this week s focus is mindful assertiveness expressing your needs and feelings with equanimity, clarity, and confidence. You may have noticed that some interactions trigger stronger emotional reactions than others. As we explored in Week 3, these reactions are often shaped by underlying schemas and these deeply held beliefs can significantly interfere with our ability to communicate assertively. Developing mindful assertiveness requires understanding how schemas influence your reactions, as these beliefs often drive defensive or passive responses that prevent clear communication. This week, you ll learn specific assertiveness skills that help you express your needs and boundaries effectively, even when old schemas are activated. By combining schema awareness with assertive communi cation techniques, you ll navigate challenging interpersonal situations with greater ease and confidence, strengthening your relationships in the process. Schemas in the Body: Somatic Markers of Beliefs Schemas, or core beliefs about ourselves whether we see ourselves as unworthy and unlovable, or as capable and deserving of love shape the type of co-emerging body sensations. These physical sensations, or somatic Week 8: Improving Relationships with Mindful Communication 256 STAGE 3 INTERPERSONAL STAGE markers, [2] reflect and reinforce the emotional and cognitive content of our schemas [3, 4]. Over time, the body develops patterns of pleasant and unpleasant co-emerging sensations constriction or expansion, warmth or coolness, movement or stillness, heaviness or lightness that arise alongside these underlying beliefs. These interoceptive signatures are somatic expres sions of unresolved emotional content tied to the schema, creating enduring patterns of sensations that sustain the belief through this co-emergence, unless actively addressed. When the sensations are sufficiently intense, they can lead to defensive reactions or withdrawal. In this way, schemas subtly, yet powerfully, influence our interactions, often without us realizing it. Imagine a situation where someone pushes your buttons perhaps by offering feedback that feels critical or dismissive, or that does not recognize and value your effort. As the schema is triggered, sensations that were origi nally stored in memory when the schema first formed in childhood emerge perhaps as a heavy, sinking feeling or as increased movement manifesting as agitation. These sensations bring with them the same emotional weight that they had in childhood, and this felt sense in the body reinforces beliefs like m not good enough or I need to defend myself. So how can we work skillfully with this automatic and often subcon scious process, especially in emotionally intense situations? Research has shown that when a schema is activated, engaging with the experience in ways that contrast with the expectations stored in memory can help release or restructure the underlying memory or belief a process known as provid ing a mismatched experience [5, 6]. Through the practice of experiential ownership last week, you have already begun engaging in this powerful process of memory reconsolidation during your interactions with others. By bringing equanimity rather than reactivity to sensations co-emerging with schemas during interpersonal tension, you ve started to create that essential mismatch experience, which allows for the gentle release or restructuring of deeply held beliefs. In this way, you are no longer living in patterns dictated by the past and are freer to engage with each moment as it is, creating space for healthier, more flex ible ways of relating to yourself and others. In short, you are making space for growth. Accessing Core Schemas with Transversal Scanning This week, we build on your training with sweeping en masse by introduc ing transversal scanning a technique that brings greater awareness and equanimity to the deeper layers of sensations connected with core schemas. Transversal scanning, which involves directing attention deep within the body s interior, is described in detail later in the chapter. For now, we ll focus on how this practice helps us address some of our most deeply entrenched schemas, those we most identify with. WEEK 8: IMPROVING RELATIONSHIPS 257 While earlier scanning techniques emphasized sensations at or just below the skin, transversal scanning takes our attention further within, giving us access to sensations linked to core beliefs about ourselves and others. These sensations are usually linked to beliefs formed early in life that continue to affect how we interpret experiences and interact with the world. Transversal scanning provides a means to become aware of body sensations that may have remained outside of our conscious awareness until now. By experienc ing these sensations with equanimity, we begin to loosen the grip of unhelpful beliefs that have been quietly influencing our behavior and relationships. Consider a challenging situation from earlier this week: perhaps you received constructive criticism at work or had a tense moment with a family member. Later, during a transversal scanning practice, you might notice a heavy, sinking sensation in your abdomen that wasn t apparent before. As you scan this area, feeling the sensations with equanimity, without identify ing with them, allowing them to be there just as they are, you may become aware of a belief tied to them perhaps something like m not good enough or I can t meet others expectations, so I won t be accepted by them, or even No matter how much I bend over backward, it s never good enough for some people. By continuing to feel these sensations with equanimity, you provide your brain with a safe and nonemotional context mismatched experience (as explained above) and this shifts your relationship to both the sensation and the belief. Rather than feeling like an absolute truth, this belief begins to lose its power. In more detail, each time you encounter these sensations without identifying with them, you disrupt the automatic association between an external trigger (e.g., criticism), the bodily sensation (e.g., the feeling of inadequacy), and the associated belief (e.g., m not good enough ). Over time, this weakens the schema s grip, enabling you to respond to similar situations with greater freedom and flexibility. Importantly, you don t need to know the exact origin of a sensation or its specific memory association for this practice to be effective. For instance, you might notice a heaviness in your chest or a tightness around your throat without understanding its source. That s perfectly okay. Most of the time, the associated memories remain beneath the surface of awareness. Transversal scanning allows us to feel these sensations without analyzing, becoming attached to, or resisting them, or even needing to know where they came from. Instead, we remain equanimous with these sensations, allowing them to arise and pass without reinforcement. This consistent practice weakens the schema s influence in our lives. Through transversal scanning, you move deeper into the body to signal to the mind that the old reactive patterns are no longer needed, as things are different now in the present. In practical terms, if a sensation of tightness in the chest typically arises when you feel dismissed or overlooked, feeling this sensation with equanimity lets the mind update this assumption that you are unimportant or being rejected, helping release the emotional and physical 258 STAGE 3 INTERPERSONAL STAGE markers of the schema. This process supports a gentle restructuring of long held beliefs. As you go about your day, this helps you engage in interactions more freely, without old beliefs dictating your response. It creates space for healthier and more flexible ways of relating to yourself and others. This week, you ll have the opportunity to learn how to apply these skills in daily interactions. Later in the chapter, we will introduce the practice of assertive communication, a practical way to reduce the impact of schemas and express yourself with clarity and confidence. Training Mindfulness Skills Checking Your Readiness How was your experience with sweeping en masse this past week? Can you now pass your attention through the whole body in a single, uninterrupted flow? Are you feeling very subtle vibrations or tingling sensations through out the body? And, importantly, can you remain equanimous even when sensations are pleasurable? If so, you re likely observing similar shifts in daily life as well things may go more smoothly and feel more easeful in your interactions with the outside world too. Some people describe that life feels like a flow, mirroring what they feel in their body during scanning. When to Proceed to the Next Step If attention is able to pass freely, or flow, through some areas of the body during each 30-minute practice, you are ready to move on to the next method: transversal scanning. It s important to note that experiencing this flow consistently is not necessary feeling it some of the time is enough to progress to the next step. If You Need More Time However, take a few more days with sweeping en masse if you cannot yet feel a free flow of sensations as attention sweeps through the body, at least on occasion. As with the previous chapters, the guidance here assumes that you are ready to move to the next scanning method. Please postpone read ing the rest of this chapter until you are ready to move on to transversal scanning. Exposure to Interpersonal Situations Reflecting on last week, how was your experience with bipolar and in vivo exposure in challenging interpersonal situations? Were you able to use expe riential ownership to remain equanimous with intense sensations and reduce potential reactivity in at least two anxiety-provoking scenarios? Did you notice any positive changes in others responses, such as a decrease in their reactivity? These shifts often occur because our inner experience of equanimity WEEK 8: IMPROVING RELATIONSHIPS 259 subtly changes our behavior, which in turn has a positive effect on those around us. When we remain equanimous, it can diffuse tension in others. Now, it s time to build on these skills by integrating assertive communica tion into your interactions. However, if there were logistical challenges last week that limited your opportunity to practice experiential ownership, it is best to take a few extra days or even a week to practice this skill in more situations. During this time, if you feel confident with sweeping en masse, you can begin practicing transversal scanning while continuing to practice experiential ownership. Transversal Scanning As you may have noticed, each body-scanning method we ve used so far aligns naturally with the way the brain adapts its connectivity we progress to the next technique only when the brain signals readiness. Reflecting on your practice of part-by-part unilateral body scanning 4weeks ago, you may recall beginning to feel both sides of the body symmetrically toward the end of the week, even though this wasn t specifically part of the instructions. Similarly, during symmetrical scanning 3weeks ago, were you able to feel tingling sensations in small areas of the body by the end of the week, even though you were not trying to sweep? With each method symmetrical scanning, partial sweeping, and most recently, sweeping en masse you likely found yourself experiencing sensa tions with greater fluidity and depth, sometimes even sensing deep within the body without intentionally focusing there. This carefully sequenced approach to body scanning is not arbitrary. Each technique deepens aware ness of sensations at increasingly refined levels, mirroring the way the brain builds new neural connections and expands access to increasingly subtle experiences. The Practice You are now ready to begin one of the most advanced body-scanning methods taught in MiCBT: transversal scanning. Unlike vertical scanning along the surface of the body, this method involves moving your attention horizontally, scanning deeply from front to back and back to front through each body part. As in Week 3, you ll focus on small areas approximately 3in. (5 8cm) in diameter. Begin at the forehead, moving your attention slowly and deeply to the back of the head, feeling all possible sensations along the way. Continue part by part, moving attention horizontally through the head. Once the head has been fully scanned from front to back, reverse the direction, scanning from the back of the head to the front. After completing the head, continue with the neck, shoulders, arms, hands, trunk, and lower limbs in the same way. When you reach the tips of the toes, reverse direction, returning to the top of the head, 260 STAGE 3 INTERPERSONAL STAGE part by part, from front to back and back to front. Throughout this practice, approach every sensation with equanimity, free of judgment. Recognize that every sensation is a natural indicator of change not a part of who you are. Note that when we move attention transversally through the body, we are activating brain pathways, just as we ve been doing with previous body scanning methods. You may wonder whether a particular sensation origi nates from bone, muscle, or a particular organ, but technically, the sensations you feel during scanning are generated by your brain. Your intentional effort to feel sensations within the body activates brain cells in the somatosensory and insular cortices, which allow you to perceive sensations in specific body parts. This process also explains phenomena like phantom limb pain, where amputees may feel sensations, including pain, in a missing limb. The brain cells responsible for generating sensations in that part of the body remain active, producing sensory signals as if the limb were still there [7]. For trans versal scanning to be effective, it is enough to feel, accept with equanimity, and move on. Skills You Will Learn This week, transversal scanning will deepen your interoceptive awareness, enhancing your ability to observe sensations within the body with sustained equanimity. By directing attention internally, you will become increasingly aware of sensations tied to emotions and long-standing beliefs that may have previously influenced your behavior without your conscious recognition. By feeling these sensations with equanimity, you gain the ability to experi ence deeply ingrained patterns of thinking and feeling with greater objectiv ity. This enables you to review past events that arise in present-moment awareness through a lens of deeper understanding and acceptance. This capacity to engage with your past with more insight is an important con tributor to personal growth. This refined interoceptive awareness, paired with equanimity, also plays a crucial role in transforming reactivity in challenging interpersonal situa tions. This is particularly valuable when core beliefs are triggered, as they typically increase the tendency to react emotionally. Transversal scanning directly supports your capacity to maintain both interoceptive awareness and equanimity during such moments, providing an essential foundation for practicing assertive communication, as we ll explore in greater detail below. Integrating Skills in Daily Life Mindful Assertiveness Assertiveness is the skill of confidently expressing one s needs or point of view in a way that respects both oneself and others [8]. It involves express ing our thoughts, emotions, and needs clearly and respectfully, without WEEK 8: IMPROVING RELATIONSHIPS 261 being passive or aggressive. Assertiveness skills can be learned and have beneficial effects on mental health. For example, high school students who participated in an 8-week assertiveness training experienced signifi cantly reduced stress and anxiety [9]. Assertiveness training is also used in mental health treatments to reduce avoidance and as part of anger man agement programs [10]. In MiCBT, assertive communication is practiced with mindfulness and equanimity, allowing for calm and effective com munication that values each person s experience, even in emotionally charged interactions. This integration of experiential ownership with carefully structured asser tiveness statements, called mindful assertiveness, provides a practical framework to address challenging interpersonal situations. Mindful asser tiveness means expressing your needs and boundaries while remaining aware of, and equanimous toward, the body sensations that arise when core schemas are triggered. It allows us to voice what we need or think openly, even when old beliefs tell us we shouldn t. This is particularly helpful in addressing core beliefs related to self-worth, such as My needs don t mat or If I say what I think, I ll be rejected. For those who prioritize oth feelings over their own needs, often driven by empathy or a fear of causing harm, a common underlying belief might be, If I assert myself, I hurt their feelings or risk damaging the relationship. Rather than allowing core schemas to drive our responses, we can acknowledge the underlying belief while feeling and accepting co-emerging sensations, and choose to respond assertively. This cultivates more open, balanced, and fulfilling interactions especially with loved ones. As this way of responding becomes established, it creates a positive feedback loop, fos tering renewed hope, trust, and mutual satisfaction. The Threefold Skillset Now you re ready to put mindful assertiveness into practice. Combining experiential ownership with the seven statements of assertiveness enhances three core interpersonal abilities: 1 Taking ownership of your inner experience: This involves taking full responsibility for the sensations you feel, recognizing them as arising from your own thoughts and evaluations. 2 Recognizing the other s suffering: Observing signs of distress or reactiv ity in the other person and understanding that they are reacting to their own sensations, often without being aware of this. This perspective allows you to neither blame them nor take responsibility for their feelings or behavior. 3 Clear, nonjudgmental expression of your needs: Communicating your needs and experience assertively and confidently through seven concise, respectful statements, without reacting to the other person s emotional state. 262 STAGE 3 INTERPERSONAL STAGE So far, you have practiced the first two abilities through experiential own ership, which has helped you take responsibility for your internal experience and recognize others reactivity as an expression of their suffering. This week, you will develop the third skill: assertive communication. In MiCBT, this ability to communicate assertively and equanimously is called interper sonal equanimity. Common Misunderstandings About Assertiveness Practicing assertiveness effectively can be challenging at times, especially when we are just beginning to develop this skill. Assertive communication that works well in one situation may not be helpful in another. For example, expressing differing views to your partner can foster healthy dialogue but may be counterproductive if they are intoxicated or emotionally reactive. Similarly, some assertive statements might be more appropriate for certain age groups or cultural contexts, highlighting the importance of adjusting our approach to suit the situation. Beyond situational considerations, assertiveness is sometimes mistakenly equated with being forceful or even aggressive. This misunderstanding can strain relationships, as we attempt to move away from passive behavior only to unintentionally create new tensions. Example 1 For instance, years ago, before the MiCBT program was fully developed, standard assertiveness training was used with a 48-year-old single mother hospitalized for severe depression and alcohol abuse. One significant factor contributing to her depression was her lack of assertiveness. As she described it, I let people walk all over me; I m so stupid. One of the people she was referring to was her 20-year-old son. He had recently dropped out of university, spent most of his days at home, and relied on unemployment benefits to cover personal expenses, including mar ijuana, which he prioritized over contributing to household costs. Living on a limited disability pension, she was continually frustrated that he ate much of the food at home but never bought groceries or helped around the house. As she began to learn assertiveness skills, she worked on expressing her needs constructively. On one occasion, she was furious because she had injured her arm in a fall and was struggling with household tasks yet her son wouldn t help. During therapy, she seemed to understand that the goal of assertiveness was to foster a win-win solution and improve relationships. However, at her next appointment, she clenched her fist and gently hit the palm of her other hand several times while saying, You would be so proud of me; I was so assertive! She explained that, after reaching her breaking point, she gave her son an ultimatum and placed all his belongings outside the front door. He had to leave! WEEK 8: IMPROVING RELATIONSHIPS 263 It was a delicate situation: validating her actions risked reinforcing her misunderstanding of assertiveness as aggressiveness, while disapproving them could have left her feeling invalidated, worsening her sense of failure. Over time, she and her son were able to reconnect, and he eventually moved back in under specific conditions. With ongoing effort and through navigat ing further crises, they were able to improve their relationship; he started mowing the lawn and contributing to the household costs. This case underscores the complexities of assertiveness, emphasizing that it involves more than verbal communication to be effective, it requires emo tional awareness, interpersonal sensitivity, and a commitment to fostering mutual respect by remembering that you can be as assertive as you remain kind. Attachment to Self as a Barrier to Assertiveness A fundamental barrier to assertiveness is our attachment to the sense of self. This attachment shapes how we structure what we are communicat ing, with a strong emphasis on views, values, and needs. If you remember our discussion of the co-emergence model of reinforce ment in Week 3, when a topic feels personally significant when it relates or sensations co-emerge in the body with an inten sity proportional to the importance we give to it. This creates the dise quilibrium state that sets the stage for reactivity, reinforcing our need to protect this sense of self and making it harder to assert ourselves calmly. This protection can manifest as avoidance, such as staying silent to pre serve our identity as agreeable, or as aggression, perhaps by attacking others to defend our identity as being right. In both cases, we are not truly asserting our needs we are merely defending our sense of self. This creates a fundamental problem: when we are focused on protecting our identity, we lose the ability to genuinely engage with others, and it becomes difficult to express our needs clearly while remaining open to others perspectives. Our message is likely to be unheard. In more detail, insisting, I am right becomes a way of defending who we think we are rather than engaging with what s actually happening. And even if we were right about something, being right doesn t make us happier or improve our relationships. We may perceive our own pain or frustrations as more significant than the other person s, or prioritize our needs over theirs simply because they feel more real and immediate to us. Conversely, we might sacrifice our needs entirely to maintain our identity as someone who keeps the peace. Without learning to recognize these manifestations of attachment to self, it becomes difficult to genuinely balance our needs with those of others. Ensuring Your Message Is Heard Assertiveness training may seem straightforward on paper, but emotional reactivity often makes it challenging to apply in real-life situations. Without 264 STAGE 3 INTERPERSONAL STAGE sufficient awareness and equanimity, our communication can easily lead to misunderstandings, further conflict, or avoidance. Mindfulness skills are essential for bridging this gap. Through mindfulness, we gain the clarity and composure needed to han dle difficult situations and decisions with greater ease. This practice helps us become more attuned to the timing and context of our assertiveness, ensur ing that our communication is effective and appropriate. By cultivating equanimity, we can express our needs and perspectives without clinging to rigid expectations of how things should unfold. Mindful assertiveness also takes into account both our own emotions and the suffering of others. When our interactions are accompanied by equanim ity and genuine respect for the other person s experience, communication becomes not only more effective but also more compassionate. This bal anced approach reduces the risk of escalating tension and fosters a greater sense of connection. For this, if you want your message to be heard, the message must be louder than the messenger. In other words, it needs to be delivered with as little reactivity as possible, allowing the message itself to take center stage. When we communicate in an accusatory or emotionally charged manner, the other person often focuses on our tone or attitude rather than the sub stance of what we are saying. This is why meaningful points can so easily get lost in unproductive arguments, as reactivity overshadows the core of our message. On the other hand, when the message is clear and delivered calmly, it shines through and can be the focal point of the conversation. The other person is more likely to listen with openness rather than reacting defen sively to the way the message is delivered. By combining the interpersonal mindfulness skills that you have been training through experiential own ership with thoughtful, intentional structure, assertive communication becomes a tool for fostering understanding, respect, and resolution. Seven Assertive Statements Before starting a conversation, it s important to ascertain that the context is suitable and conducive to productive communication. For example, if your partner is absorbed in an activity they value, such as working on a project or watching the news, interrupting them may lead to unnecessary tension. Instead, consider asking when would be a good time to talk. Choosing an appropriate time and setting demonstrates respect for their attention and time and helps create a more receptive atmosphere. In addition to timing, assess the other person s emotional state. If they seem agitated, tense, or visibly upset, they may not be able to fully take in what you re saying. In such cases, it may be best to practice experiential ownership, which is specifically designed for navigating challenging WEEK 8: IMPROVING RELATIONSHIPS 265 moments like these. The seven statements of mindful assertiveness are intended to be used when the other person is calmer and more open to meaningful dialogue. Once you ve established a helpful context, and grounded yourself in expe riential ownership, you are ready to express your needs or perspective using the following structured yet adaptable assertive statements: 1 State the facts: Begin by describing the situation or behavior exactly as it is objectively and factually. This sets a neutral and nonjudgmental tone, creating a safe foundation for the conversation. 2 State how you feel: Use statements to express how you feel, convey ing ownership of your feelings without assigning blame. 3 State what you think: Share your perspective using statements, keep ing the focus on your own experience. 4 State your possible error in judgment: Acknowledge the possibility that you may have misjudged or misunderstood the person or situation. This openness reduces defensiveness, demonstrates thoughtful understand ing, and invites mutual respect. 5 State what you want: Clearly articulate your need or point of view, making it easier for the other person to understand and respond accordingly. 6 Thank the person if cooperation occurs: Express genuine appreciation for their cooperation, reinforcing positive interactions and goodwill. 7 Negotiate (discuss a win-win solution if no cooperation): If your initial request isn t met, work together to find a win-win solution, demonstrat ing your willingness to compromise and reach common ground. As these steps become familiar, you may find yourself naturally integrat ing them into your conversations, even taking a shortcut or two! Until then, following this structured approach provides a reliable framework for assertive and respectful communication. Remember that these steps are most effective when combined with the mindfulness skills you ve been developing stay aware of your body sensations and maintain equanimity throughout the conversation. State the Facts What is a fact? A fact is an objective, observable occurrence something that has happened or is currently happening. In contrast, judgments are personal interpretations or opinions. Starting a conversation by stating facts helps keep the discussion grounded and clear, reducing the likelihood of misunderstandings or defensiveness. When expressing facts, avoid exaggerations like never and always, as these can feel accusatory and distort the reality of the situation. For repeated patterns of behavior, using terms like rarely and often provides a more realistic and constructive description. 266 STAGE 3 INTERPERSONAL STAGE For example, instead of saying, You never clean up after yourself, more factual and balanced description might be, Apart from last Sunday night, you haven t cleaned up after yourself for the past two weeks. Similarly, rather than saying, s always me who has to pick up the children from school, you could say, Seven months ago, we agreed that you would pick up the children on Mondays and Thursdays, but it has only happened once since then when I was in bed with the flu. By focusing on facts, you present an accurate representation of the situation without mischaracterizing it or assigning blame. This makes it easier for the other person to listen without becoming defensive and engage constructively as you move through the discussion. State How You Feel In this second step, calmly express what you re feeling without accusing or blaming. Using statements is particularly effective in this context. For example, instead of saying, s not fair that you don t clean up after your self, you could say, I feel frustrated and sometimes angry when I need to clean up after you. Similarly, rather than saying, You never seem interested in my day, and it makes me feel like you don t care about me, you might say, I feel disappointed and a bit disconnected when we don t get a chance to talk about each other s day. Stating your feelings is important because it focuses on sharing your personal experience rather than passing judgment on the other person. This creates a sense of safety, allowing the other person to listen with more interest and trust in the conversation. Do you recall the mirror neurons discussed in the last chapter? When you share your feelings with someone who cares about you, even to a small extent, their mirror neurons are likely to activate, allowing them to sense some of the discomfort you are describing. This can help them empathize and better understand your experience. While expressing your feelings ver bally, remember to maintain experiential ownership taking full responsi bility for the sensations you are experiencing, with equanimity! State What You Think In this step, you articulate the thoughts and beliefs behind your feelings, help ing the other person understand the reasoning that led to your emotional response why you re feeling the way you ve described. For example, you might say, I feel angry because it seems to me that our commitment was not as important to you as it was to me, or I feel sad because I m interpreting what you did as meaning you don t care about me, or I feel a bit anxious because I think you re speaking to others behind my back. These statements explain how your evaluation of the situation causes your emotions, offering insight into your experience without placing blame. You are recognizing that your own views or beliefs have led to you feeling a certain way. This step is directly informed by the co-emergence model of reinforce ment introduced in Week 3, which highlights that it is our beliefs and intermediate in my recent scanning practice, i noticed that my thumbs and pinky fingers were lifting slightly off 3 3.2 book

I'm worried I'm not doing the practice right, and I'm going backward. I'm also worried I'm going to relapse into depression again, even though I know how far I've come. This last worry is understandable given that you've experienced a number of relapses in the last 20 years, but experiencing emotions during practice is not an indication that you're going backward. In fact, it may well be that you're helping decrease the chance of depression recurring by bring- ing equanimity to deeply buried emotions that have been brought up by sweeping through the entire body. In other words, it could be that the sad- ness is a memory of a past experience of sadness, rather than being some- thing new. This can be difficult to determine, and it's important to monitor, but either way, there is no need to be alarmed. Instead, during your practice, continue to observe without identifying with or reacting to it. If you do find yourself reacting, that's okay too. One of the key skills here is recognizing that you're reacting, and then practicing equanimity while feeling the sensations produced by that reactivity! This is what we call "meta-equanimity," or equanimity toward reactivity itself. Let's recap. When you feel an intense sensation, do your best to not react to it. If you find yourself reacting to intense sensations or emotions, the next step is to do your best to not react to your reaction. If you react to your reaction, do your best not to react to having reacted to your reaction, and so on! If you can manage this, then you're still cultivating equanimity in the end, and that's what truly matters. Keep this in mind, and you'll find your practice continues to strengthen, helping you move forward. intermediate when i sweep in depth, i sometimes feel very blissful, but other times i also feel some emotions i c 4 4.1 book You talk about se/f-compassion, and I've also read about it, but to be honest, it sounds really indulgent to me. I also can't quite reconcile self-compassion with the idea that there is no real permanent self. Can you help clarify this for me? This is a thoughtful question, and I can see why it might seem philosophically contradictory at first. I remember having similar doubts 26 years ago, wondering how being compassionate with ourselves could fit sind 317 ® os. 28.20 173431 318 STAGE 4 COMPASSION STAGE with our effort to develop egolessness. However, when we look more closely at what the self is made of, we see there is no contradiction. First, we need to understand that saying "I" or "me" is necessary for practical purposes, but it's important to understand that these labels do not reflect a permanent, unchanging self. When we practice loving-kindness meditation and say, "May I be peaceful" or "May I be free from reactivity," what we really mean is "May this human being be free from reactivity" or "May this human being be peaceful." There is no permanent "Bruno" or "Andrea"; there is just this human being. The identity we hold, our sense of worth, and how we are perceived by others are all fabrications of the mind, mere beliefs we've been reinforcing since childhood. When we let go of iden- tifying with the artificial construct of a separate self, we can be just another human being, and it becomes easier to offer ourselves the same compassion we freely offer to others. Self-compassion isn't self-indulgence. By embracing this practice, we soften the boundaries between self and other, aligning our actions with a deeper sense of interconnectedness and kindness: Let's consider this from another angle: Would you think of being compassionate toward your mother or partner as indulgent? Probably not. So, why should it be any different when we show compassion to ourselves? Are we any less deserving of kindness and care than others? Often, it comes down to the judgments we make about who deserves compassion, including ourselves. Those of us who are self-critical are often the ones who need self- compassion the most. Our resistance to self-compassion can stem from judgments we hold about our own worthiness. Finally, it's important to note that the mind and brain processes that generate compassion are the same, whether we are offering it to ourselves, others, pets, or even trees! The production of oxytocin in the brain during moments of compassion depends on the sense of connectedness we feel, not necessarily on who or what we are connecting with. By practicing self-compassion, we cultivate a more compassionate mind overall. intermediate you talk about se/f-compassion, and i've also read about it, but to be honest, it sounds really indu 4 4.1 book I understand the importance of acting ethically in our life, but these five ethical challenges you mentioned sound a bit moralistic to me. Going back to the time of the Buddha, he didn't provide detailed rules on sexual behaviors or relationships; instead, he encouraged us to assess the impact of our actions. Mindfulness helps us discern the effects of our choices and actions--if a relationship or behavior supports our well-being and reduces suffering, it is considered beneficial. sindd 318 ® os. 28.20 173431 intermediate i understand the importance of acting ethically in our life, but these five ethical challenges you m 4 4.1 book I'm not sure how flexible we can be with lies. On the whole, lying can be problem- atic because it jeopardizes the trust people have in one another. For instance, imagine you're a tech worker at a company, and a close friend confides in you that they've been secretly applying for jobs because they're unhappy at work. These types of situations illustrate how complex ethics can be. Instead, they are patterns of behavior that can be observed, questioned, and, if necessary, changed. Take a moment to observe the thought "I can't abstain for a week." Recognize that this is simply a thought, not an unchangeable truth. Equanimity toward the sensations that co-emerge with thoughts helps us create space between our thoughts and our actions. This awareness of craving, whether it's related to alcohol, food, or other habits, gives you the opportunity to recognize how it influences your behavior and well-being. But I struggle with the concept of egolessness. It seems so Buddhist, and I'm not sure how useful this philoso- phy is for someone living in the West. How do I reconcile this with my own background? Great question! At first glance, the Buddhist concept of egoless- ness might seem to clash with Western ideas of individuality and personal identity. However, in practice, it doesn't undermine our sense of agency or our ability to act effectively in the best interests of ourselves, our families, and our communities. Instead of assuming that there is a thing or a person somewhere in our head called "the self," insights gained through mindfulness meditation help us see that our sense of self is the product of ongoing identification with our senses, thoughts, body sensations, and habits. Once we change how we per- ceive, think, feel, and react, our sense of self changes too. Hence, insight into egolessness helps us see our sense of self as an ever-changing process or construct. Whether slowly or rapidly, everything passes. This is a fact of life. Why, then, should we claim that "everything in this universe changes except myself?" Are we that special? The first step is recognizing that the self exists only as a continually chang- ing phenomenon. The next and most profound realization comes when we see that while the body and mind are in constant flux, the notion of a fixed self is a construction of the mind. Once this is realized, we feel free to live life in the way we choose, unbound by expectations determined by childhood schemas or self-imposed limitations. This realization also shifts how we view others. We begin to understand that people think, feel, and act with craving and aversion based on their imagined perception of a self--sometimes a self to defend, other times a self to hate or praise, but rarely a self to relinquish. vind 321 ® os. 28.20 173431 322 STAGE 4 COMPASSION STAGE Since you mentioned philosophy, you might appreciate this quote from Albert Einstein, a great Western scientific mind.
