corresponding thoughts that give rise to body sensations, which we experience as emotions—not the situation itself. Recognizing that what you feel stems from your interpretations, rather than directly from the other person or external triggers, shows that you are taking responsibility for your inner experience. When discussing challenging topics, keep in mind that your interpretation and its co-emerging sensations may be influenced by a core schema—one of those ingrained beliefs formed in childhood—that has become activated in the current context. For example, a belief such as “I’m not good enough” or “People always let me down” might heighten the intensity of your sensa- tions and increase the likelihood of an emotional reaction. In these cases, your emotional response would reflect past experiences rather than the present situation. Being aware of this can make it easier for you to take responsibility for your personal experience and remain equanimous, even when discussing matters that require change. Expressing your thoughts and feelings in this way can also have a positive impact on the other person. By seeing you take responsibility for your feelings and interpretations, they are less likely to feel guilty, ashamed, or defensive. This paves the way for a more open and constructive dialogue. State Your Possible Error in Judgment. As we explored in Week 3, your evaluations of situations—what you think about them—are shaped by many factors, including your beliefs, values, needs, personality, culture, and spirituality. These filters influence how you interpret and respond to the world around you. It’s important to remember that the person you are communicating with has their own unique filters and sense of “I,” even if you feel very close to them. To account for these potential biases, acknowledge and state the possibility that your judgment might not be accurate. While you may be correct about the facts, your interpretation is naturally limited by your perspective and level of insight. This step requires the openness to have your views challenged and the self-confidence to handle it constructively. Becoming overly attached to being “right” can make it difficult to admit judgment errors, which can create unnecessary tension in the conversation. Recognizing that your understanding of the situation may be incomplete is not a sign of weakness. As we will explore in more detail in Week 10, being right doesn’t make you happy! On the contrary, acknowledging the possibility of error demonstrates strength, wisdom, humility, objectivity, and a degree of egolessness. State What You Need With this step, it is time to say what you need. Yes, you have every right to have your own view and express what you want, even if it feels as lofty as asking for the moon! Of course, this doesn’t guarantee you'll get what you ask for, but clearly and respectfully expressing your needs is both reasonable ouindd 267 ® os. 28.20 73427 268 STAGE 3 INTERPERSONAL STAGE and valuable. Using “I” statements reinforces that you are taking responsi- bility for your request, emphasizing your needs without placing undue responsibility for them on the other person. It is more effective to express what you do want, rather than focusing on what you don’t want. Saying what you don’t want can come across as a complaint and may leave the other person uncertain about how to respond. For example, instead of saying, “I don’t want you to speak to me with dis- respect,” you might say, “I want to be spoken to with respect.” Similarly, rather than saying, “I don’t want to live with this uncertainty anymore,” try, “Td feel more at ease if I had a better idea of when you'll be home.” This approach communicates your needs directly and constructively, making it easier for the other person to consider whether they can meet them. Sometimes, expressing what you need doesn’t involve asking the other person to change their behavior. Our need might simply be to state our per- spective. For example, we may just need to say, “I’ve decided to leave,” or one parent might say to the other, “When you speak to our daughter in that way, she feels upset, but she doesn’t feel like she can tell you herself. I’m let- ting you know so you’re aware.” Acknowledging the other person’s perspective or efforts can help create an atmosphere of mutual respect and prevent misunderstandings, such as the impression that your needs take precedence over theirs. For example, you could say, “I know you’ve been putting in a lot of hours on your project, but I'd really appreciate spending some time together as we'd planned.” Or, if addressing a sensitive issue like alcohol abuse, you might say, “I know how much you’ve tried to stop drinking on your own in the past, but I now really need you to seek professional help so we can both enjoy life again.” This way, you validate their efforts or experiences while clearly expressing your needs. Express Your Appreciation This final step provides an opportunity to show gratitude for the other per- son’s willingness to come to an agreement. Expressing thanks helps foster a respectful and positive atmosphere. For example, you might validate their engagement with a simple statement, such as, “Thank you for taking the time to talk this through with me,” or “I really appreciate your openness and understanding.” If appropriate, you might go a step further by acknowledging their kindness or compassion, saying, “Thank you, I feel understood and cared for.” Nonverbal expressions of gratitude, such as a smile, a warm gesture like a hug, or a gentle touch, can also be meaningful when the con- text allows. Expressing your appreciation can also be through a more tangi- ble reward, especially with children, making sure the reward is proportional to the effort. Even if a full agreement is not reached, expressing a kind word or gesture demonstrates that you value the other person’s effort in listening to you and considering your perspective, while also showing that you don’t take their
